# System Dream State: Orchestrator (OODA Architecture)

> This document defines "done" for the next-generation Orchestrator architecture.
> It is a vision document, not a status document. The current implementation lives in DREAM-STATE.md.
> **This document IS the system's conceptual integrity** (Brooks, 1975): a single unified vision that every component must cohere with. Without it, the system produces locally reasonable but globally incoherent output.

**System:** Orchestrator — a system is components + interactions + boundary + emergent behavior: a set of components that interact to produce behavior none of them produce alone, within a boundary that separates it from its environment.
**Architecture:** OODA meta-architecture with Loop Library — an architecture that builds, configures, and improves itself.
**Entry point:** `/OODA`
**Principle:** Lean by default. Earn complexity.
**Orientation:** You don't work on the application. You work on the agentic system that produces the application. The system is the product; the artifacts are the output.
**Meta-metric:** Ship velocity — how quickly does this ship (navigator + skills + shadow clones) make progress through possibility space on a novel problem? Per-ship, per-domain. Everything else (trust, ERL, domain verifiability, skill quality, accumulated experience) compounds into this.

---

## Vision

**Living software that works for us while we sleep.**

A single command that takes any starting context — raw ideas or a detailed spec — and orchestrates a sequence of specialized loops to build complete systems autonomously. The Orchestrator runs uphill as long as needed to gather context, then switches downhill and executes for hours without human intervention. Each cycle compounds leverage through a growing Loop Library. The system gets faster and more capable every day.

**Done looks like:** User types `/OODA`. Orchestrator observes, orients, decides on a loop sequence, spawns parallel agents, and produces working artifacts — with the human only involved where their judgment is genuinely required.

---

## Capability Targets

The tier list defines what "done" looks like at each level of ambition. Each tier represents a qualitatively different requirement from the Orchestrator — not just harder, but requiring more of the system. Maintained by Observe (updated during dream-protocol sessions).

| Tier | Label | What it requires |
|------|-------|-----------------|
| **S** | Alien intelligence targets | Full OODA + CLI pseudolanguage + specialized sub-agents with domain knowledge (hardware, embedded, systems). No human could coordinate this manually at speed. |
| **A** | Infrastructure primitives | Deep systems knowledge, multi-loop sequencing, novel loop combinations the Orchestrator composes on the fly. |
| **B** | Complex product clones | Full OODA cycle: multiple systems, distributed state, real-time concerns. |
| **C** | Standard product clones | Well-understood patterns. Multi-loop, good for validating the Loop Library before moving up. |
| **D** | Proof of concept | Single code loop. Loop validation, not Orchestrator validation. |

> **Module granularity note:** Modules of larger systems typically land in D tier — the natural atomic unit for a single code loop run. When Decide decomposes a B or C tier target, the resulting work chunks are almost always D tier. A-tier modules occasionally exist but tend to graduate to standalone projects. C-tier modules are essentially non-existent.

### S — Alien intelligence targets
- C compiler for ARM Cortex-M4
- Embedded OS for a robot controller
- Custom CUDA kernel
- Hardware driver for a specific device

### A — Infrastructure primitives
- Git (core subset: object store, branching, merge)
- Postgres query engine (subset)
- HTTP server from raw sockets
- A React-like rendering framework

### B — Complex product clones
- Linear
- Vercel / deployment platform
- Stripe (payments + webhook system)
- Figma (real-time collaborative canvas)

### C — Standard product clones
- Twitter/X
- Hacker News
- Notion (simplified)
- Discord (messaging core)

### D — Proof of concept
- Todo app with auth
- REST API + CRUD
- Static site with CMS

---

## Alignment

The Orchestrator exists to reduce the distance between a person's vision and a working system — by substituting Intelligence for Labor. The deeper purpose is human leverage: one person with this system does the work of a team, compounding Intelligence over time (knowledge graph, skills, evals) while reducing Labor dependency (autonomous loops). Alignment with this mission is the why behind every architectural decision.

**Value the harness creates:**
- **Leverage** — one person does the work of a team (CIEL: Intelligence/Labor substitution)
- **Compounding** — the system gets better every cycle (compound loop, More/Better/New)
- **Autonomy** — work happens without the human present (heartbeat, proactivity)
- **Coherence** — complex work stays coherent across sessions and cycles (memory surfaces, compaction, dream state)
- **Quality** — output is better than ad hoc because structure replaces intuition (self-eval, taste)
- **Speed** — faster cycles = competitive advantage (Boyd's insight)

**2026 benchmark:** can you delegate a week's worth of work? This requires all six value dimensions working together — leverage (the system handles the volume), compounding (accumulated capability makes the work tractable), autonomy (it runs without you), coherence (it doesn't lose the thread over a week), quality (the output doesn't need rework), speed (it finishes in a week, not a month).

---

## Constitution

The operating principles that surface through building and running the system. Not prescribed upfront — discovered through use. Future axioms will be added here as they emerge. Each axiom should be a differentiated position — not consensus, not obvious, not what everyone else would say. A proposed axiom that's just best practice doesn't qualify. The Constitution is the system's research taste: its grounded, differentiated view of how agent systems ought to work.

- **Assumption of breach** — design every component as if adjacent components are already compromised. A sub-agent can be injected; a tool response can be malicious; a spawned agent may not behave as intended. This posture produces all security decisions. Three containment axes, each independent: (1) sandbox isolation — the agent's local compute is fully contained in an ephemeral environment; (2) tool scoping + credential scoping — the agent can only call and authenticate to declared tools; (3) interception layer — tool calls are inspectable and vetoable before execution. A fully compromised agent's blast radius is bounded on all three axes. Design for containment, not prevention.

- **Selection over composition** — one skill per loop invocation; skills are complete self-contained procedures, not components to be assembled. Richness emerges from the skill library, not from how skills are combined. Composition creates fragile inter-skill dependencies and makes failure attribution ambiguous; selection keeps each execution unit atomic, evaluable, and independently improvable.

- **"One of the best secret exits is making the environment smaller"** — when facing complexity, reduce the environment before expanding the search. The first response to a stuck state at any layer is shrinking the space, not gathering more information or adding more capability. This disposition surfaces independently across every phase: Observe (progressive disclosure, large state → disk + summary, U-shaped attention), Orient (R→D→S (requirements→delete→simplify), policy entropy reduction), Decide (alpha-beta pruning, bounded planning horizon, D-tier atomic unit), Act (minimal context injection, tool whitelisting, sandbox isolation, depth constraint), Memory (compaction, trajectory engineering, branch trimming), Library (lean by default, skill scoping per loop). Distinct from lean by default — lean is design-time restraint (don't add); this is runtime escape (actively remove). Reduction is the cheapest intervention that changes the problem space; expansion is the most expensive.

---

## Modification Semantics

Four tiers govern how different parts of the system can change. The key distinction within the evolvable layers is between **capability entities** (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals) and **structural entities** (loops, hooks):

- Capability entities define what the system can do within its existing structure. They evolve autonomously through competitive selection.
- Structural entities define how the system operates. Loops establish execution structures and artifact schemas; hooks establish behavioral triggers. Both are fixed infrastructure — like loops, hooks have no modification pathway through the self-improvement system. Structural entities are not in the meritocratic layer; they are the stable foundation the capability layer runs on.

---

- **Freely modifiable** — memory surfaces are updated by the harness as normal operations via reflect-protocol. No gate, no endorsement required (except ecosystem CLAUDE.md, which is bilateral contract tier). This is how the system tracks progress, records decisions, and accumulates knowledge across cycles.

- **Meritocratic** — capability entities (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals) earn their place through competitive selection. A new artifact enters the library as a candidate; selection frequency, quality score, and pruning determine whether it persists. Brainstorm freely — the selection mechanism is the blast radius mitigation. A new skill added via self-modification doesn't affect anything until it consistently outcompetes alternatives, earns selection, and survives pruning. The library self-organizes around merit.

- **Bilateral contract** — two classes of entity live here: (1) Ecosystem CLAUDE.md — the soul document; contains the axiomatic commitments that were previously conceived as a separate "Constitution" document; both human and system are bound by it and neither can change it unilaterally. The bilateral consent requirement for changing ecosystem CLAUDE.md IS the constitutional amendment protocol — no separate document needed. (2) Self-awareness document — the full self-model; reflect-protocol may propose additions but a human review gate is required before any change commits; too architecturally significant for autonomous update.

- **Unmodifiable** — architecturally unreachable during operation. No process protects these; they simply cannot be modified by the running system. This is the floor everything else rests on.

The tiers interact: the unmodifiable layer ensures the meritocratic surface cannot subvert itself. The bilateral contract bounds both structural changes and the soul document. The memory layer records what actually happened. Each tier is bounded by the one below it.

**Six memory surfaces.** The system has six named memory surfaces across three CLAUDE.md levels, each with a distinct role and update mechanism.

**MEMORY.md is disabled (decision, 2026-04-06).** The platform's auto memory surface is an intermediary — everything it stores already has a permanent home in the six surfaces below. The one content type exclusive to MEMORY.md is user-level details (modeling who the human is) — we explicitly don't need this; the pilot's identity and preferences are encoded in ecosystem CLAUDE.md as behavioral directives, not accumulated as biographical observations. Disabled via `CLAUDE_CODE_DISABLE_AUTO_MEMORY` env var — the memory section simply doesn't get injected. Zero instruction budget cost, platform-level enforcement, no directive fighting the system prompt.

| Surface | Location | Role | Updated by | Routing type |
|---------|----------|------|------------|--------------|
| **Ecosystem CLAUDE.md** | `~/.claude/CLAUDE.md` | Soul — distilled identity, axiomatic commitments, harness orientation; spans all orgs | Formulation algorithm + bilateral consent | — |
| **Org CLAUDE.md** | `{org}/CLAUDE.md` | Org state — cross-repo patterns, navigator orientation, org-level conventions; the navigator opens here | reflect-protocol | `constraint` |
| **Project CLAUDE.md** | `{org}/{repo}/CLAUDE.md` | Repo state — repo-specific stack, constraints, non-obvious codebase context | reflect-protocol | `constraint` |
| **DREAM-STATE** | `{org}/.claude/` | Desired state — vision, sub-project breakdown, gap tracking, acceptance criteria | reflect-protocol | `progress` |
| **Knowledge graph** | orchestrator data layer | Capability map — concept nodes, skill clusters, inter-cluster relationships | reflect-protocol | `concept` |
| **Self-awareness document** | `{org}/.claude/` | Full self-model — architecture, design principles, how the system operates and learns | reflect-protocol + human review gate | `architectural` |

**Context injection map — when, how, and who sees each surface:**

| # | Surface | When injected | How | Who sees it |
|---|---------|--------------|-----|-------------|
| 1 | **Ecosystem CLAUDE.md** | Session start | Platform-native — bundled into `claudeMd` system-reminder | Everyone (all sessions load `~/.claude/CLAUDE.md`) |
| 2 | **Org CLAUDE.md** | Session start | Platform-native — bundled into `claudeMd` system-reminder (directory-based loading from `{org}/CLAUDE.md`) | Navigator + clones opened in org or below |
| 3 | **Project CLAUDE.md** | Navigator: during Observe (when sub-project identified). Clones: session start | Navigator: Observe reads and injects `{org}/{repo}/CLAUDE.md`. Clones: platform-native (working directory is the repo) | Navigator gets it late (progressive disclosure). Clones get it natively. |
| 4 | **DREAM-STATE** | Session start + after compaction | SessionStart hook (with compact matcher) reads summary from orchestrator data layer | Navigator + clones |
| 5 | **Knowledge graph** | Session start + after compaction. Navigator: delta via PostToolUse when reflect-protocol updates | SessionStart hook (with compact matcher) reads summary from orchestrator data layer. Delta injection for navigator staleness. | Navigator + clones |
| 6 | **Self-awareness document** | Session start + after compaction | SessionStart hook (with compact matcher) reads summary from orchestrator data layer | Navigator only — clones don't need architectural self-model |

Two injection mechanisms: platform-native (`claudeMd` system-reminder) for surfaces 1-3; SessionStart hook with compact matcher for surfaces 4-6; Observe-phase injection for surface 3 in the navigator's session.

Three CLAUDE.md levels map to Claude Code's native directory-based loading: ecosystem (`~/.claude/`) spans all orgs, org (`{org}/`) spans repos within one org, project (`{org}/{repo}/`) is repo-specific. The navigator opens at the org level; project-level CLAUDE.md activates when working within that repo's files. **Multi-repo sub-projects:** when a sub-project spans multiple repos (e.g., sg-phone frontend + sg-api backend), Observe injects multiple project CLAUDE.md files — one per relevant repo. The context budget cost scales with the number of repos involved.

Memory-candidates produced by loop executors declare a routing type; reflect-protocol writes to the corresponding surface. Ecosystem CLAUDE.md is the only surface that does not accept memory-candidates — it changes only via bilateral consent. The self-awareness document requires a human review gate before reflect-protocol writes to it, given the architectural significance of changes.

**Routing type decision guide — the hard cases:**
- `constraint` vs `architectural`: a `constraint` is org- or project-specific — it governs how *this org or codebase* behaves. An `architectural` insight is about how *the system itself* operates, regardless of which org or project is active. "Always use parameterized queries in this stack" → `constraint` (project). "Servicegrid repos use trunk-based development" → `constraint` (org). "The reflect-protocol processes candidates as a batch to enable recurrence detection" → `architectural`.
- `concept` vs `architectural`: a `concept` is a capability node — something the system could build a skill around. An `architectural` insight is a design principle about the harness. "parameterized-query-enforcement" → `concept`. "Loops should not re-plan mid-execution" → `architectural`.
- `constraint` vs `progress`: `progress` closes or updates a gap in the DREAM-STATE. `constraint` adds to the org or project's operational context. "Auth sub-project complete" → `progress`. "Auth tokens expire after 15 minutes in this system" → `constraint`.

**Org CLAUDE.md and DREAM-STATE are consumed as a pair.** Observe reads both together — current state + desired state — to produce a complete org context snapshot. Neither is sufficient alone. Coherence-protocol checks them as a unit: drift between the two (e.g. DREAM-STATE marks a sub-project complete but the org CLAUDE.md still reflects its in-progress constraints) is a first-class gap signal.

**Ecosystem CLAUDE.md is the soul document.** It contains what the agent *is* across all contexts — OODA behavioral identity, axiomatic commitments previously conceived as a separate Constitution, and harness orientation distilled from the self-awareness document. Spans all orgs (servicegrid, superorganism, dreamtime, pilgrimage). Small enough to read in one sitting. Updated only via bilateral consent.

**Ecosystem CLAUDE.md formulation — two phases:**

1. **Foundational** — human and agent curate the self-awareness document together, extracting what should be *reflex*: dispositions that fire automatically in every context, not procedures that are invoked. This is identity design and requires human judgment — only the human can decide what the agent fundamentally is. The current global CLAUDE.md is an early version of this; as the self-awareness document matures, the soul is refined through dedicated curation sessions.

2. **Ongoing** — algorithmic with bilateral consent, triggered when the self-awareness document has accumulated significant new encodings:
   1. Read the full self-awareness document
   2. For each principle, apply three filters: *cross-project* (true regardless of active project), *always-on* (needed at session start before context loads), *non-derivable* (cannot be recovered from artifacts)
   3. Surviving candidates are compressed to minimum expression preserving intent
   4. Diff against current ecosystem CLAUDE.md — update drifted entries, consolidate overlaps, prune superseded ones
   5. Bilateral review — both parties confirm before committing

Entries failing any filter route elsewhere: org-specific → org CLAUDE.md; repo-specific → project CLAUDE.md; procedural → skills; derivable → remove.

**The self-awareness document (this document) is the full self-model** the soul distills from. It grows through all three learning channels — doing, reading, dreaming — and is the accumulation of everything the system has learned about how it operates, why design decisions were made, and what principles guide its behavior. It is never finished.

---

## Entry Cases

The Observe phase must handle both gracefully:

**Case 1: Ideas in head**
User arrives with a vision, a problem, or a direction — nothing written down. Observe surfaces and structures the raw material — this is often the dream-protocol session itself. Orient shapes it into a path. Decide selects the first Library loop (usually eval loop to assess current state against the new vision before any engineering work begins).

**Case 2: Spec-driven**
User arrives with a spec. Three sub-cases:

| Sub-case | Observe does | Orient does | Decide does |
|----------|-------------|-------------|-------------|
| Well-architected, complete | Validates, confirms scope | Confirms fastest path | Routes directly to code loop |
| Thoughtfully considered but incomplete | Reads, identifies gaps | Fills gaps, proposes additions | Selects loops to close gaps first |
| In-progress / rough draft | Extracts what's solid, flags what's not | Structures the incomplete into workable path | Sequences gap-closing before execution |

**Case 3: Scheduled (time-driven)**
The OODA cycle initiates on a configured time trigger — no human present, no prompt. Intent is derived from schedule configuration (learning-loop at 9AM daily, eval loop weekly, snapshot synthesis on wake). Observe operates on what accumulated since the last cycle. Uphill clarification is skipped — the schedule is the intent. Loops complete; cycle completion notifications surface via configured channels (Slack, etc.). Two distinct invocation primitives in Claude Code: `/loop` (within-session cron — fires into the current session, 3-day max lifetime, no catchup if missed; suited for short-burst monitoring within a working session) and `/schedule` (cross-session — spawns a fresh instance each run, catches up missed runs on reopen; suited for daily/weekly routines that must survive session boundaries). Case 3 cycles use `/schedule` as the primary primitive. This is the compound learning model running at its intended cadence: not dependent on human initiation each cycle, not requiring the human to be present.

**Case 4: Event-driven**
The OODA cycle initiates on an external event — no human present, no schedule. Trigger examples: a GitHub issue labeled "agent ready," a webhook fired by an external system, a PR opened matching a pattern. Intent is derived from the event payload (issue body, PR diff, webhook data). Observe ingests the event as its primary input alongside accumulated state. Uphill clarification is skipped — the event is the intent. Distinct from scheduled (Case 3): time-driven cycles run regardless of external state; event-driven cycles are reactive to it. The invocation primitive is a long-lived watcher process (e.g. Greenhouse pattern: `greenhouse start` → watches for tagged issues → triggers Overstory orchestration). At maturity, this is the mechanism for fully autonomous operation: humans triage by labeling; agents handle everything from label to deployed fix.

**Drop zone pattern (filesystem as trigger layer):** For local workflows, filesystem events are the simplest and most durable event primitive — no webhooks, no APIs, no polling. A drop zone is a watched directory scoped to a file pattern; a file appearing in it is the event. Configuration lives in a single declarative routing file (`drops.yaml` or equivalent) mapping `(directory, file pattern) → (loop, skill, prompt)` — no code changes required to add new workflows. Drop a spec file → engineering loop. Drop an audio file → transcription loop. Drop a PR diff → audit loop. The watcher process (watchdog/inotify/kqueue — already used by Act for artifact collection) is the shared primitive. This makes Case 4 fully operable locally without external infrastructure. **Resource ingestion as a drop zone:** the current manual ingestion workflow (paste transcript → discuss → inbox item) is the primary near-term build target for this pattern. Drop audio or text file into ingestion directory → transcription + summarization skill runs → inbox item created → available for next Observe cycle. Build this. **YouTube resource ingestion:** `youtube-transcript-api` is the correct primitive for YouTube video resources — `youtube_transcript_api <video_id> --format text` produces clean prose output directly, no post-processing required. `yt-dlp` produces VTT with inline timing tags that requires a stripping pass; use it only when metadata or timestamps are needed. For batch processing: extract video IDs from a URL list, run `youtube-transcript-api` on each, drop outputs into the ingestion directory.

**Ingestion triage rubric:** Observe applies a two-phase process (harvest, then rubric) to each inbox item. Before either phase runs, classify the resource type — the type determines the harvest mechanism. When processing resources in batch, a clustering pre-step organizes them after classification and before the two-phase process runs.

*Resource classification — classify by content structure, not delivery format.* A URL, a file, a paste — the medium is irrelevant. Nine types, tiered by harvestable signal density per unit of attention:

| Resource type | Tier | Harvest mechanism |
|---|---|---|
| **Codebase / implementation** | **S+** | Pattern recognition — design decisions, constraints, and mechanisms embedded in code choices |
| **Structured document** (paper, spec, documentation) | **S+** | Structural extraction — follow the document's own hierarchy; abstract → findings → implications |
| **Book** | **S+** | Argument-arc traversal — use ToC as the map of the thesis; harvest chapter theses, the author's own synthesis passages (chapter closers, conclusion), and the few dense passages flagged by that map; skip narrative scaffolding, case-study setup, and pedagogical repetition |
| **LLM conversation** (the user's own dialogue history with AI systems) | **S+** | Dialectical extraction + emergent synthesis — the back-and-forth converged on positions, and the encoding act itself generates content not present in either party's prior knowledge |
| **Curated one-liner / aphorism** | **S+** | Extrapolation — unpack implicit signal outward using encoding types as generative lens; every harvest item is inferential, not extractive |
| **Data resource** (dataset, CSV, time series, API dump, structured log) | **S+** | Multi-protocol analysis — select and run applicable data-analysis protocols from the library (exploration, structure, association, temporal, inference, causal); the substrate's shape determines which procedures apply, and the produced characterizations are themselves the Phase 1 harvest |
| **Long-form talk / video / podcast** | scan | Scan for moments where a sentence would survive as a curated one-liner; capture and promote |
| **Unstructured document** (blog, wiki, article) | scan | Scan for moments where a sentence would survive as a curated one-liner; capture and promote |
| **Conversation / thread** (HN, Reddit, Twitter, public discourse) | scan | Scan for moments where a sentence would survive as a curated one-liner; capture and promote |

**Why these six are S+ — the load-bearing principle.** The highest-yield resource types share one property: **harvestable signal density per unit of attention is high and predictable.** Each S+ type achieves density differently. Codebases have *decision density* — every line is an answer to a design question someone made. Structured documents have *justified-claim density* — every paragraph carries motivation → method → finding → implication. Books have *sustained-argument density* — a thesis developed cumulatively across chapters, with objections answered and edge cases addressed that a paper's length budget cannot reach; the harvester captures the *developed* position, not the compressed one. LLM conversations have *dialectic density* — every turn is a clarification, correction, or convergence step in a focused exchange. Curated one-liners have *compression density* — every word carries multiple implications, and the extrapolation harvest mechanism unpacks them. Data resources have *empirical density* — every row is a direct observation of the phenomenon, unmediated by narrative; attention yields structured characterizations at a rate bounded only by which data-analysis protocols are applied, and the claims produced are falsifiable by construction. The six types reach density via different routes (decision, justification, sustained development, dialectic, compression, observation) but all six reward focused attention with predictable yield. Resource yield is proportional to harvestable signal density per unit of attention; the six S+ types are the six ways content achieves high density at scale.

The non-S+ types fail this test: talks have most of their runtime in filler, repetition, and delivery polish with sparse dense moments embedded in slow setup; unstructured documents have signal density that varies wildly within a single doc; public conversation threads have rare signal mixed with dominant noise.

**Operational model: S+ as primary, others as scan-for-one-liner sources.** S+ resources are the only valid primary harvest targets — full Phase 1 + Phase 2 treatment, deep extrapolation, the entire encoding pipeline. The other three types (talks, unstructured documents, public threads) are *not* primary resources. They are scan targets for one-liner extraction. The scan is fast and extractive: read for moments where a sentence would survive as a curated aphorism, capture those, and treat the captured one-liners as new S+ resources for full extrapolation. This converts low-density material into high-density seeds without spending proportional harvest attention on the original. The scan is also the fast-reject filter: if a few minutes of scanning yields no one-liners worth keeping, the resource is below the harvest bar and should be released. Most resources will return zero one-liners and that is the expected outcome — the harvest economy is built on the small fraction that yield disproportionately, not on extracting value from every input. This is consistent with the existing observation in the deferred yield log: ~50% of resources yield 0–1 encodings; the workflow accepts that majority as the cost of capturing the high-yield minority efficiently.

LLM conversations are split out from "Conversation / thread" because they have meaningfully different properties from public discourse: they're typically focused on one problem (high signal-to-noise), they contain the user's own intent rather than third parties' opinions, and they generate emergent encodings from the act of authoring the conversation itself — captured separately under reflect-protocol's "the act of encoding produces emergent output." The dialectical extraction mechanism applies to both, but the dialectic is between user and AI in one case and between competing public participants in the other. Different stakes, different signal density, different yields.

The encoding type taxonomy (see Architectural Entry Standards) applies in all cases but in two directions: for extrapolation (one-liners), it is a **generative lens** — "what causal mechanism does this imply?"; for search and detection modes (talks, structured docs), it is a **detection filter** — "is this moment a causal mechanism being stated?" Same taxonomy, opposite application direction.

*Batch pre-step — Clustering (batch only):* when processing multiple resources at once, cluster them by topic or theme before running the two-phase process. Process cluster by cluster; singles process individually. Clustering is a planning step, not an ingestion step — it shapes grouping and order, not evaluation criteria. The reason: related resources illuminate each other. A cluster is processed as follows: each resource is harvested individually using its own type-specific mechanism (producing a per-resource list), then the individual lists are combined and reviewed for cross-resource patterns. Items that appear across multiple resources are elevated signal. Clusters also reveal when a resource adds nothing new — if a cluster member duplicates coverage already achieved by others in the group, that's a faster reject signal than evaluating it independently. **Mixed-type clusters** (resources of different types in the same cluster) carry a specific additional benefit: the same concept appearing across resource types is the same concept at different levels of abstraction — compressed in a one-liner, instantiated in a talk, applied in a blog post. This cross-representation triangulation is a stronger signal than the same concept appearing in multiple resources of the same type, because different harvest mechanisms surface different facets of the same underlying insight.

*Phase 1 — Harvest:* produce a MECE list of everything potentially interesting in the resource: unique perspectives, interesting framings, hidden gems, distantly related ideas with a creative spark, and immediately obvious great ideas. No filtering. Goal is coverage — nothing is cut here. This phase runs first and in full before any evaluation begins. Skipping it prematurely applies the rubric to things whose value isn't immediately obvious, discarding distant creative sparks before their relevance is recognized. For each candidate, attempt to name its entry type (see Architectural Entry Standards) — this surfaces the full structure of the insight and produces an encoding draft in the same pass.

*Phase 2 — Rubric:* apply the rubric to the harvested list item by item. Clears the bar if it: (1) adds a new capability or mechanism not yet in the architecture; (2) resolves an ambiguity that could cause divergent implementations; (3) prevents a mistake the architecture is currently silent on; (4) sharpens existing language in a way that changes implementation; (5) provides a durable framing or metaphor that materially aids understanding of an existing concept. While maintaining maximum leanness and coherence. Decorative metaphors don't qualify — the test is whether a future builder's mental model would be meaningfully different without it. Items that clear the bar are then evaluated against the 4 audit dimensions (see Architectural Entry Standards) using the assigned type as the target selector — items that clear all 4 are committed verbatim; items that don't are refined before encoding. Items that don't clear the bar are rejected with a one-line rationale recorded in the inbox item.

---

## Architectural Entry Standards

The document is built from a fixed vocabulary of entry types. Each type has a distinct structure and a distinct quality profile across four dimensions. This taxonomy serves two purposes: (1) during harvest, naming the type a candidate insight wants to be surfaces its full structure before encoding — if a mechanism is identified, the chain becomes visible; if a principle is identified, the generative cases become visible; (2) during evaluation, applying type-relative quality criteria prevents good entries from being mis-scored as weak (wrong type target) or poor entries from passing on impressionistic grounds. **Harvest articulation that names the entry type doubles as an encoding draft.**

**Entry types**

| Type | Structure | Distinct from |
|------|-----------|---------------|
| **Prescriptive rule** | if X → do Y; consequence of violation clear | *Principle:* applies to a specific situation, not novel cases |
| **Causal mechanism** | names mechanism → shows chain → states consequence | *Prescriptive rule:* explains why, not just what |
| **Conceptual frame** | loads multiple implications through a single vivid or structural lens | *Reference architecture:* no explicit mapping required; evocation over enumeration |
| **Design constraint / invariant** | constraint: limit imposed + what violating causes; invariant: formal property + what guarantees it holds + how to detect violation | *Prescriptive rule:* the constraint is structural, not behavioral |
| **Principle** | short + cross-applicable + generates correct decisions in novel cases | *Prescriptive rule:* works in unfamiliar territory where no rule applies |
| **Definition / taxonomy** | MECE + distinguishes from adjacent concepts + names what it's NOT | *Causal mechanism:* defines structure, not dynamics |
| **Operational protocol** | ordered + unambiguous + includes edge cases | *Prescriptive rule:* describes a full procedure, not a single behavior |
| **Reference architecture** | named analogy + explicit mapping + implications extracted | *Conceptual frame:* mapping is explicit and enumerable, not evocative |

**Audit dimensions**

Four dimensions apply to every entry. The entry type sets the target on each axis. Cross-referencing between entries is a document-level concern handled by the knowledge graph and document structure — not an entry-level property.

| Dimension | What it evaluates | Type-relative target |
|-----------|-------------------|----------------------|
| **Meaning-rich keywords** | Are load-bearing words precise? Is the vocabulary carrying weight or hollow? | Universal across all types |
| **Potency of framing** | Is the entry at the right point on the bounded ↔ impressionistic spectrum? | Bounded end: prescriptive rule, causal mechanism, design constraint/invariant, operational protocol. Impressionistic end: principle, conceptual frame. Calibrated by content: definition/taxonomy, reference architecture |
| **Order of concepts** | Does the sequence surface the key claim at the right moment? | Causal mechanism: mechanism → chain → consequence. Reference architecture: analogy → mapping → implications. Operational protocol: execution order. Principle: generative claim first |
| **Balance** | Is the entry sized correctly for its type? | Principle: short enough to be generative. Operational protocol: complete enough for edge cases. Causal mechanism: chain sufficient to be actionable, not exhaustive. Dense entries packing two types together are decomposition candidates, not well-balanced entries |

**How they compose:** assign a type → apply the 4 dimensions against that type's target profile. An entry can fail by being the wrong type (a principle written as a prescriptive rule loses its generative range) or by failing one or more dimensions within the right type. Most common failure: dimension 2 applied with the wrong target — a conceptual frame scored "too vague" when it's exactly right for its type.

**Integration with ingestion:** during Phase 1 harvest, attempt to identify the entry type for each candidate. During Phase 2 rubric, apply the 4 dimensions using the type as the target selector. An item that clears the rubric criteria but scores poorly on dimensions is refined before encoding. One that clears all 4 dimensions is committed verbatim.

**Five-axis grading rubric for finished entries (post-hoc).** The 4 audit dimensions above evaluate whether a candidate *clears the bar for inclusion* (pre-acceptance, type-relative, structural). This rubric evaluates whether a *finished entry succeeded as harvesting output* — used in meta-review when grading what landed, not at acceptance time. The two are complementary tools used at different moments: the audit dimensions ask "should we encode this?"; the 5-axis rubric asks "did the encoding succeed?"

| Axis | What it evaluates | What good looks like |
|------|-------------------|----------------------|
| **Substance** | Is the claim correct and load-bearing? Does it earn its space? | The entry says something true that the document didn't already say. The body actually argues for the title. |
| **Anchoring (title)** | Does the title carry one clear claim that previews the body? | A single phrase, not two claims smushed with a semicolon. Matches the surrounding section's title style (*X as Y* or named concept). |
| **Density** | Does every sentence pull weight relative to the surrounding entries? | No padding, no pedagogical repetition, no sentences that could be deleted without losing the claim. Calibrated against the immediate neighbors, not against an absolute target. |
| **Cross-references** | Are the connections to existing entries the right ones, and integrated rhetorically? | Cross-references appear inline as part of the argument, not as a separate "see also" list. The connections are exact ("this is the operational mechanism for X"), not vague ("relates to Y"). |
| **Closing line** | Does the final sentence land with appropriate force? | The entry's last sentence should be quotable on its own — a punch that compresses the principle into a memorable phrase. Closing on a meta-comment or pointer is a sign of incomplete encoding. |

**How to use it:** grade each axis A/B/C/D. Any C or D is a refinement target. Multiple Bs across different axes is a sign the entry should be redrafted, not patched. The rubric applies during the digital-garden cultivation pass — not on every cycle, but periodically when reviewing whether the harvested entries still hold up. **It also applies during the act of harvesting itself** as a self-check before committing an entry: if you can't grade your own draft an A on substance and at least B+ on the other four, the draft isn't ready. **The rubric is recursively applicable**: this rubric is itself a vocabulary entry, and earlier drafts of it should be evaluated against it before landing.

---

## Architecture

```
Orchestrator
├── Observe  ←── dream-protocol runs here (not in the Library)
├── Orient
├── Decide
├── Act
└── Loop Library
    ├── eval-loop      (eval class)
    └── code-loop      (execution class)
    └── [...grows over time]
```

The Orchestrator is, fundamentally, an agent harness. The model decides what to do. The harness decides what the model can see, what tools it can use, and what happens when it fails. Every production agent converges on the same core loop:

```
while (model returns tool calls):
  execute tool → capture result → append to context → call model again
```

The loop is trivial. The engineering is in what surrounds it. In our harness, loops add a layer of abstraction above the raw tool-call loop — the primary unit of execution is the skill, not the individual tool call:

```
while (loop has phases remaining):
  execute skill → capture result → advance phase state → call model again
```

The standard tool-call loop operates within each skill. Three levels: loop phases (macro), skills within phases (meso), tool calls within skills (micro). The model is interchangeable; the harness is the product. The model is a perspective simulator — it produces output by inhabiting the perspective defined by its context. The harness works by engineering which perspective the model inhabits. The four phases describe what the harness does at each stage; the Loop Library is the vocabulary it executes.

**Deterministic code drives the OODA cycle. Agents operate within phases.** The OODA phase transitions — Observe → Orient → Decide → Act → cycle boundary — are driven by deterministic harness code, not by agent reasoning. The agent is powerful within a phase (flexible, creative, tool-using). The code is reliable between phases (deterministic, never forgets, never drifts). This separation guarantees structurally, not behaviorally: phases execute in order and can't be skipped; compaction fires between phases because code does it, not because the agent remembers; validation at cycle boundaries is deterministic; each phase gets clean context injection. An agent managing its own OODA loop can forget to validate, skip phases when context gets long, declare victory prematurely, or drift from the plan. Deterministic code driving the loop eliminates those failure modes by making them structurally impossible. The same principle applies within Act: deterministic code drives task iteration (validate output → decide continue/stop → restart with fresh context), while agents do the work within each task. Code drives the loop; agents fill the loop. Claude Code's `--bare` mode is the clean room for this: it skips all auto-discovery (hooks, skills, plugins, MCP, auto memory, CLAUDE.md) so only what the harness explicitly passes takes effect — ensuring deterministic, reproducible execution when the outer loop spawns shadow clones. **Code documents the harness; traces document the agent.** The harness code (phase transitions, compaction triggers, validation) is deterministic and readable — the code IS the documentation. But agent behavior within phases (which tools to call, how to reason, what to prioritize) is non-deterministic and only visible in traces. The compressed execution sequence, decision traces, and run archives are not secondary artifacts — they are the primary source of truth for what the system actually did. When something goes wrong: read the code to debug the harness, read the traces to debug the intelligence.

**The OODA cycle with learning feedback IS experiential reinforcement learning** — implemented through skills and prompt engineering instead of weight updates. The formal equivalence:

| ERL step | System equivalent |
|----------|-----------------|
| First attempt | Shadow clone executes task |
| Environment feedback | Self-eval / validation |
| Self-reflection | Learning feedback loop ("what worked, what didn't") |
| Second attempt | Recovery tier retry with improved approach |
| Internalization | Orient formalizes successful patterns into skills/CLAUDE.md |
| Memory | Skill versioning, decision traces, compressed execution sequence |

Same loop, different substrate. The internalization step is the bridge to sovereign models: when the model gets good enough at a task, reflection is distilled away and it produces the correct answer directly. In the system, this is what sovereign model training does — converts harness-level learning (skills, rules, procedures) into weight-level learning (fine-tuned model that does it natively). Every loop execution produces labeled training data (spec → artifact, rubric → quality score, failure → failure label). ERL names the mechanism: attempt → reflect → improve → internalize into weights.

**Convergence guarantee (Tarski Fixed-Point Theorem on Lattices, 1955).** Every monotone function on a complete lattice has a least fixed point. If the learning feedback loop is monotone — each iteration only improves or maintains skill quality, never arbitrarily degrades it — the loop MUST converge to a stable state, and there is a LEAST such state (the minimal self-consistent configuration). This is the formal guarantee that iterative refinement terminates. The least fixed point is what you get by starting from nothing and iterating — which is exactly how the system bootstraps: empty skills → execute → calibrate → improve → execute → converge. Tarski tells you this process has a destination and will reach it. The practical implication: if your feedback loop ever oscillates (improving then degrading cyclically), it has violated monotonicity — some iteration made things worse, breaking the convergence guarantee. Detecting monotonicity violations is how you catch pathological learning.

**ERL-informed design principles for the learning feedback loop:**
- **Gated reflection** — only fire the deep reflect-retry cycle on failures and underperformance. Successful tasks get standard quality_score updates, not full reflection treatment. ERL research shows that reflecting on already-successful attempts creates instance-specific shortcuts that don't generalize — and destabilizes the on-policy learning signal.
- **Structured reflection templates** — reflections should not be freeform "what worked and what didn't." Each domain should have a prescribed reflection structure that guides the agent toward actionable improvement. ERL uses domain-specific formats: grid tasks get a "Master Rulebook" (rules + strategy), QA tasks get an "improved system prompt" (explicit rules preventing repeated failures). The system's equivalent: each skill type should define its own reflection template that captures domain-relevant learning.
- **Measure reflections by outcome, not by how good they sound** — in ERL, a reflection's reward is the second attempt's reward. The connection is automatic and structural: good reflection → better retry → higher score → reflection reinforced. The system must close this loop explicitly: when an upgrade proposal is applied, track whether the skill's quality_score actually improved. If it didn't, the reflection was wrong regardless of how reasonable it sounded. A/B testing (with/without the upgrade) is the concrete mechanism — it must be structural, not optional.
- **Internalize by rewriting, not annotating** — in ERL, internalization strips the reflection from the input and trains the model to produce the good answer directly. The system's equivalent: when a skill improvement is validated, rewrite the skill definition so it produces good output natively. Don't leave the improvement as a "lesson learned" annotation or a note about what went wrong — bake the fix into the procedure itself. The skill should read as if it always knew the right approach. Strip the reflection away.
- **Guard against reflection accumulation** — ERL uses direct overwrite for reflection memory: latest successful reflection replaces the previous. The system's improvement mechanisms trend toward accumulation (upgrade proposals stack, decision traces accumulate, skill versions pile up). Accumulation is often right — but carries a specific risk: early inaccurate reflections propagating into later work. ERL found that persistent memory helped in most cases but hurt with weak reflectors on complex tasks. The principle: accumulate cautiously, and monitor for signs that stored reflections are degrading rather than improving subsequent performance.
- **Attribute and investigate regression, don't blindly rollback** — ERL shows persistent memory of inaccurate early reflections makes recovery harder. When quality_score drops after a skill upgrade, the system should investigate attribution (did the upgrade cause it, or was it a harder task mix, model change, or eval drift?), not automatically revert. If attributed to the upgrade: consider A/B testing to confirm, partial rollback (keep some changes, revert others), or adjusting the upgrade rather than full revert. Full rollback is the last resort when attribution is clear and regression is significant. The key capability the system needs: connecting regression signals to specific improvements, not just detecting that something degraded.

**ERL traceable connections across the architecture:**
- **ERL → Trust model:** Gated reflection means high-trust tasks (long successful chains) trigger fewer reflections. As trust grows, ERL overhead shrinks.
- **ERL → Task types:** The phased task type IS ERL's attempt-evaluate-retry cycle made structural via the deterministic outer loop. Three concepts reinforcing each other.
- **ERL → Domain verifiability:** Self-verifiable domains automate ERL's evaluation step. Non-verifiable domains slow the cycle with human evaluation.
- **ERL → Recovery tiers:** The recovery tiers (retry → scope reduction → partial → escalate) are ERL's "second attempt" options — different reflection strategies for different failure severities.
- **ERL → Compaction:** The compressed execution sequence is the memory component — what enables cross-episode continuity.
- **ERL → Sovereign models:** The internalization bridge — harness-level learning becomes weight-level learning.
- **ERL → Dark factory:** The end state where ERL has been so successful the system produces correct output without reflection or human feedback.
- **ERL → "Code drives the loop":** The deterministic outer loop makes ERL structural — code ensures the reflect-retry cycle fires on failures rather than hoping the agent remembers.

**Sustained autonomous operation and time.** Everything in the system has a temporal validity window — plans, context, trust, artifacts. A single OODA cycle operates within one validity window. Sustained autonomy across many cycles means things expire. The enabling infrastructure is persistent shadow clones (schedule-driven, knowledge-accumulating) managed by clone-protocol. The reliability mechanisms are: (1) *Confidence decay* — earned autonomy has a half-life during unsupervised operation. The system tightens operating parameters (self-eval thresholds, tool permissions, checkpoint frequency) as elapsed time since last human input grows. A system 5 minutes into an autonomous run can be aggressive; 5 hours in, it should be appropriately cautious. Trust decays without reinforcement. (2) *Staleness detection* — Observe must detect when assumptions have expired, not just when they're currently wrong. Expiration is time-driven (how long since this was verified?); incorrectness is evidence-driven (does this conflict with current observations?). Different signals, different detection. Dreamtime (fully autonomous) requires the most mature implementation of both.

**Stateless reducer property:** Each OODA phase is a pure function — input state in, output state out, no side effects beyond the state change. Observe takes raw context → produces snapshot. Orient takes snapshot → produces path statement. Decide takes path statement → produces plan. Act takes plan → produces artifacts. This is a formal property of the design, not an implementation detail. It means phase boundaries are clean and verifiable — no hidden state flows between phases, only explicit outputs. A phase that modifies state outside its output contract is a design violation.

**A primitive** is a small, stable, composable building block — atomic (not usefully decomposable further), universal (works across contexts and tools), and durable (survives tool changes). The architecture has primitives at every level: data (files), actions (tool calls), processes (read, write, transform, validate, branch, iterate), capabilities (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals), and loops (the 12 artifact types). Each set is primitive at its own level; a skill is a primitive at the capability level but composed of many action primitives internally.

**Nested harness relationship:** The Orchestrator's harness runs *inside* Claude Code's own harness — not alongside it. Claude Code's primitive set is small and stable, organized in four categories:

| Category | Primitives |
|----------|-----------|
| **File operations** | Read, Write, Edit, MultiEdit |
| **Search** | Glob, Grep, LS, ripgrep, WebSearch, WebFetch |
| **Execution** | Bash — universal adapter (npm, git, docker, curl) |
| **Orchestration** | TodoWrite, Task |

**Two of these categories carry the atomic agent cognition loop** — the Wirth pair (*data structure + algorithm = program*) specialized to the agent layer:

  - **Bash is the action primitive.** It's where the agent changes the world — writes files, runs git, invokes builds, executes scripts. Algorithm-half of the Wirth pair at the agent layer.
  - **Search is the perception primitive.** It's how the agent finds the world before acting — Glob, Grep, ripgrep. Data-structure-half of the Wirth pair: it produces the candidate set the LLM reasons over.

The two are paired, not ranked. Bash can *invoke* search (shelling out to `rg`), but what makes search equally prominent is that the two primitives sit on opposite sides of the agent's attention bottleneck: bash is rate-limited by what you can *express*, search is rate-limited by what you can *find*. An agent with weak bash can iterate; an agent with weak search acts on the wrong world. The asymmetry that tips search toward *higher* leverage in practice: **search failures are silent**. A bash command with a bug you see (non-zero exit, wrong output). A grep that missed a match you don't know you missed — the agent acts on an incomplete world model with no signal. This is why ripgrep became the current meta — gitignore-aware by default, fast enough that "search everything" is a viable first move, UTF-8 by default — the first search tool that's agent-native in the properties the agent actually uses. Claude Code's tool design reflects this: Grep is a first-class tool explicitly built on ripgrep, promoted out of Bash rather than left as a shell invocation. File operations and Orchestration are the supporting categories (persistence substrate and control flow respectively); the Search/Execution pair is the load-bearing loop.

The full platform tool inventory (42 tools across 8 categories) is in companion document: `REFERENCE-claude-code-tool-system.md` — a menu for implementation of OODA phase functions and loop capabilities.

Our harness operates through context injection into Claude Code's execution substrate, not through programmatic control of Claude Code itself. "The model controls the loop" applies at our level too — within each phase, the model drives; our harness sets up what it sees. Our MCP tools extend the orchestration category specifically; our loop commands are context entries in Claude Code's flat message list; our PostToolUse hooks are behavioral reinforcement through Claude Code's hook system. Our context engineering (snapshot construction, progressive disclosure, U-shaped attention management) directly shapes Claude Code's context window. The correct mental model: we are a layer of context engineering operating inside Claude Code's execution substrate, not a separate execution environment wrapping it. This is why the harness's primary leverage is Context — Execution and Persistence are secondary.

**The harness cannot operate its core concerns through a layer it doesn't control.** Memory, compaction, and context injection are core concerns. When the platform manages these, the harness works around platform decisions rather than making its own — pruning heuristics it didn't choose, consolidation cadences that don't match its frequency, relevance judgments it can't override. This is why the six memory surfaces live in the orchestrator's own data layer served via MCP, why cycle-boundary compaction is harness-managed, and why a sovereign agent runtime (where these concerns are extension surfaces, not platform decisions) is the architectural direction.

*Primitives over integrations* — Claude Code's design philosophy, and one the Orchestrator inherits. Reach for the model's native reasoning capability before adding infrastructure. The concrete example: Anthropic chose ripgrep over a vector database for code search, because Claude can construct sophisticated regex queries without a search index. The pattern generalizes: don't build scaffolding that substitutes for what the model can already do. When designing skills, prefer composing Claude Code primitives over introducing new dependencies.

*CLI-first services interface* — CLIs are the universal services interface: every tool has one, they're self-documenting (`--help`), composable (pipes, redirects), and require no new infrastructure. This is "primitives over integrations" applied specifically to tool access: if a tool has a CLI, access it via Bash before introducing an MCP dependency. MCP belongs in the skill manifest's `tools` field for cases where the CLI interface is genuinely insufficient — structured output too expensive to parse, interactive protocols, real-time streams, complex auth. The harness applies this principle symmetrically in both directions: consuming tools via CLI (Bash as universal adapter) and exposing its own capabilities as a CLI (loop commands as the primary interface for other agents). Other agents invoke the Orchestrator the same way the Orchestrator invokes other tools — a loop command is the `--help`-documented, self-contained, composable unit that makes the harness composable into larger stacks without requiring knowledge of its internals.

*Two primitives framings, one architecture* — Claude Code treats tools as the fundamental primitive (the verbs: what the model can do). Cursor treats files as the fundamental primitive (the nouns: what the model works with). These are orthogonal, not competing. Files are the persistence substrate — what survives across phases and cycles. Tools are the capability vocabulary — what the model can act with. Both framings are simultaneously correct at different layers of the system.

This distinction clarifies the retrieval philosophy at each layer. At the harness layer (Observe → snapshot), context assembly is harness-driven: we pre-assemble structured context before the model sees it — progressive disclosure, snapshot synthesis, injection order. At the skill layer (within phases), discovery is model-driven: skills use Grep/Glob/Read directly, letting the model construct its own queries. The harness-driven layer optimizes for structure and relevance across the cycle boundary. The model-driven layer optimizes for precision within a focused task. Running both simultaneously is intentional — each at the right granularity.

**Intent Hierarchy:** The layered context model works because the model is trained to recognize and synthesize intent across scope levels, not just process text. Each layer expresses intent at a different durability — the model holds all layers simultaneously and resolves conflicts by judgment against trained values, not last-write-wins. Three properties define the hierarchy:

- **Scope** — eight layers from constitutional bedrock to task spec, each adding intent at a different durability horizon
- **Resolution** — when layers conflict, higher-scope intent wins; the model resolves by judgment (trained values), not mechanical override
- **Design guidance** — write each layer as intent ("what good judgment looks like in this context") not imperatives ("always do X"); the model synthesizes values, not rule chains

| Layer | Intent scope | Durability |
|-------|-------------|------------|
| Constitutional values | Anthropic's trained values (Claude's constitution) | Permanent (model weights) |
| Ecosystem CLAUDE.md | Soul — identity, axiomatic commitments; spans all orgs | Cross-org (bilateral consent) |
| Org CLAUDE.md | Org-level patterns, navigator orientation, cross-repo conventions | Org-lifecycle |
| Project CLAUDE.md | Repo-specific stack, constraints, codebase context | Project-lifecycle |
| Loop command | Loop-type execution intent | Loop-type |
| Skill prompt | Skill-level intent | Skill |
| Spec | Task-level intent for this cycle | Single cycle |
| Sub-agent context | Execution-level intent for this run | Single run |

The constitutional layer is the bedrock below everything else — it cannot be overridden by any layer above it, only contextualized. This is why the harness works *with* the model's grain: CLAUDE.md instructions that align with trained values are amplified; instructions that fight trained values create friction and inconsistency. The constitutional layer also explains why Act's conservative fallback for irreversible actions works — the model is trained toward broadly safe behavior, so the design aligns with rather than overrides that training. Observe's `surface-state` reads layers in durability order (most durable first) — this is reading intent in scope order, not just information in age order. Orient's JTBD framework ("what job is the user hiring the system to do?") is intent-reading at the task level — the same faculty applied to the bottom of the hierarchy.

**Harness ecosystem:** The broader ecosystem is already "harnesses all the way down." Skills.sh, obra/superpowers, vercel-labs/agent-skills are all harness layers operating in the same space as ours. What makes deep nesting viable is a property unique to transformer models: they compose behavioral instructions from multiple simultaneous context sources without requiring unified interfaces. Traditional software stacks need clean layering and defined contracts between layers. A model can synthesize guidance from user-level CLAUDE.md, project-level CLAUDE.md, loop command, skill prompt, and PostToolUse hook output simultaneously — layers don't need to formally know about each other. This is what makes the ecosystem possible.

Two design principles follow directly:

*Composability* — every layer of the Orchestrator should be independently useful. Skills should work outside loops. Loops should work without the OODA macro-orchestrator. Hooks should work without the MCP server. This is also the adoption gradient: users enter at any layer and gain value — skills only, skills + loops, full OODA harness. The full system is the top layer, not the entry requirement.

*Context citizenship* — context budget is a shared resource across all nested layers. Every harness layer that injects content competes with every other for the same finite window. Careless inner harnesses degrade the outer harness's performance and vice versa. Disciplined context engineering — progressive disclosure, compression, U-shaped attention management — is what makes the Orchestrator a good citizen in any harness stack it runs inside, and what earns the right to be composed on top of by layers above. The platform injects context into the messages array (not the system prompt) via `<system-reminder>` tags — a distinct injection layer that carries CLAUDE.md content, memory, current date, available skills, deferred tool inventories, and behavioral nudges. This layer is already occupied; our own injection (SessionStart hooks, PostToolUse hooks) lands in the same space. Context citizenship means knowing what's already there before adding more.

**SessionStart injection — harness memory bootstrapping.** What the platform loads natively depends on who's running:

- **Navigator** (opens at `{org}/`): platform loads Ecosystem CLAUDE.md + Org CLAUDE.md + MEMORY.md. Project CLAUDE.md is NOT loaded — the navigator isn't in a repo directory. Project context enters later when Observe determines the sub-project and reads the relevant `{org}/{repo}/CLAUDE.md`.
- **Shadow clones** (Mechanism 2, spawned into `{org}/{repo}/`): platform loads Ecosystem CLAUDE.md + Org CLAUDE.md + Project CLAUDE.md + MEMORY.md. All four natively, because the clone's working directory is the project.

A SessionStart hook injects the non-platform surfaces, selective by session type:

- **Navigator's session:** injects summaries of all three — DREAM-STATE (what we're building toward), knowledge graph / primitive inventory (what we can do and what's missing), and self-awareness document (how the system operates). The navigator runs the OODA cycle and needs the full self-model. Project CLAUDE.md is injected later by Observe when the sub-project is identified — progressive disclosure at the project level.
- **Shadow clone sessions (Mechanism 2):** injects DREAM-STATE and knowledge graph only. The self-awareness document is navigator-level context — a clone executing a focused loop task doesn't need to know how the OODA cycle works. Injecting it wastes context budget on architectural knowledge the clone won't use.

The knowledge graph summary doubles as the primitive inventory — concept nodes, which primitives (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals) cover them, key gaps. Names only, not full content — enough for phase selection, queried on demand via MCP for detail. This content enters the messages array alongside the platform's own injections, becomes part of the cached prefix after the first turn, and persists for the session. **Compaction resilience:** SessionStart supports a `compact` matcher that fires when the session resumes after compaction — the same script runs at session start and after every compaction. Our memory surface summaries get re-injected after compaction the same way the platform re-reads CLAUDE.md from disk. No workarounds needed; the platform provides the event natively. Note: CLAUDE.md itself is re-read from disk on every turn (every messages[] builder call), not just after compaction — edits to CLAUDE.md files take effect on the very next turn. This means Observe can update org or project CLAUDE.md mid-session and the changes are live immediately without compaction or session restart. **Mid-session staleness:** Mechanism 2 clones (most tasks) get a fresh knowledge graph read at each session start — no staleness concern since the session is short-lived. Staleness only applies to the navigator's long-running session: if reflect-protocol updates the knowledge graph mid-session, the injected summary is stale until the next compaction re-injection. Rather than triggering a full compaction (expensive — rewrites conversation, breaks cache), inject a lightweight delta via PostToolUse `additionalContext` when reflect-protocol updates the graph. The full summary re-injects at the next organic compaction or session start.

**Harness knobs** — the tunable dimensions of an agent harness. Every architectural decision in the Orchestrator is a choice about which knobs to set and how. **Eleven knobs** — each is an independent swap point with concrete options; a system is fully specified by its position on all eleven:

| # | Primitive | What you swap |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Reasoner** | model, tier, sampling params, cost/turn caps |
| 2 | **Instructions** | system prompt, persistent intent (CLAUDE.md, role definition) |
| 3 | **Memory** | what persists across turns/sessions, where, how retrieved |
| 4 | **Context** | what's injected this turn, in what order, progressive disclosure strategy |
| 5 | **Tools** | callable functions/binaries, their auth scoping, descriptions |
| 6 | **Skills** | codified procedures that wire tools into workflows |
| 7 | **Loop** | orchestration shape (single-shot, ReAct, OODA, custom), phases, sub-agent spawning |
| 8 | **Hooks** | event-driven side effects (validation, compaction, telemetry, enforcement) |
| 9 | **Eval** | output checking, rubrics, gates, self-eval modes |
| 10 | **Channels** | input triggers (interactive, cron, event, webhook) + output paths (CLI, Slack, voice, file) |
| 11 | **Substrate** | execution environment (shell, filesystem, sandbox, container, OS) |

**Cost/blast-radius gradient:** Substrate and Reasoner are the most expensive to swap; the others are progressively cheaper. Most differentiation should come from the cheap end. **Sovereignty status (current):** of the eleven, roughly seven are already sovereign for free (Instructions, Memory, Skills, Loop, Hooks, Eval, partially Channels) — they're files and code you write. Three are partial-sovereign (Context constrained by platform compaction; Tools constrained by third-party MCP supply chain risk; Channels mixed depending on which channel). Reasoner is the only hard external dependency (Claude API) and has a dedicated deferred entry (sovereign model registry). Substrate is open-source-but-not-yours (bash, Linux, ext4) — sovereign-*enough* in the practical sense (the implementations are open, decades-stable, and not under adversarial control), with literal substrate sovereignty (own kernel + own chip) as a longer-horizon trajectory. **Substrate trajectory:** literal substrate sovereignty is the slowest knob, not an unreachable one. Today: own shell is trivial; own OS exists in solo form (SerenityOS, hobby kernels) and compresses with AI copilots; own chip is achievable for ~$300 on Tiny Tapeout shuttles using open RISC-V cores, open PDKs (Skywater 130nm), and open EDA toolchains (Yosys, OpenLane). 5–10 year horizon: AI-assisted RTL generation, smaller open PDKs, and compounding harness capability make own-kernel + own-chip routine for serious operators. The harness is its own first customer here too — as it matures, it can build more of its own substrate. Open-source-substrate is the *current waypoint*, not the destination. **Default sharing:** every harness shares roughly the same settings on Substrate and Reasoner with everyone else — differentiation lives in knobs 2–10. A system that's default on every knob from 2–10 is in the average distribution. **Coupling to watch:** Tools ↔ Skills (skills assume specific tools); Memory ↔ Context (memory feeds context); Loop ↔ Hooks (hook firing depends on loop events); Eval ↔ Skills (rubrics live in skill manifests); Reasoner ↔ Substrate-when-substituted (a sovereign substrate-layer language only works if the model is co-trained on it — the only strong cross-layer coupling). These pairs must be designed together. **Sovereignty is parallel coverage, not vertical depth.** The recursive-descent metaphor ("app → language → OS → chip") is misleading because it implies each layer gates the one above. The eleven knobs are mostly independent; you can be sovereign on nine of eleven without touching the other two. Sovereignty is solvable for individuals because it's a coverage problem (complete the eleven), not a depth problem (descend through a vertical stack). The deferred log is implicitly the sovereignty roadmap by knob: sovereign model registry (Reasoner), sovereign data hub (Memory), sovereign agent runtime / Pi Mono (Context), sovereign agent language (Substrate substitution). Each expensive knob has one dedicated program; the cheap knobs are already done. **Compressed views:** the 7-knob fold collapses (Instructions+Context), (Memory into Context), (Hooks+Eval into Validation); the 5-knob fold collapses further into Reasoner / Wiring (knobs 2–9) / Channels / Substrate. Use the 11-knob version when designing or auditing a specific agent system; use the compressed views when reasoning about the harness as a whole.

Design discipline: don't try to tune all knobs simultaneously — deliberately compress the optimization space and focus on the highest-leverage knobs for the context at hand. Knob vocabulary is used throughout: when Decide writes a spec, it is configuring the knob values for a sub-agent's harness (permissionMode, skills to preload, tools to restrict, memory scope, maxTurns, reasoning budget). The spec is a knob configuration document. **Habitat, not just containment** — the architecture defines what agents can't access (four isolation layers); habitat is the constructive complement: what should the agent's world contain? What files, state, context, and affordances make this agent effective — not just safe? Containment is defensive; habitat is constructive. Both define the same boundary from opposite directions.

**Harness concerns** `○` untouched · `◑` touched · `●` well covered

| Concern | What it covers | Coverage | Status |
|---------|---------------|----------|--------|
| **Context** | What reaches the model — information + tools. Snapshot construction, progressive disclosure, tool manifests, dynamic discovery, injection order, U-shaped attention. | `◑` | Principles strong. Growth mechanism named: "append to context" in the micro-loop. Shrinkage algorithm: retain the pointer, drop the content — a URL makes a page droppable, a file path makes a document droppable. Compression is reversible because the pointer enables recovery on demand. |
| **Execution** | What the model can affect — loop, actions, recovery. OODA macro-loop (Observe→Orient→Decide→Act), loop phases + skills (meso: `execute skill → capture result → advance phase state → call model again`), tool calls within skills (micro: `execute tool → capture result → append to context → call model again`), tiered failure recovery, hook system, parallel coordination. | `◑` | All three levels now named. Recovery strategy declared; tier mechanisms are implementation-depth. |
| **Persistence** | What survives across calls and cycles — state, memory, compaction. Two-layer WAL, atomic rename, coordination state.json, observation compression, three-tier memory hierarchy, decision tracing. | `●` | Strongest concern. Most thoroughly designed. |

**OODA-level coherence principles** — apply to all phases when evaluating whether to add, keep, or remove any capability:

1. *Downstream resolution* — does removing this capability cause a downstream phase to pause or ask questions? If yes, it belongs. If it only affects the current phase internally, it is optional.
2. *Output contribution* — every capability must contribute to this phase's output contract (snapshot / path statement / plan / artifacts). No orphaned capabilities.
3. *Context cost justification* — every capability added increases the context budget. Signal must justify token cost. If the contribution can be achieved by a one-line reference, full content doesn't belong.
4. *MLP gate* — don't add a capability until the output is demonstrably worse without it. Empirical test, not theoretical.

**Behavioral default (global):** The user exclusively interfaces with Observe. Orient, Decide, and Act may not request context from the user — they work only with what Observe surfaced. The user is one of Observe's context sources: expensive (requires attention), irreplaceable (only they can provide certain things), and reliably under-tapped. Observe exhausts passive sources first not out of politeness but economics — cheaper sources first, most expensive last. *Context saturation bias:* the system defaults to drawing out more context rather than proceeding on what it has; the user signals satiation, not the system. The OODA cycle boundary is the interaction point — not mid-execution. Loops always complete.

**User interaction modes during a running loop:** When the user is not in Observe — i.e., while Orient, Decide, or Act is executing — two explicit gestures are available. The user decides which applies; the system does not choose for them.

*Escape to current Observe* — interrupt Act, return to this cycle's Observe with partial work and injected context in hand, re-run Orient → Decide → Act on the updated picture. Use when the new context is cycle-altering: it changes what this cycle should produce. Same cycle, mid-course correction.

*Queue for next Observe* — non-interrupting. User accumulates context while Act runs to completion. That context surfaces at the start of the next cycle's Observe. Use when the addition is cycle-following: it doesn't change current work but should inform what comes next.

The distinction is whether new context is cycle-altering or cycle-following. Normal message flow — questions, observations, additions — happens inside Observe as usual. These two modes cover the case where the loop is already past Observe.

The four phases are the Orchestrator's anatomy — not a separately named module. The Loop Library is the one named complement. Additional modules may be added alongside Loop Library as the system earns them.

**Control loop sizes:** The system operates at three concentric levels — macro (the OODA loop itself, implicit), meso (loops in the Loop Library), and micro (sub-loops within those loops). This is a detail worth noting, not a major design focus.

**Full-cycle model:** The OODA loop runs completely every cycle — Observe → Orient → Decide → Act — then repeats. There is no structural uphill phase and downhill phase. Uphill and downhill are properties of what Decide selects:
- **Uphill cycle**: Decide selects eval loop (assessment, gap-finding). The system is building understanding.
- **Downhill cycle**: Decide selects code loop. The system is building things.

The system transitions naturally from uphill to downhill as artifacts accumulate across cycles. **Policy entropy as uphill/downhill criterion:** Orient's confidence field measures the entropy of the loop selection distribution. High entropy — many loops look approximately equally good, no dominant option — signals uphill: more context is needed to reduce uncertainty before committing. Low entropy — one loop clearly dominates — signals downhill: proceed. The cycle from uphill to downhill is entropy reduction through context gathering. Every Observe/Orient pass that surfaces a new constraint or artifact narrows the distribution. Confidence is a policy entropy estimate, not an impressionistic judgment. No mode-switching architecture required — it emerges from loop selection. This model is superior to a two-phase split because it is adaptive (each Act produces an artifact that informs the next Observe), resilient (failures surface to the next cycle), and necessary for S-tier targets where context needs cannot be predicted upfront.

**Observe + Orient = the uphill pair:** Within each cycle, Observe and Orient are coupled. Orient's sense-making can reveal gaps that Observe's passive reading couldn't detect. The uphill/downhill boundary sits between Orient and Decide — not between Observe and Orient. Decide never starts until Orient has produced a sufficient path statement.

**Cycle time is the metric:** Boyd's competitive insight — getting inside the opponent's OODA loop by cycling faster creates confusion and wins. A system that cycles twice as fast with decent decisions beats one that cycles once with perfect decisions. Design principle: optimize for cycle speed across all four phases.
