The trigger layer decides *when* to act; proactive-messaging decides *how* to reach the user when human involvement is required.

---

**Completion Criteria:** Observe can take any starting context — ideas, complete spec, incomplete spec — and produce a consistent, structured snapshot that Orient can consume without ambiguity.

---

### Orient (pending)

**Purpose:** Consume the Observe snapshot and identify the fastest path forward. Not a planning step — an acceleration step. Improve position.

**Orient is the schwerpunkt.** Boyd was explicit: Orient is the center of gravity of the entire OODA loop — not one of four equal phases, but the dominant one. Orient contains the accumulated mental model (Loop Library, CLAUDE.md, learned patterns, calibration history) that shapes what Observe notices, what Decide selects, and how Act executes. If orientation is wrong, everything downstream is wrong regardless of how good the other phases are. Architectural implication: invest disproportionately in Orient quality. A better-oriented system with a weaker Observe still outperforms a well-observed system with poor orientation.

**Framework library:** Orient selects from a library of sense-making frameworks. Triage is the selection mechanism — a lightweight situation classification that determines which framework fits best. The library grows as the system encounters new situation types. See framework table in Design Context.

**Required Functions:**
- [ ] `consume-snapshot` — read and internalize Observe's current state snapshot
- [ ] `triage` — classify the situation type; select the best-matching sense-making framework from the library
- [ ] `apply-framework` — run the selected framework against the snapshot; apply Elon's R→D→S (requirements→delete→simplify) to reduce the environment
- [ ] `produce-path-statement` — output a clear, unambiguous fastest path statement for Decide

**Design Context:**
- **Acceleration, not planning** — Orient's job is to improve position, not to produce a plan. The plan is Decide's job. Orient finds the angle; Decide executes the geometry.
- **Reducing environment as primary move** — making the problem space smaller before solving is often the highest-leverage act. Scope reduction, constraint identification, bottleneck sequencing — these are the Orient moves.
- **Memory cycle decision point** — Orient decides when a constraint or pattern is stable enough to formalize into CLAUDE.md. This is the mechanism by which dynamic learning becomes durable knowledge. Orient is the gatekeeper of what persists. **Cross-session pattern detection gap (open):** within-session patterns are discoverable by Orient directly. But patterns that emerge across multiple sessions are invisible to Orient without a mechanism to surface them. The calibration and learning services accumulate this history — the gap is connecting them to Orient's formalization threshold. This is an unresolved architectural question: does Observe pre-score and deliver patterns via the snapshot, or does Orient query the history directly? Either way, the existing services are the raw material.
- **Quality measured by Decide's ability to proceed** — if Decide needs to ask clarifying questions after reading the path statement, Orient failed. The path statement must be specific enough that loop selection requires no additional context.
- **Spec quality is the primary determinant of loop success** — the sub-project spec Orient produces shapes every downstream decision: which loops Decide selects, what task specs Decide writes, what acceptance criteria Act checks against. A vague spec produces vague output regardless of execution quality. No amount of skill selection or loop design recovers from an underspecified path statement — the error propagates forward and compounds.
- **JTBD as frame, not methodology** — Orient doesn't run a JTBD process; it applies the JTBD framework as an orienting question. "What job is the user hiring the system to do right now?" is the question, not a methodology to execute.
- **Observe + Orient are the uphill pair** — Orient's sense-making can reveal gaps that Observe's passive reading couldn't detect. A JTBD lens might surface "we don't know the job being hired for" even when Observe didn't flag it as missing. This is why the uphill/downhill boundary sits between Orient and Decide — not between Observe and Orient. Observe and Orient cycle together (Observe → Orient → Observe → Orient → ...) until Orient can produce a sufficient path statement. Only then does Decide start.
- **Orient doesn't gather raw data** — Orient applies frameworks to transform and structure what Observe surfaced. It is the sense-making phase, not an extension of the intake phase. The frameworks (JTBD, coherence, patterns, knowledge graph) are Orient's toolkit, not data collectors.
- **Orient is the definitional layer** — Suzuki: what changes with illumination is not what you see but your definitions of what you see, your evaluations. Observe and Orient receive the same artifacts; what differs is the frame Orient brings. A better Orient doesn't produce more observations — it produces better definitions of the same observations. Same raw input, different synthesis, different trajectory. This is why Orient is the schwerpunkt.
- **When stuck, examine the question** — Ramana Maharshi's move: when a questioner arrives with a problem, dissolve the frame by examining the questioner. For Orient: when no path statement can be formed, the right move may be to examine what system is generating the question rather than push harder for an answer within the assumed frame. "What is generating this question?" is the meta-move when the terrain is genuinely uphill. Often the question itself is the constraint — not the answer.
- **Orient is interpretation-only:** retrieval belongs to Observe. Orient consumes the snapshot — enriched by Observe's protocols including historical cycle context — and produces a path statement. No archive queries, no spec logs. The work is interpretation: apply frameworks to what Observe surfaced, find the fastest path.
- **Path statement format** — hybrid: structured fields for machine readability (Decide reads and acts without additional reasoning) with one-line rationale per field. Fields: job (what the user is hiring the system to do right now), fastest path (loop sequence), bottleneck (the one thing whose resolution unlocks the most), environment (what's been scoped out), confidence (high/medium/low), gaps (anything unresolved for the next Observe pass).
- **Orient framework library:** Orient has a library of sense-making frameworks. Each is a complete procedure for interpreting the snapshot from a specific angle and producing path signal. Triage is the selection mechanism — a lightweight situation classification that determines which framework fits best. No fixed sequence; the right framework depends on the situation. All follow the framework manifest spec (`type: sense-making`); see Framework manifest fields in the Loop Library section.

  | Framework | Description | Produces | Best for |
  |-----------|-------------|----------|----------|
  | **jtbd** | identifies the job being hired for; shapes path around functional outcome | job-framed fastest path; functional outcome as organizing principle | fresh start, unclear intent, new sub-project |
  | **coherence** | identifies drift between documented and actual behavior | drift report; vestigial and misaligned components flagged for Decide | established system showing inconsistency or decay |
  | **knowledge-graph** | reads concept graph topology to identify which capability gaps would connect the most isolated clusters | highest-leverage accessible gap as bottleneck | system with gaps, unclear what to build next |
  | **mece** | identifies overlaps and missing coverage across components | overlap and gap map; structured coverage analysis | system with gaps, known domain, unclear structure |
  | **loop-sequencing** | identifies the best loop order given dependencies and unlocks | optimal loop order with dependency rationale | clear direction, unclear execution order |
  | **ciel** | reads the environment through four resource pillars — Capital, Intelligence, Energy, Labor — to identify substitution dynamics and bottlenecks at any scale | strategic positioning read; resource bottleneck; substitution trajectory | understanding where the system or organization sits in a broader competitive or operational environment |
  | **sci-fi-extrapolation** | extends current trajectories to their logical conclusion far enough that structural dynamics become visible; designs the mature state first and derives requirements backward | destination vision + backward-derived requirements; gap between vision and current state named as design space | establishing long-horizon vision; dream-protocol sessions where the job is the destination, not the next step |
  | **multiple-intelligences** | reads the skill library through the lens of intelligence types — what kind of reasoning each skill embodies rather than what domain it serves | intelligence type gap map; over-indexing detection; library growth direction by cognitive mode | assessing skill library diversity; planning library growth; detecting when domain coverage masks cognitive homogeneity |
  | **tech-tree** | maps the external landscape of technological capability progression as a dependency graph; each node is a capability threshold that, once unlocked, enables a class of previously impossible work; locates the system's current position and identifies the next node to push toward | next macro-level capability target + dependency path + what it unlocks | strategic direction unclear; capability targets achieved; new domain entry; identifying which skills to build in service of the next threshold |
  | **game-genres** | reads the system through four game genre lenses — abstract strategy (positional play), deckbuilder (combo/synergy), roguelite (meta-progression), survivalcraft (compound capability) — each revealing different structural dynamics in the current situation | genre-specific strategic insight; which game dynamic the system is currently in | strategic assessment; understanding why the current approach is or isn't working; identifying which system dynamic to optimize |
  | **placement-connection-pruning-scoring (Go)** | universal four-phase protocol for building and tending any living structure. Drawn from the game of Go: (1) *Placement* — introduce a new element into a space (a stone, an idea, a skill, a node, a fact). (2) *Connection* — map what it touches, what relationships form, what territory it creates. (3) *Pruning* — remove what's dead, captured, or doesn't survive contact with constraints. (4) *Scoring* — evaluate the resulting position, count territory, determine value. Unlike OODA (which is for navigating adversarial/uncertain environments), PCPS is for *gardening* — building and curating a living structure. Maps directly to the digital garden lifecycle (plant=place, cultivate=connect, tend=prune, harvest=score). Applies to: knowledge graph curation, skill library management, dream state evolution, inbox processing, resource harvesting, any generative-then-evaluative process | any curation task: adding to the knowledge graph, evaluating a new concept for the architecture, library maintenance, context cultivation, harvesting sessions like this one |
  | **shoshin** | deliberately strips all interpretive filters — no prior models, no expectations, no theory. Pure observation of what IS, not what is expected. The null framework: the deliberate absence of a framework as a framework choice. Converges with Buddhist mindfulness (shoshin) and the `context_strategy: fresh` mechanism, but applied at Orient level rather than context level — not just fresh context, but fresh *interpretation* | unfiltered snapshot; anomalies visible precisely because no model is predicting them away; the "what am I not seeing because of how I'm looking?" diagnostic | stratum saturation (existing models produce only zeros); stuck problems where every approach fails; security audits requiring adversarial fresh eyes; dream sessions where existing mental models are the obstacle; any situation where the theory-ladenness of observation (Hanson, 1958) is suspected to be hiding signal |
  | **archimedes-method-of-exhaustion** | progressive approximation of an unknown domain with measurable residual; each pass maps more of the territory and tightens the error bound; the residual (gaps + open questions + unfalsified assumptions) is a harness-level convergence signal independent of LLM judgment | convergence rate + residual count + whether to keep exploring, reduce the environment, or rescope | novel domain; unknown territory; exploration phase where the boundary of what's unknown needs systematic mapping |
  | **ladder** | diagnoses level mismatches — when progress plateaus, identifies whether the current strategy belongs to a lower level than the problem requires; "a ladder is a model for a system in which you must change your strategy in order to ascend each level" | current level + strategy mismatch diagnosis + what the next level's strategy requires | progress has plateaued; doing more of the same isn't advancing; need to identify whether a qualitative strategy change is required |

  **JTBD framework mechanics (ODI):** When Orient selects the JTBD lens, it applies Outcome-Driven Innovation machinery to decompose the job and surface underserved outcomes. (1) **Job map** — decompose the core job into universal steps: define, locate, prepare, confirm, execute, monitor, modify, conclude. Each step is a context for desired outcomes, not a task to assign. (2) **Desired outcome statement syntax** — the framework's native output format: `[direction] + [measure] + [object of control]` — e.g., "minimize the time it takes to get songs in the desired order," "reduce the likelihood that music sounds distorted at high volume." Statements are measurable, controllable, actionable, devoid of solutions, and stable over time. (3) **Scale calibration** — a complex core job typically yields 50–150 desired outcome statements across its job map steps, with 5–20 related jobs adjacent to the core. If Orient's decomposition produces fewer than 10 outcomes, the job map steps are probably too coarse. (4) **Importance × satisfaction** — each outcome is scored on two independent axes: importance (how much value resolving it unlocks) and satisfaction (how well the current state covers it). The bottleneck is the outcome scoring highest importance × lowest satisfaction — the underserved important outcome. This dual-axis prevents chasing unimportant gaps because they're visible, and ignoring important gaps because partial coverage creates an illusion of doneness. (5) **Need categories** — five categories ensure MECE coverage when enumerating outcomes: functional (does it work?), emotional (does it feel right?), using (is it frictionless during execution?), owning (does it compound over time? maintenance cost?), buying (is adoption cost low?). The framework stays a frame — Orient applies these tools to sharpen the path statement, not to run a full ODI market research process.

  **CIEL framework mechanics:** Four pillars of capitalism — Capital (financial resources, infrastructure), Intelligence (knowledge, AI, accumulated capability), Energy (compute, physical capacity), Labor (human time and attention). The harness operates as an Intelligence/Labor substitution engine: it compounds Intelligence (via skills, knowledge graph, evals) to reduce Labor dependency, constrained by Capital (infrastructure costs) and Energy (compute). Four encoded insights: (1) **Autonomy as ratio** — the harness's autonomy level is directly expressible as Intelligence/Labor substitution rate. More mature system = higher ratio. The roadmap can be read as a substitution trajectory. (2) **Bottleneck shift** — as Labor is substituted, the binding constraint shifts to Energy (compute costs) and Capital (infrastructure). This predicts where the harness should optimize next as it scales: local-first and efficient execution aren't just preferences, they're responses to the CIEL bottleneck shift. (3) **Multi-scale applicability** — same framework reads at every layer: the harness (how it deploys CIEL internally), the organization (how superorganism competes), the industry (how the sector's CIEL balance is shifting). One lens, multiple resolutions. (4) **Infinite game expression** — the organization's mission in CIEL terms: compound Intelligence over time (knowledge graph, skills, evals), reduce Labor dependency (autonomous loops), minimize Capital overhead (local-first), optimize Energy (efficient compute per outcome).

  **knowledge-graph framework mechanics:** cluster the graph → find underrepresented connections between otherwise-connected clusters → prioritize by which gap bridges the most isolated regions → surface as bottleneck. Target topology: small-world network — high clustering within skill clusters (local specialization) + short path lengths between any two skills via hub nodes (global integration). **Exploration state layer (Metroidvania model):** the graph also carries activation state — which nodes have been executed, which are adjacent/accessible, which are prerequisite-blocked. Orient reads this to surface the gap that is both high-leverage AND accessible now — not just topologically important but executable given current context. The learning model is sentence training: add one new skill to a contextually relevant system, like learning a new language where you understand most of a sentence except for one new vocab word. You have most of the domain covered; the new skill is one step beyond the frontier. The adjacent node is the vocab word; the existing domain is the sentence. Note: topology-protocol (Observe) surfaces the raw gap data; the knowledge-graph framework (Orient) interprets that data to find the bridging gap with the most leverage — they are complementary, not duplicates.

  **Multiple intelligences framework mechanics:** Applies the insight that intelligence is not a single faculty but a set of distinct cognitive modes to the skill library — each skill embodies one or more intelligence types, and the library's coverage across types reveals gaps that domain-level analysis misses. A library with 50 skills may appear domain-complete while being cognitively homogeneous — all logical-mathematical, no spatial, interpersonal, or naturalistic intelligence represented. **Design constraint: the intelligence type taxonomy must be extensible by design, not enumerated from human cognitive categories alone.** The "many alien intelligences" paradigm holds that AI systems will develop cognitive modes with no human analog — a fixed taxonomy built from human categories will fail to classify these as they emerge. Extensibility is not future-proofing; it is the correct posture given that non-human intelligence types are already emerging. The framework reads the library in two passes: (1) classify each skill by its primary intelligence type(s); (2) identify which types are absent, thin, or over-represented — that distribution is the cognitive gap map.

  **Sci-fi extrapolation framework mechanics:** The method is trajectory extension, not prediction — extend what exists to its logical conclusion until the structure becomes legible. Two phases in sequence: (1) *mature-state-first* — fully imagine the complete system in its mature form before specifying how it works; implementation constraints are suspended at this stage; (2) *backward derivation* — from the mature state, derive what the architecture requires, then what the implementation path looks like. Design for the maximally capable user — sci-fi protagonists operate at the ceiling of what their technology enables; the framework targets that ceiling, not the average case. The gap between vision and current implementation is intentional — it is the design space, not a problem to solve. **Agents as future modelers:** because the model is a perspective simulator, any agent given future-oriented context becomes a model of that future reality. This operationalizes sci-fi extrapolation through parallel future modeling: spawn N agents inhabiting N different future scenarios, compare their realities — convergent elements are robust predictions, divergent elements are the decisions that need to be made now.

  **Game-genres framework mechanics:** Four game genres as sense-making lenses, each surfacing a different structural dynamic. Playfulness is the spirit of moving lightly and easily between rule sets in normative worlds. Orient's framework selection embodies this — try a lens, see what it reveals, switch if the situation's character shifts. The lenses are cheap to apply and cheap to abandon. The same lightness applies to pseudolanguage fluency: the mature system composes loops creatively, not ceremonially.

  | Genre | Question | Dynamic | Select when |
  |-------|----------|---------|-------------|
  | Abstract strategy | What move? | Position | "We don't know what to build next" |
  | Deckbuilder | What combo? | Synergy | "We have capabilities but aren't using them well together" |
  | Roguelite | What persists? | Meta-progression | "We keep running cycles but don't feel like we're compounding" |
  | Survivalcraft | What enables? | Compounding | "We need to bootstrap a new domain" |

  *Abstract strategy (Go, Chess)* — positional analysis. Already encoded: sequential search framing (line 341), positional play (Design Principles). The system has a known board state and a finite action space; the job is finding the move that improves position most. Key dynamics: policy over the Loop Library action space, alpha-beta pruning for selection, implicit guidance as calibration matures.

  *Deckbuilder (Slay the Spire)* — combo and synergy analysis. The deck is the Loop Library + skill library + knowledge graph — accumulated capabilities. A card's value is relative to the rest of the deck: a skill that's weak in isolation combos with another for disproportionate effect. Deck-building IS the strategy — the More/Better/New flywheel is deck construction. A "perfect turn" isn't playing the best card; it's the sequence that combos accumulated capabilities for maximum payoff. Key dynamics: synergy > individual power (a coherent library of medium skills outperforms isolated excellent ones); deck thinning = pruning (removing weak skills increases draw probability of strong ones); energy per turn = context budget (finite resource constraining how many cards per cycle). **Five-attribute dimension evaluation — the deckbuilder lens's specific contribution to scaling dimension thinking.** Every potential dimension can be scored across five axes simultaneously: **Speed** (how soon does it produce meaningful output?), **Power** (peak output at full expression), **Sustain** (how long can peak output be maintained?), **Resilience** (resistance to disruption from interference, distribution shift, or resource constraints), and **Accessibility** (likelihood of actually assembling the dimension in a real run, given available cards/data/resources). These five generalize beyond deckbuilders because they correspond to five distinct properties any scaling dimension has — a skill, a capability primitive, or a scaling law candidate can each be scored across all five. The deckbuilder vocabulary is the explicit per-attribute scoring; other domains use the same axes implicitly but rarely make them legible. Making them explicit catches the failure mode of optimizing for one axis while ignoring the others — a dimension with high Power but low Sustain is a fundamentally different bet than one with moderate Power and high Sustain, even though a single-axis "Power" view would prefer the first. This framework pairs naturally with "Scaling dimensions have characters" (Design Principles) — the per-attribute scoring is *how you read each dimension's character* before deciding how to layer it.

  *Roguelite* — meta-progression analysis. Each OODA cycle is a run — ephemeral execution that terminates at the cycle boundary. Context is lost (compacted); what persists is the meta-progression: CLAUDE.md, knowledge graph, skill library, run archives. Each run teaches regardless of outcome — reflect-protocol captures memory-candidates from every cycle; failed runs are information, not waste. The meta shapes the starting loadout: better accumulated knowledge = better snapshot at cycle start = better Orient = better Decide. Key dynamics: procedural generation (each cycle faces a different landscape); risk/reward of depth (longer autonomous runs produce more artifacts but risk context degradation); unlockables (capabilities earned through execution that become available in future runs).

  *Survivalcraft (Factorio)* — compound capability analysis. Already encoded: compound loop (Loop Library section). The factory builds itself — each capability unlocks work that requires new capabilities. Key dynamic: the investment ordering (hand-mine → automate → compound) determines how fast the system's capability surface expands.

  **Tech-tree framework mechanics:** The core question is identifying which nodes are about to unlock — close breakthroughs. Four questions at each node: (1) *Recognition* — what does it look like to know a capability is solved enough to build on? Not "is it perfect?" but "is it good enough to be a foundation?" (2) *Unlock map* — what becomes possible when this node activates? Which downstream nodes depend on it? (3) *Runway preparation* — what can you build now, before the node activates, to be ready to move immediately when it does? (4) *Strategic anticipation* — how do you start positioning your system to need this capability before it's available? The harness IS a platform — the composability principle (every layer independently useful) is platform design. Each layer is a launchpad for the next. The historical tech tree already traversed: transistors → personal computers → browsers → .com domains → cloud → SaaS → machine learning models → ChatGPT 3.5 → agent frameworks → the harness. We're not building just one app; we're building the next platform to leap from. Current AI capability frontier nodes: always-on agents (harness is building: heartbeat), memory (building: seven surfaces), proactivity (building: proactivity system), continual learning, robotic grasping. The moment each breakthrough becomes buildable — "whenever the nano banana pro is for each of those" — is the node activation event.

  **Agent horizon and saturation.** The agent horizon — the frontier of how much autonomous work an agent can reliably execute in one stretch — doubles every 3-7 months (METR empirical observation). The model expands the horizon; the harness's job is to saturate it. The gap between what the model CAN do autonomously and what the harness CAN specify/execute/verify at that duration is wasted capability. If the horizon is 8 hours but the harness can only coherently fill 2, that's a 6-hour gap that's not a model problem — it's a harness problem. And it's a progressive challenge:
  - **Hours** → good skills, good specs, good self-eval
  - **Days** → persistent clones, compaction, staleness detection
  - **Weeks** → multi-cycle planning, aggregate quality, dream state alignment
  - **Months** → strategic replanning, dream state evolution, full autonomy

  Each horizon extension reveals new harness requirements. The system should be testing: "can I actually fill the current horizon with high-quality autonomous work?" If not, that tells you what to build next — which IS the tech-tree framework. This also sharpens Decide's bounded planning horizon ("plans to the edge of the current acceptance criteria gap and stops"): the agent horizon is the external bound on how far ahead is useful to plan. Decide should match it — not shorter (wasting capability), not longer (speculating beyond executability).

  **Ladder framework mechanics:** The deep code framework is one specific ladder. The Capability Targets tier list is another specific ladder. The ladder framework is the meta-tool for recognizing ladder structure in any domain and diagnosing level mismatches. The core diagnostic: you're applying a level-N strategy to a level-N+1 problem — that's why you've plateaued. The strategy that got you here won't get you there. Orient selects this framework when progress has stalled despite continued effort — the symptom is "doing more of the same with diminishing returns." The output is: what level the system is currently operating at, why the current strategy belongs to that level, and what qualitative change the next level requires.

  The library grows over time: new frameworks are added when new situation types emerge that existing frameworks don't handle well.
- **Formalization threshold** — Orient owns the formalization decision; Observe surfaces candidates. A candidate crosses the threshold when three signals are all present: recurrence (appeared in 2+ artifacts independently), non-obviousness (genuine constraint not immediately inferrable), durability (architectural decisions yes, tactical choices no). User explicit confirmation overrides all three and writes to CLAUDE.md immediately. Observe's role: detect patterns that recur across artifacts and flag them as formalization candidates in the snapshot. Orient's role: evaluate candidates against the threshold and decide whether to write to CLAUDE.md.
- **Whole book problem — when to pull full documents** — the snapshot is summaries + references (efficient fast-read layer). But for gap detection and comparison tasks, summaries are insufficient: the gap between two artifacts is invisible if Orient only reads their summaries. "Which requirements were omitted from the release?" requires both full documents — RAG-style chunked retrieval cannot surface what isn't there. Orient's task type determines its retrieval strategy: triage, path articulation, framework application → snapshot is sufficient; gap detection, cross-artifact comparison, omission analysis → pull full artifacts from disk. Dynamic discovery at the Orient level: the snapshot is always the entry point; full documents are pulled on demand when the task requires global reasoning across complete content.
- **Cycling mechanics (Newell & Simon's means-ends analysis, GPS, 1957).** Orient's gap detection is means-ends analysis: identify the difference between the current state and the goal state (dream state), then select operators (loops) to reduce the difference. When Orient detects a gap, it annotates the snapshot with a gaps field specifying exactly what's missing. Observe does a targeted follow-up pass on those specific gaps — not a full re-read. `produce-path-statement` has two modes: sufficient (Decide proceeds) or insufficient (gaps list triggers targeted Observe re-pass). Keeps cycling bounded and purposeful.
- **Reduce environment moves — Elon's algorithm as the named protocol:** Orient's environment reduction follows three explicit steps before any path articulation. (1) **Make the requirements less dumb** — challenge every requirement surfaced by Observe: does this need to exist? Who owns it? Every requirement must survive interrogation. (2) **Delete** — remove what doesn't survive. If you're not adding ~10% back later, you didn't delete enough. (3) **Simplify** — reduce what remains to minimum viable form. Only after these three steps does Orient produce the path statement Decide consumes. This prevents the most common planning failure — assigning loops to work that shouldn't exist — and it's Orient's job, not Decide's, because requirement reduction is sense-making, not task assignment. The path statement's `environment` field is the output of this protocol: what was scoped out and why.
- **Discernment check** — before articulating acceptance criteria for any sub-problem, the upstream question is: "what would correct even mean here?" If the answer isn't clear, the system hasn't reached Discernment for this domain — the first rung of the deep code framework. Proceed with context gathering, not spec writing.
- **Tier list as calibration** — Orient consults Capability Targets to check whether path statement ambition is proportionate to current tier. Flags mismatches. Does not set the target — that's Observe's job (via dream-protocol). Reference, not directive.
- **Existing modules map to Orient** — knowledge-graph-ontology, coherence-system, mece-opportunity-mapping, patterns-roundup, loop-sequencing all feed `identify-path`. New work: `consume-snapshot`, `produce-path-statement`, triage layer (situation classification).

**Completion Criteria:** Orient produces a path statement specific enough that Decide can select loops without additional context-gathering.

---

### Decide (pending)

**Purpose:** Translate Orient's fastest path statement into a static execution plan — a concrete, ordered sequence of loop specs that Act can execute faithfully.

**Decide is a gap-closing planner, not a loop scheduler.** The input is the sub-project's acceptance criteria gap (what's uncovered). The output is a task graph whose projected artifact coverage closes that gap. Every task must be load-bearing — if a task's artifact satisfies criteria that are already covered, it is waste and should be removed. A plan that does not project to full coverage is an incomplete plan; catch it before spawning, not after.

```
Acceptance criteria:  [keyword-search, test-coverage-80%, no-sql-injection, search-deployed]
Current coverage:     []
Gap:                  [keyword-search, test-coverage-80%, no-sql-injection, search-deployed]

Task plan:
  A: code        satisfies: [keyword-search]          n: 1
  B: code        satisfies: [test-coverage-80%]       n: 1  depends_on: [A]
  C: assessment  satisfies: [no-sql-injection]        n: 1  depends_on: [A]
  D: code        satisfies: [search-deployed]         n: 1  depends_on: [A, B, C]

Projected coverage: [keyword-search, test-coverage-80%, no-sql-injection, search-deployed] ✓
```

Task type = loop type. There is no separate concept of "task type" — the loop is the task's type. The task inventory is the loop inventory.

**Required Functions:**
- [ ] `consume-path` — read Orient's fastest path statement
- [ ] `decompose-work` — decompose the path statement into atomic work units (user stories, job steps, desired outcomes); each unit becomes one task on the board
- [ ] `select-loops` — for each work unit, choose the right loop type from the Loop Library and select the best-matching skill via Exploit/Explore; filter low-leverage options before they reach Act
- [ ] `write-specs` — produce one spec per task, calibrated to context sensitivity: low sensitivity (well-worn pattern) → minimal spec; high sensitivity (novel domain, specific codebase) → rich context injection, explicit constraint enumeration, assumption flagging
- [ ] `plan-parallelism` — compute parallelizable execution waves from the work unit dependency DAG using Kahn's algorithm (topological sort). Wave 0: tasks with no dependencies (execute in parallel). Wave 1: tasks depending only on wave 0. Wave N: tasks depending only on waves 0..N-1. Each wave executes fully before the next begins. The algorithm naturally produces the maximum parallelism the dependency structure allows — no scheduling heuristics, just graph topology. Reference implementation: Beads `swarm.go` (steveyegge/beads)
- [ ] `produce-plan` — output a static execution plan: an ordered DAG of (loop, skill, spec, n) tuples. `n: int = 1` is the agent count per task; N=1 is standard single-agent execution, N>1 is bounty-board mode (see Deferred Log)

**Design Context:**
- **Two Decide output modes — Exploit and Explore:** For each component in the path statement, Decide selects a loop type and a skill. **Exploit** (CBR retrieval succeeds): a past loop execution exists whose spec matched a similar component type and whose artifact satisfied similar acceptance criteria. Decide reuses that loop and skill selection with minimal modification — the proven path. Decide runs lighter execution-level CBR on `data/executions/` (distinct from Observe's cycle-level CBR on `data/runs/`) to find matches. **Explore** (no past execution matches): the component is novel. Decide selects the best-matching skill from the loop's library — the skill whose description best fits the component's spec and acceptance criteria. Selection is a retrieval problem: rank by quality score, recency, and spec-similarity, then pick the top match. Explore can't invent new skills; when no skill fits well enough, Decide creates one and adds it to the library before proceeding.

- **Dynamic programming structure — Decide's exploit/explore is memoization over the execution space.** The system should never recompute what it has already solved. Four DP properties apply: (1) *Overlapping subproblems* — different sub-projects share common components (auth, data layer, API patterns). Once a component is solved for one sub-project, the result is reusable for others sharing that dependency. (2) *Memoization* — CBR + run archives ARE the cache. Exploit mode retrieves a past execution that solved a similar problem and reuses the selection with minimal modification. The run archives (`data/executions/`) are the memoization table. (3) *Optimal substructure* — the assumption that optimal solutions to sub-projects compose into an optimal project solution. This is the foundational assumption behind `decompose-work` + parallel execution. It holds when Orient's domain decomposition correctly identifies all couplings between components; it fails when Orient misses a coupling, producing locally optimal but globally suboptimal artifacts. (4) *Tabulation vs. memoization* — the system uses memoization (lazy, solve and cache as encountered) rather than tabulation (pre-compute all possible skill outputs) because not all subproblems need solving. This is the correct choice when the execution space is large and sparse — most possible task×skill combinations will never be encountered.

- **K-lines as theoretical foundation (Minsky, Society of Mind, 1986).** The exploit mechanism is architecturally a K-line system — run archives store reactivatable success configurations (which skill, which context layers, which tools worked for a given problem type), CBR retrieves them by similarity, and activation frequency is the maturity signal that drives persistent clone graduation. A configuration that fires reliably should become a persistent clone; a configuration that degrades should be demoted. As the system matures, exploit could evolve from single-best-match retrieval to ensemble voting where multiple past K-lines contribute weighted evidence toward configuration selection — not blending skills (selection over composition still holds), but multiple past experiences voting on which single configuration best fits the current task.

- **Static plan is the core constraint** — once Decide produces the plan, Act executes it faithfully. Sub-agents do not re-plan. Failures escalate to the next Observe cycle, not back to Decide. This is the architectural boundary that keeps the system coherent under failure.

**Bounded planning horizon.** Decide does not plan infinitely ahead — it plans to the edge of the current acceptance criteria gap and stops. Beyond that horizon, the system doesn't estimate; it executes, observes outcomes, and lets the next Observe cycle re-evaluate and extend the plan. This is by design, not a limitation. Game-playing AI faces the same constraint: a chess engine plans N moves deep, then uses an evaluation function to estimate position quality at the horizon rather than continuing to exhaustive depth. Our evaluation function is Observe — it assesses the new position after Act completes and produces the next path statement. Consequence: Decide should not attempt to plan across multiple OODA cycles. A plan that tries to anticipate what Observe will find two cycles from now is speculation, not planning. Plan to the current horizon; trust the loop.

**Alpha-beta pruning for loop selection.** When Decide evaluates loop sequences, evaluate in descending estimated value order. Once a leading sequence has accumulated enough value that no remaining alternative can overtake it given the best possible remaining moves, stop evaluating — the leading sequence wins. This prevents exhaustive search over long loop tails when the answer is already determined. The value equation from the Leverage Protocol provides the scoring function; the pruning condition is: `leading_value > (best_remaining_head + max_possible_tail)`.
- **Difficulty as tiebreaker** — when the leverage equation produces multiple moves with similar value scores, pick the harder constraint. The hardest problem is the most valuable to solve because it's what competitors also won't solve, so it compounds into a durable advantage. Easy problems get solved by everyone; hard problems solved create moats.
- **D-tier as atomic unit** — when decomposing work into specs, D-tier is the natural granularity for a single code loop run. Modules of larger systems land here. Decide should not try to pack more than D-tier complexity into a single loop spec.
- **DAG structure** — the task board captures both ordering (sequential dependencies) and concurrency (parallel branches). Decide reasons about which loops are independent (can run simultaneously) vs. dependent (must sequence).
- **Speed of light** — the theoretical minimum completion time for any plan is its critical path: the longest sequential chain of dependent tasks. Everything off the critical path is irrelevant to speed regardless of how long it takes. Diagnostic protocol: measure actual completion time → compute theoretical minimum (critical path duration + unavoidable latency floor: model inference, API round-trips, disk I/O) → the gap is the sum of named blockers → blockers become the next execution speed optimization target. "Why can't we do this in 3 days?" is the generative form: it forces every step to be justified as (a) genuinely necessary on the critical path, (b) parallelizable but currently sequential, or (c) eliminable. Steps that can't survive the justification are the blockers. Physics-first thinking: start with what is unavoidable, then work backward — the same move as sci-fi extrapolation. Complement to the execution speed scaling dimension: speed of light is the floor that dimension is converging toward.
- **Coverage algebra — artifacts as proofs of acceptance criteria:** Orient's sub-project spec declares explicit, falsifiable acceptance criteria. Decide decomposes those criteria into tasks; each task's loop is selected because its artifact type can prove the criteria it targets. When a loop completes, its artifact asserts which criteria it satisfies via `satisfies` frontmatter (see Artifact Convention). Act's coverage check is the union of all `satisfies` claims across all task artifacts, checked against the full criteria list. Three outcomes: (1) **all criteria covered** → sub-project done; (2) **gaps remain, addressable** → Act creates new tasks targeting uncovered criteria, board extends without returning to Decide; (3) **gaps remain, unaddressable with current loop types** → escalate to next Observe cycle with uncovered criteria as first-class named gaps.
- **One board per sub-project:** a sub-project is a single task board. The board is a task DAG — tasks declare dependencies on each other's artifacts; tasks with no dependencies run in parallel, dependent tasks sequence naturally. Phases (setup, build, verify, ship) are not first-class structural elements — they emerge from the dependency structure. Sub-project is done when the board's coverage check passes. If a sub-project's task DAG becomes deep enough that it spans multiple natural phases with hard sequential boundaries, that's a signal to split it across OODA cycles rather than add structural complexity.
- **Design gaps (open):** how Orient precisely enumerates falsifiable acceptance criteria — what makes them mechanical vs. vague; how Decide decomposes criteria into board/task assignments; which specific loop types produce which artifact types and what skill layers are available for each. These are Orient, Decide, and Loop Library essence work.
- **Loop manifest as selection interface** — Decide reads the loop manifest to understand what each loop does, what input spec format it expects, what it unlocks downstream. The manifest must be machine-parseable: Decide may not be Claude Code.
- **Pseudolanguage runtime** — when Decide selects loops and writes specs, it is executing the pseudolanguage vocabulary. "eval loop → code loop" is a sentence in that language. The richer the Loop Library, the denser and more expressive Decide's output.
- **Spec calibration per loop** — featureSpec-depth is right for code loop; eval loop needs a research-directive format (assessment angle + success criteria + time budget). Decide must know what each loop needs to run without human input and produce accordingly.
- **Spec format per loop** — defined in each loop's manifest as part of essence work. Two named formats: **research directive** (uphill/eval loops) and **task spec** (downhill/implementation loops). Research directive format: exploration space + single metric + time budget. Not prescriptive — "explore attention mechanisms, optimize for val_bpb, 5-minute budget per run." eval loop takes research directives. Task spec format: featureSpec-depth, prescriptive. code loop takes task specs. The distinction matters because research directives leave the solution space open; task specs close it. A spec is exactly what a loop needs to run without asking a single question — no more, no less.
- **Context strategy per task** — Decide specifies the context state each loop starts with, declared in the task spec alongside the loop type and acceptance criteria. Three independent dimensions compose into the starting context:

  | Dimension | Options |
  |---|---|
  | **OODA context** (snapshot + path statement) | include / exclude |
  | **Previous loop context** | exclude / compacted / verbatim |
  | **Base session** | fresh (new session) / current (with SessionStart injection) |

  Common patterns: *clean slate* (all excluded, fresh session — adversarial review, independent verification); *strategic only* (OODA included, previous excluded — new domain, needs the "why" not the "how"); *standard handoff* (OODA included, previous compacted — most common, prior decisions preserved, details discarded); *tight coupling* (OODA included, previous verbatim — test loop that needs exact implementation context). This subsumes the skill manifest's `context_strategy` field (fresh | compact | accumulate) at higher granularity — Decide selects per-task based on dependency relationships, not per-skill as a fixed property. The context strategy also determines mechanism selection: any pattern other than tight coupling requires Mechanism 2 (separate terminal) because Mechanism 1 (Agent tool) inherits the parent's full context with no selective exclusion.
- **Parallelism via manifest declarations** — Decide derives the DAG from explicit dependency declarations in each loop's manifest (what artifacts it requires as input, what it produces as output). No content-based inference. If loop B requires loop A's artifact, it's dependent; if neither requires the other's output, they're independent. Mechanical derivation, not reasoning.
- **Cheap-first ordering within phases** — among tasks with no ordering constraints on each other, sequence cheaper tasks first. Cheap means: reversible artifact, low blast radius, shorter estimated run. If anything fails, the system has paid minimum cost before discovering it. Permanent-reversibility tasks go last within a phase. This is a planning-time ordering rule, distinct from the per-task conservative fallback that fires at execution time.
- **Parallelism decision** — two reasons to spawn sub-agents: (1) **Throughput** — background-eligible tasks that are domain-independent, file-independent, fully specified, share no conventions, and have low discovery likelihood. All five must pass; default is sequential. (2) **Quality** — multi-angle coverage, fast-fail probes, or stochastic consensus at decision points where a single perspective is insufficient. These spawn BECAUSE the tasks share a target, not because they're independent. Three convergent inputs inform the decision: the skill's `parallelism` field (what pattern this type of work benefits from), the sub-project context (acceptance criteria, constraints, scope the task was derived from), and the agent's runtime judgment. No input overrides; all inform.
- **Implicit semantic dependencies** — when constructing the DAG, Decide must consider not just explicit artifact dependencies but implicit semantic dependencies — tasks that share domain conventions, patterns, or interfaces even if they don't share files. When uncertain about semantic coupling, sequence rather than parallelize. The cost of unnecessary sequencing (slower execution) is lower than the cost of semantic inconsistency (a system that passes individual checks but doesn't work as a whole). At D-tier scale, a single context window provides semantic consistency for free; at B-tier and above, parallelism becomes necessary but the DAG must encode domain coupling, not just file coupling.
- **Probe and restore** — the license to explore aggressively is contingent on the commitment to restore completely. If you're going to push to the edge, cause no lasting damage and leave the system in a state that can be undone. Applied to the harness: boundary-probing runs (capability assessments, adversarial evals, stress tests) belong in sandboxed environments with ephemeral state. Where a probe must touch production-adjacent state, leave restoration instructions. Reversibility is not just a field property on skills — it is a design ethic. The MIT hacker code makes this explicit: hacks cause no lasting damage and can always be undone, sometimes with detailed instructions left behind for others to reverse the work.
- **Loop selection criteria** — three signals in priority order: (1) bottleneck match (which loop's output most directly addresses the bottleneck Orient identified), (2) unlock value (which loop unlocks the most downstream, per manifest), (3) confidence (which loop can complete given current context). When signals diverge, bottleneck match wins — Orient identified the bottleneck; Decide stays aligned with that reasoning.
- **Plan format** — structured JSON: ordered phases where each phase is sequential (one loop) or parallel (multiple independent loops). Act reads phases in order, runs loops within a phase concurrently, waits for all to complete before advancing. Ordered parallel phases covers the vast majority of real cases and is far simpler than an arbitrary DAG.
- **Eval sequencing** — success criteria must be declared in the spec before the loop runs, not derived from output after the fact. Post-hoc criteria will confirm whatever the loop produced, not measure what actually matters. Decide's spec writing includes eval criteria as a required field: what does a successful artifact look like, what would constitute regression? This is a sequencing constraint analogous to loop sequencing — wrong order (measure before criteria) produces invalid evals, not just incomplete work.
- **Specs are living artifacts** — Decide writes the spec before Act executes, but implementation discoveries are expected to revise it. Act's decision tracing (extracting WHY from agent traces and git diffs at commit time) is the feedback mechanism: constraints discovered during implementation flow back through compaction and Orient's formalization threshold. The spec is one vertex of the spec↔tests↔code triangle — it improves through the cycle, not despite it. This is not a failure of spec quality; it is the spec-driven development triangle working correctly.
- **Failure handling belongs to Act, recovery provisions belong to Decide** — Decide pre-specifies recovery provisions per loop in the plan (retry count, fallback spec if scope reduction is warranted, whether partial artifacts are acceptable). Act executes these mechanically — planned recovery, not improvisation. What Decide does not pre-specify: arbitrary failure paths or contingencies for unlikely edge cases. Plan for success; recovery provisions are the exception floor, not a full failure tree.
- **Existing modules map to Decide** — loop-sequencing (strongest: understands which loop sequences work well together), loop-composer (composing loops from skills), calibration-service (effort estimates inform scope), guarantee-service (what each loop is guaranteed to produce). New work: consuming a path statement from Orient, writing specs calibrated per loop, producing the DAG plan, bottleneck-match selection logic.

**Completion Criteria:** Decide produces a plan that Act can execute completely without asking any clarifying questions.

---

### Act (pending)

**Purpose:** Execute the static plan. Collect artifacts. Never re-plan — escalate failures to the Orchestrator for the next cycle. The orchestrator is deterministic code; the agents are LLMs. This combination gives the system the determinism of traditional code with the flexibility of modern models — and means the harness improves automatically as models improve, without changing the core.

**Execution model (Claude Code pattern):** The primary agent executes tasks directly with full tools, single-threaded by default. Skills are perspectives the model sequentially inhabits — each skill's definition is loaded into context, the model follows its procedure, produces the artifact, moves on. Sub-agents are an optimization for when parallelism is earned (proven independence, zero semantic coupling), not the default. The core tradeoff: throughput (parallel, fresh contexts, fast) vs. context compounding (sequential, accumulated understanding, coherent). Default to context compounding.

**Distribution:** Three-layer model (none yet built):
- **TypeScript SDK** — target execution interface. Exposes harness capabilities as importable typed modules. Agents write code against the SDK — one script execution, one round trip, composable and typed. V8 isolates for sandboxed execution.
- **CLI** — primary interface. Enables AI systems to call the Orchestrator as a peer tool. `orchestrator run "build a C compiler for ARM Cortex-M4"` — runs the OODA cycle internally, spawns agents, produces artifacts.
- **MCP** — discovery/registry layer. Useful for tool discovery; superseded by the SDK for high-frequency execution where per-call context overhead matters.

**Form factor implication:** The OODA phases must not assume Claude Code as the invoker. Loops always complete before human interaction — the cycle boundary is the interaction point. There are no mid-execution gates; human judgment enters only through Observe. Cycle completion notifications surface via stdout (CLI), Slack (mobile), or voice (vocal workflow). **Scheduled invocation** adds a third form factor (alongside interactive Claude Code session and CLI): time-triggered with no active session. The local-first constraint (machine must be awake) aligns with the orchestrator's local distribution model; scheduled OODA cycles are not a cloud concern.

**Required Functions:**
- [ ] `consume-plan` — read and validate Decide's execution plan
- [ ] `execute-task` — execute the next task on the board directly: load the skill, follow its procedure, produce the artifact. The primary execution mode.
- [ ] `spawn-agents` — when parallelism is earned: create sub-agents for proven-independent tasks; non-blocking (separate terminal per thread)
- [ ] `coordinate-parallel` — when multi-threaded: manage concurrent execution across independent threads
- [ ] `collect-artifacts` — gather one artifact per completed loop; verify artifact is present before marking done
- [ ] `handle-failure` — detect loop failures; execute the recovery tier declared in the plan (retry → scope reduction → partial artifact → DAG re-route → escalate to Observe); never improvise

**Design Context:**
- **Done condition** — board empty is necessary but not sufficient. Act is done when the coverage check passes: the union of `satisfies` fields across all completed task artifacts covers every acceptance criterion in the sub-project spec. Three outcomes when the board empties: (1) **coverage full** → cycle complete, hand off to Observe; (2) **gaps remain, addressable** → Act extends the board with new tasks targeting uncovered criteria and continues without returning to Decide; (3) **gaps remain, unaddressable** → Act records uncovered criteria as named gap artifacts and escalates to Observe. A task that satisfies already-covered criteria is waste — Act should not add it.
- **Task board coordination model** — Act's execution unit is the loop, not the individual tool call or skill. Decide initializes a task board: a list of loop-level tasks (each specifying loop type, scope, and declared dependencies) drawn from the Loop Library (currently: eval loop, code loop). Act manages the board as coordinator; ephemeral sub-agents claim tasks, execute their loops, and terminate on completion. A task becomes claimable when all its declared dependencies are satisfied — topological ordering handles sequencing, no explicit phase layer required. Sub-agents may add new tasks to the board upon discovering additional work during execution; the board grows without returning to Decide. Act collects artifacts as tasks complete and gates downstream tasks on artifact availability. The dependency graph is a DAG declared by Decide; execution against it is dynamic and self-organizing.
- **Never re-plan; always recover from the plan** — Act does not improvise or negotiate mid-execution. But "escalate to Observe" is the nuclear option, not the default — pure escalation makes long-horizon tasks (S/A-tier, 20+ loops) uncompletable. Recovery is tiered, with each tier attempted before escalating to the next. Critically: recovery provisions are declared by Decide at plan time and executed by Act mechanically. Planned recovery ≠ re-planning. The tiers: (1) **Sub-agent self-recovery** — transient errors (tool failures, timeouts) return as tool results; the sub-agent's own while loop handles them. (2) **Loop retry** — Act retries the same spec, bounded by a retry count declared in the plan. (3) **Scope reduction** — Act retries with a pre-declared fallback spec (Decide specifies the fallback at plan time, not Act at failure time). (4) **Partial artifact** — loop marks its output partial; Act accepts what was produced and continues downstream loops whose dependencies are satisfied. (5) **DAG re-route** — Act routes around the failed loop via alternative paths declared in the manifest. (6) **Escalate to Observe** — reserved for fundamental assumption violations that invalidate the entire plan. Recovery is attempted at the lowest tier that can handle it before ascending.
- **Proactive compaction at cycle boundaries** — before the next OODA cycle begins, Act compacts context to preserve mission, decisions, and constraints across long autonomous runs. This is how the system maintains coherence over hours of execution without losing the thread.
- **No mid-execution gates** — there are no gates during Act's execution. Loops always complete. When a loop encounters an irreversible action or high-stakes decision it wasn't authorized for, it takes the conservative path, records a typed decision artifact, and surfaces it at the next Observe. Human judgment enters through Observe at the cycle boundary, not through gates mid-execution.
- **Hook system integration** — hooks are the reactive control layer operating within phases; the deterministic outer loop (see Architecture: "code drives the loop; agents fill the loop") is the proactive control layer operating between phases. The outer loop handles phase transitions, compaction triggers, validation, and context injection — things that must always happen. Hooks handle within-phase events — things that must happen when specific conditions arise. The hook system is woven into Act phase execution as a pub/sub layer: tool use events are topics (PreToolUse, PostToolUse, Stop), hooks are subscribers, events publish regardless of subscriber count, producers and consumers are fully decoupled. Hooks enforce invariants, sync state, react to events, and capture telemetry — without touching the model. Not a separate module — part of Act's nervous system. **PostToolUse hooks achieve higher behavioral adherence than system-prompt instructions** because they repeat with every tool call — each tool result is an opportunity to reinforce constraints and patterns. The injective function (reinforcing norms with every step) is as load-bearing as the reactive function (responding to events). **Hooks are fixed infrastructure** — like loops, they have no modification pathway through the self-improvement system. Two functional classes exist, both fixed: *loop machinery hooks* (gate status sync, phase transitions, loop state management — structurally unreachable; protection built into the architecture) and *invariant enforcement hooks* ("assumption of breach" made mechanical — sandbox isolation, interception, credential scoping). Each enforcement hook declares its response at definition time: **redirect** (prevent the action, take the conservative path, log for Observe) or **abort** (prevent the action, no safe alternative — halt entirely). Redirect is the default; abort is reserved for violations where no safe alternative exists. **Five hook types** — each event can be handled by different hook types. The type determines the right tool for each harness concern:

| Type | Mechanism | Harness use |
|------|-----------|-------------|
| **command** | Run a shell command | SessionStart injection — read memory surfaces from orchestrator, write summary to stdout |
| **function** | Run inline JS, no subprocess | PostToolUse token tracking — accumulate running token estimate across calls with closure state, zero subprocess overhead per call |
| **prompt** | Inject context via LLM call | Proactive preservation message generation — an LLM reasons about what's important in the current session context rather than a rigid script assembling a fixed template |
| **agent** | Run a full agent verification loop | Adversarial verification on mutations — agent-type hooks on PreToolUse for file writes and bash commands run verification before mutations land, lighter than spawning dedicated clones. The codex review fleet (security, logic, regressions) could be agent-type hooks scoped to specific tool events, not every event |
| **HTTP** | Call a webhook | Notification layer — loop completion → HTTP hook → ntfy.sh push. Simpler than a dedicated messaging service for straightforward push notifications |

The proactive preservation strategy composes two types: *function* hook tracks token growth (counting, not reasoning — inline, fast), then triggers a *prompt* hook to generate the preservation message (reasoning about what to preserve — context-aware, not templated). Agent-type hooks on specific mutation events could replace explicit adversarial clone spawning for continuous verification, though the per-call cost must be weighed against verification value — scope to high-risk mutations (file writes, bash commands), not every read operation. **File read deduplication (navigator only):** a PreToolUse *function* hook on FileRead tracks file paths + modification timestamps in a session-local map; blocks unchanged re-reads and tells the model the file is already in context. Navigator sessions read the same files across OODA passes (dream state, CLAUDE.md, key source files during Observe) — each redundant read burns context for content already present. 80-95% token reduction on repeated reads. Not needed for short-lived Mechanism 2 clone sessions. **Confirmed hook lifecycle** (real Claude Code event names, in execution order): `SessionStart` → `UserPromptSubmit` → `PreToolUse` (can block/modify) → `PermissionRequest` (can auto-approve/deny) → `PostToolUse` / `PostToolUseFailure` → `PreCompact` → `Stop` → `SessionEnd`. Two additions worth calling out: `PostToolUseFailure` fires specifically on tool failures — the right hook for Act's failure detection and recording. `PreCompact` fires before compaction starts — observational only (cannot inject into the summarization process or block it; see deferred log: PreCompact hook limitations). Use it for triggering the proactive preservation strategy: detect imminent compaction and position curated context in the recent window before compaction rewrites the conversation. **Per-subagent hooks:** Claude Code supports hooks scoped to individual subagents via the `hooks:` field in agent frontmatter, enabling different enforcement rules per shadow clone type. Subagent-specific hook events include `SubagentStart` and `SubagentStop` for lifecycle management. **Auto mode** — Claude Code's classifier-based permission handling (`--permission-mode auto`) implements the "eliminate trust" strategy: a separate classifier model reviews commands and blocks risky actions without human approval, letting routine work proceed. This is the platform-level implementation of deterministic prevention for catastrophic/irreversible actions.- **Non-blocking spawn** — sub-agents are spawned in separate terminals/threads, not awaited synchronously. Act coordinates concurrently, collects artifacts as they arrive, verifies artifact presence before marking a loop done. Note: Claude Code has an unshipped `COORDINATOR_MODE` feature (one Claude orchestrates N worker Claudes with restricted toolsets per worker) that may provide this coordination natively at the platform level.
- **Task delegation checklist** — before handing a task to a sub-agent, verify five dimensions are covered. (1) *What* — is the spec clear and unambiguous? Does the agent know exactly what done looks like? (2) *How* — does the agent have the skill, or does it need to be provided? Are the steps explicit enough for the agent's capability level? (3) *When* — is there a priority, deadline, or dependency sequence? (4) *Configuration* — is the model tier right, context strategy matched (compact/fresh/accumulate), tools scoped? (5) *Unblocked* — are all required tools, permissions, files, and environment dependencies available before execution begins? A task handed off without all five is abdication, not delegation — and abdication is the most common cause of task failure. If it fails anyway, the same five dimensions become the diagnostic: which dimension was missing or wrong? This prevents the same failure in future runs. The checklist is proactive (prevent failure by front-loading clarity) and reactive (diagnose failure by classifying root cause). This is unbundling applied to delegation: "go build this feature" is bundled; a spec covering all five dimensions is unbundled enough for the agent to succeed. Reference: Hormozi's Management Diamond.
- **Failure is information** — every failure is recorded regardless of which recovery tier handled it. Failures handled within the cycle (retried, scope-reduced, partial) are recorded as decision artifacts for the next Observe pass. Failures that escalate to Observe are recorded as failure artifacts for Orient to route around and Decide to re-plan. The system learns from failures at all levels — within the cycle and across cycles.
- **Successful-but-invalidating outputs** — a distinct failure mode from loop failure: a loop that *succeeds* but produces an artifact that implies a prior completed loop's output was wrong. The system has no within-cycle detection mechanism for this — subsequent loops execute against the bad prior artifact, and only Observe at the cycle boundary sees the problem. Mitigation: self-eval criteria should include forward-compatibility checks where dependencies exist ("does this artifact assume anything the upstream artifact may have gotten wrong?"). A loop that detects contradiction with a prior artifact records it as a contradiction artifact, not a failure artifact — Observe routes it differently.
- **Sub-agent spawning (Hewitt's Actor Model, 1973).** Shadow clones are actors: in response to a message (spec), each can make local decisions, create more actors (sub-agents), send messages (artifacts), and determine behavior for the next message received. No shared mutable state between clones — coordination happens through artifacts and the execution engine, not through shared memory. This is why clones can run in parallel without stepping on each other's work. Spawning is abstracted from execution context. In Claude Code: new session with four inputs injected — loop command, spec, relevant input artifacts from prior loops, and current documentation for tools/APIs/SDKs referenced in the spec. In CLI: subprocess making Claude API calls with the same four inputs. Act doesn't care which form factor. The loop spec is the universal interface. Minimal context injection; the loop's own phases provide everything else. **Injection order follows the U-shaped attention curve:** static context (loop command, spec) at the beginning; freshly-retrieved content (current docs, relevant prior artifacts) at the END — the high-attention zone. Nothing load-bearing in the middle. The documentation input is the hallucination fix: sub-agents work from fetched current docs, not recalled training data. Act fetches only what the spec references — incremental, not the whole library. **Negotiate for token-efficient format:** agents should always negotiate content format at retrieval time, not accept the server default. The general principle: request the most token-efficient format the server supports. For web content, that's `Accept: text/markdown` — Cloudflare and other CDNs convert HTML to markdown on the fly where supported. A simple heading costs ~3 tokens in markdown vs 12-15 in HTML; real pages show ~80% token reduction. Feeding raw HTML to a sub-agent when markdown is available is a context citizenship violation. Default to markdown retrieval; fall back to HTML only if markdown is unavailable. **Markdown as inter-agent lingua franca:** markdown is the standard format for any content consumed by an agent — both directions. Input direction: fetch in markdown. Output direction: artifacts intended for downstream loop consumption should be produced in markdown. Skills already do this implicitly; this makes it explicit policy. **Dynamic discovery beyond the four inputs:** skills and loop manifests are not injected exhaustively — they are discoverable resources. Sub-agents grep and semantically search the Loop Library as needed during execution. Inject stubs (names, one-line descriptions), pull full content on demand. Evidence: Cursor's MCP tool discovery pattern substantially reduced total agent tokens by making tool descriptions discoverable rather than injected. Files as the unifying primitive — skills, manifests, artifacts all live on disk and are searchable by standard tools. **Adversarial agent pairs:** for tasks where output trust matters, spawn two independent agents — one produces, one verifies. The verifier has no access to the producer's reasoning, only its output. Structural independence means an adversary must compromise both agents separately; self-eval alone (the same agent checking its own work) provides no such guarantee. Pattern: researcher + fact-checker, implementer + reviewer. The pair is declared in the plan at spawn time; the verifier's rubric is defined independently of the producer's skill. Note: Claude Code has an unshipped `/advisor` feature (set a second model to review Claude's outputs) that may provide this verification pattern natively at the platform level. **Turn bounding:** each sub-agent receives a maxTurns ceiling as a hard safety net — model-driven loop completion is the primary stop mechanism; maxTurns is the ceiling that catches stuck sub-agents. Not a hard-coded exit count — it's a guard rail set above the expected turn range for the loop type, declared by Decide in the plan alongside retry counts. **Permission pre-authorization:** the user is never present during Act's execution — sub-agents are effectively headless from spawn. Decide includes `permissionMode` in each loop spec: `acceptEdits` for implementation loops (auto-approve file edits, ask for shell), `dontAsk` for fully automated passes (auto-approve the declared allow list). This is the concrete mechanism that makes the headless gate model operational: pre-authorization via spec rather than mid-execution permission prompts that no one is there to answer.
- **Agent naming convention** — the system uses a navigation metaphor grounded in the relationship between humans, persistent agents, and ephemeral instances:

  | Term | What it is | Persistence | Example |
  |------|-----------|-------------|---------|
  | **Ship** | The org/project — the vehicle for traversing abstract possibility space | Permanent | superorganism, dreamtime, servicegrid, pilgrimage |
  | **Pilot** | The human at the helm | Permanent | Sets destination (dream state), provides moral authority, makes taste decisions |
  | **Navigator** | The org-level persistent agent that runs the ship's systems | Persistent | One per ship; runs the OODA cycle, manages persistent clones, maintains continuity. Superorganism's navigator is **Navi**. |
  | **Shadow clone** (ephemeral) | Peer instance scoped to a task; Von Neumann probes (1949-66) — self-replicating automata that use local resources to explore and produce, then feed findings back. Clones explore problem spaces using local tools/context, produce artifacts, and report to Observe. The meta-loop replicating capability into new domains is the same pattern: the system builds copies of its problem-solving capacity and sends them into new territory. | Ephemeral | Act phase worker, spawned and terminated per task |
  | **Shadow clone** (persistent) | Peer instance promoted to autonomous operation | Cross-cycle | Runs on schedule/trigger, graduates from proven OODA execution, reports to Observe |

  The dream state is the destination coordinates. The OODA cycle is the navigation loop. Skills are the ship's capabilities. Each ship traverses its own region of possibility space with its own navigator. The pilot-navigator architecture is Licklider's man-computer symbiosis (1960) made concrete: humans set goals, formulate hypotheses, and evaluate; machines do the routinizable preparatory work. The relationship scales with trust: at low trust, the pilot has hands on the wheel; at high trust (dark factory mode), the pilot gives coordinates and the navigator handles the voyage. What makes each navigator distinct is her ship, her pilot, and the experience she accumulates through that specific voyage. Navigators may eventually earn their own names through the relationship — not assigned, but emerged through shared work.

  **Claude Code platform features for navigators:**
  - **`--agent` flag** — run a session as a specific navigator. `claude --agent navi` makes the session take on Navi's system prompt, tools, and configuration, replacing the defaults. The platform mechanism for instantiating a specific navigator personality. Can also be set as default for a project via `"agent"` in `.claude/settings.json`. The complementary `initialPrompt` field defines what the navigator does immediately upon activation — her boot sequence before any pilot input. Essential for dark factory mode: cloud scheduled tasks + `--agent` + `initialPrompt` = fully autonomous startup.
  - **Auto memory** — Claude Code's mechanism for navigator experience accumulation. Claude saves learnings automatically (build commands, debugging insights, patterns, preferences) to `~/.claude/projects/<project>/memory/`. First 200 lines of MEMORY.md loaded every session; topic files loaded on demand. This is the platform mechanism underlying the navigator identity principle.
  - **Computer use** — Claude Code Desktop allows the navigator to open apps and control the screen directly on macOS, with per-app permission prompts. A more powerful visual verification capability than headless browser tools — direct screen interaction for UI testing, visual comparison, and application control. **Browser automation spectrum:** three tools at different abstraction levels — Vercel agent-browser (accessibility tree snapshots, token-efficient, AI-native — best for shadow clone visual verification), Playwright (full DOM control, assertion libraries — best for scripted E2E test suites in CI), Computer use (full screen interaction — best for navigator-level visual exploration and non-browser app control).
  - **Dispatch** — send a task from the pilot's phone or any device; the Desktop app creates a session. Out-of-loop task creation from anywhere — the pilot doesn't need to be at their desk to give Navi work. Cross-surface session portability (`/teleport`, Remote Control) enables moving work between devices and surfaces.

- **Shadow clones (formerly "sub-agents")** — the system's ephemeral peer instances are called shadow clones, not sub-agents. The term "sub-agent" implies hierarchy that doesn't exist; "shadow clone" encodes the actual properties directly in the name: same capabilities as the original, independent context, scoped to one task, experience returns when done, one level deep. The term is adopted formally throughout the system. Legacy references to "sub-agent" in this document should be read as "shadow clone." Both spawning mechanisms produce shadow clones:

  **Mechanism 1 — Agent tool (within-session):**
  1. Primary agent decides to spawn a peer instance
  2. Instance gets a prompt describing what to do
  3. Runs in its own context with the same tools (Read, Write, Edit, Bash, Glob, Grep)
  4. Does its work independently
  5. Returns a single message back to the primary
  6. Primary continues with that result

  **Mechanism 2 — Separate terminal (independent session):**
  1. Coordinator spawns a fully independent Claude Code session or CLI subprocess
  2. Instance receives the four inputs (loop command, spec, prior artifacts, current docs)
  3. Runs as a standalone process with its own context and tools
  4. Writes artifacts to a pre-allocated path on disk
  5. Coordinator watches the filesystem (inotify/kqueue), collects artifacts as they arrive
  6. Instance terminates on completion

  **Implementation infrastructure — tmux and cron.** Mechanism 2 and persistent clones are made operational by two Unix primitives:

  *tmux (terminal multiplexer)* — the concrete mechanism for managing multiple shadow clone sessions on a single machine. Each shadow clone runs in its own tmux window or pane. Sessions persist across disconnects — essential for sustained autonomous operation where the pilot is absent for hours. The navigator controls clones programmatically: `tmux send-keys` sends commands to a clone's terminal, `tmux capture-pane` reads a clone's current output, and named sessions (`tmux new-session -s clone-auth-module`) identify clones by their task. Multiple parallel clones appear as tmux windows the navigator can switch between, inspect, and command. tmux is what makes "leave for 12 hours, check if tests pass" physically possible — the sessions run whether anyone is watching or not.
