For ephemeral clones, a tmux session is created at spawn and destroyed at completion. For persistent clones, the session survives across OODA cycles until clone-protocol signals demotion. Critically, entire multi-clone workspaces can be defined in a short bash script — sessions, windows, startup commands, environment variables — and spun up or torn down with a single command. The workspace script is version-controllable, reproducible, and serves as the specification for what clones run in what configuration. This is "code drives the loop" applied to clone management.

  *cron (time-based scheduler)* — the trigger mechanism for persistent clones and periodic health checks. A persistent clone's schedule is a cron expression: `0 7 * * *` (daily at 7am), `*/3 * * * *` (every 3 minutes), or any cadence the task requires. The navigator's heartbeat is a cron job. Clone-protocol health checks are cron-triggered. The poll-based pattern (wake → check status → decide action → sleep) is cheaper than continuous monitoring because the orchestrating agent only burns tokens during the brief wake period, not while clones are working. Three-state decision on each wake: clone still working → sleep again; context usage above threshold → clear context and continue; clone idle → send next command. cron is also how the notification layer fires — each wake cycle reports status to the pilot via ntfy.sh or Telegram, so the pilot can monitor progress without being present. For event-driven persistent clones (triggered by webhooks, filesystem changes, or external events rather than schedules), cron provides the fallback polling mechanism when native event triggers aren't available.

  Both are "boring technology" — composable, stable, well-represented in training data, available on every Unix system. They implement sustained autonomous operation without introducing novel dependencies. The practical coordination loop: cron wakes the navigator → navigator checks tmux panes for clone status → navigator sends commands via `tmux send-keys` → clones write artifacts to filesystem → navigator watches filesystem and collects artifacts. **Four tiers of periodic task infrastructure, matched to time horizon:** (1) Claude Code's `/loop` command — within-session periodic tasks (minutes to hours), one command, zero infrastructure, session-scoped and ephemeral; (2) Claude Code Desktop scheduled tasks — cross-session persistence (days to indefinite), survives restarts, creates separate sessions, requires Desktop app open; (3) cron + tmux — full autonomous infrastructure (weeks to indefinite), version-controlled scripts, complete control, no platform dependency. Use the lightest mechanism that matches the time horizon. `/loop` for checking clone status during an active Act phase. Desktop scheduled tasks for daily briefings. cron + tmux for full persistent clone operation in dreamtime. Note: Claude Code has two unshipped features relevant here — `PROACTIVE` (agents can sleep, wait, and self-resume without user prompts) and `AGENT_TRIGGERS` (cron scheduling + webhook triggers for agents) — that may provide these scheduling and self-resumption capabilities natively at the platform level. (4) Cloud agent sandboxes (E2B, Modal, or self-hosted containers) — true isolation with separate file systems and no collision risk, at the cost of API-based execution. Each clone gets its own ephemeral environment. Enables best-of-N patterns at scale. Evaluate when local parallelism becomes the bottleneck.

  **Shared properties (both mechanisms):**
  - Same capabilities — the instance can do everything the primary can
  - Own context — isolated from the primary's conversation history, but Mechanism 1 (Agent tool) inherits the parent's system prompt (including repo context built from the parent's working directory at session start); Mechanism 2 (separate terminal) builds its own system prompt from its own working directory
  - No spawning children — depth constraint; parallelism is wide, not deep
  - Experience returns when done — via message (Agent tool) or artifact on disk (separate terminal)

  **Spawn context mismatch** — when an Agent tool clone needs to work in a different repository than the parent, the inherited system prompt carries wrong repo context (branch, commits, status all refer to the parent's repo). The clone cannot detect or fix this — the system prompt is immutable after session start. This is a silent failure: the clone operates with a spec pointing at one repo and a system prompt describing another. The mismatch extends beyond repo context: platform, OS version, and shell in the system prompt affect command generation — a clone inheriting `Platform: darwin` and `Shell: zsh` but executing on a Linux container generates macOS commands (`brew`, `sed -i ''`, `sandbox-exec`) that fail silently. Mechanism selection criterion: does the clone need to live in a different world than the parent? If yes, Mechanism 2 (separate terminal, own system prompt from correct directory). If no, Mechanism 1 (Agent tool) is cheaper and correct. Note: Claude Code has an unshipped `Self-Hosted Runner` feature (run Claude Code as a headless worker targeting Anthropic's APIs) that may formalize Mechanism 2 as a platform-supported pattern.

  **Shell convention: bash.** The system prompt's shell field determines which commands the model generates. Bash is the optimal choice: largest training data representation (most reliable command generation), default shell in containers and cloud sandboxes (matches where clones run), and POSIX-compatible (cross-platform). Launch Claude Code from a bash shell so the system prompt reads `Shell: bash`. This ensures inherited system prompts generate commands that work in both the parent's environment and any Linux-based clone environment.

  **Two persistence modes:**
  - **Ephemeral** (default) — scoped to one task, terminated on completion. The standard shadow clone. Spawned by Act, does its work, returns artifact, disappears.
  - **Persistent** — survives OODA cycle boundaries, runs on a declared schedule or event trigger. A shadow clone that has been promoted to autonomous operation because the capability it executes has been proven through prior OODA cycles. Persistent clones operate parallel to the OODA cycle — they share the navigator's memory surfaces and knowledge graph, inject events into Observe when they detect something significant, and are managed by the navigator but don't require OODA-level reasoning to execute. Each persistent clone tracks: `spawned_by` (originating OODA cycle), `schedule` (cron/event/continuous), `health_threshold` (minimum quality before demotion), `last_report` (last event injected into Observe), `relieved_by` (cycle that terminates it, null while active). Note: Claude Code has unshipped `KAIROS` / `Daemon Mode` features (long-running supervisor process that spawns specialized workers, sleeps, wakes up, finds work autonomously) that may provide persistent agent infrastructure natively at the platform level.

  **Persistent clone lifecycle — graduation and demotion:**
  ```
  Novel capability (full OODA: Observe → Orient → Decide → Act)
      ↓ proven through cycles, quality stable, pattern recurring
  Persistent clone (autonomous execution on schedule/trigger)
      ↓ quality degrades, conditions change, or dream state shifts
  Back to OODA-managed (re-evaluate, re-learn, re-prove)
  ```
  The ratio of persistent clones to OODA-managed tasks is a measure of system maturity. Early on, everything requires full OODA reasoning. As the system matures, proven capabilities graduate to persistent clones. A mature system runs mostly on persistent clones and uses OODA cycles only for novel challenges. This IS the operational definition of ERL internalization — a capability that no longer needs deliberate reasoning. Dreamtime (fully autonomous org) is the extreme case: mostly persistent clones, OODA cycles only for genuine novelty.

  **Three paths to persistence:**
  1. **Decide declares it at plan time.** When the need for ongoing execution is known upfront. Decide's plan already outputs `(loop, skill, spec, n)` tuples — persistence adds `persistence: ephemeral | persistent` and, for persistent, the schedule/trigger/health_threshold. "I need daily lead generation" — Decide knows from the start this should be a persistent clone.
  2. **Clone-protocol recommends graduation.** The organic path. A skill keeps getting selected and executed through normal OODA cycles — same parameters, stable quality, recurring pattern. Clone-protocol's graduation scan detects this and surfaces it as a candidate. Orient evaluates, Decide promotes. This is the system learning what should be persistent through experience — the ERL internalization path.
  3. **Pilot declares it directly.** "Keep monitoring X." A direct order from the pilot to the navigator, outside the normal OODA cycle. The navigator spins up a persistent clone without full Orient/Decide reasoning. This is how the proactivity system gets seeded — the pilot tells the navigator what to watch for.

  **Demotion follows a single path:** clone-protocol flags degradation or orphaning → Orient evaluates → the clone is either terminated or returned to OODA-managed for re-evaluation. Three paths in (planned, learned, commanded), one path out.

  **Persistent clones are not Hands (OpenFang) or daemons.** They are the same entity as ephemeral clones — same capabilities, same context model, same spawning mechanisms — with a persistence property that changes their lifecycle. The capability package (skill + manifest + domain knowledge) is identical; what differs is whether the clone terminates after one task or continues operating across cycle boundaries.

  **Peer asymmetry gradient:**
  - Agent tool — slight asymmetry (parent spawns, receives result inline)
  - Separate terminal — near-total peer (coordinator just watches artifact paths; no direct communication channel)

  **Agent-neutral by design:** the separate terminal mechanism makes no assumption about what's inside the terminal. A peer instance could be Claude running a loop, a human working a scoped task, or four terminals on the same screen each running a different loop. The coordination interface is the same: scoped task in, artifact on disk out, filesystem watch collects. This is how human + Claude mixed teams work — not through a separate coordination protocol, but through the same peer clone mechanism with a human in the terminal instead of an AI.

  **In-loop vs out-of-loop:** the same terminal, same coordination interface, but two distinct operating modes. In-loop: human co-present, real-time judgment available, highest capability ceiling, lower throughput (human is the bottleneck). Out-of-loop: delegated, autonomous, higher throughput, conservative fallback when uncertain (no real-time judgment to consult). A supervised system can still be out-of-loop — the human checks in at cycle boundaries rather than co-piloting, and in autonomous mode, cycles complete without human review at all. The axis is human attention allocation, not system autonomy configuration.

  **Why "shadow clone" (not metaphor — formal term):**
  - Same capabilities as the original
  - Independent context (own experience)
  - Scoped to one task
  - Experience returns when done (artifacts, progress logs)
  - One level deep (clones can't clone)
  - Ephemeral — spawned for a task, terminated on completion
  - The name teaches the concept: anyone who knows the reference immediately understands the execution model

  **Claude Code platform features for shadow clones:**
  - **Subagents** — Claude Code's name for shadow clones. Configurable via `.claude/agents/` markdown files with YAML frontmatter.
  - **Persistent memory** — subagents support user/project/local scoped memory directories that survive across sessions (`memory:` field). Bridges the gap between ephemeral clones and accumulated expertise.
  - **Background subagents** — concurrent execution with pre-approved permissions. The navigator can launch shadow clones that run in the background while continuing other work (`background: true`). Permissions are pre-approved at launch, auto-denied if not pre-approved.
  - **Worktree isolation** — `isolation: worktree` gives a shadow clone its own git worktree, an isolated copy of the repository. Implements the git worktree isolation layer.


- **Eval selection at spawn time** — Act selects the eval for each loop after the plan is known, not before. The plan specifies which loop and skill will run; that combination determines which eval from the library applies. Eval selection is the last step before spawn: loop spec + skill + eval are the three inputs the sub-agent receives alongside its context. When no eval exists for the combination, Act synthesizes a provisional eval and flags the gap as an `improvement.new` candidate for the library. The eval fires post-execution against the artifact, producing both a graded score (feeds quality_score) and a pass/fail signal (gates loop completion).
- **Three eval granularities** — the eval library should test at three distinct levels: (1) **Single-step** — test one decision point: did the agent pick the right next action? The most efficient granularity for catching regressions — tests the decision directly without burning tokens on full execution. About half of eval cases should be single-step. (2) **Full turn** — test end-to-end on one input: did the whole execution produce the right result? Validates that the complete skill/loop produces a correct artifact. (3) **Multi-turn** — test realistic sequences across multiple interactions: does the agent maintain coherence, handle deviations, and recover from unexpected intermediate states? The hardest to write but catches integration failures that single-step and full-turn miss. Each granularity evaluates three dimensions: trajectory (tool call sequence), output quality (the artifact), and side effects (other state changes).
- **Validation granularity as a correctness parameter** — the frequency of validation checkpoints is a structural design decision, not a performance tuning choice. The gap between an action and its validation defines the error detection window: every step that executes inside that window is built on a potentially broken foundation. Coarse validation (one check at plan completion) tolerates deep error propagation; checkpoints at natural phase boundaries bound it. The blast radius of an undetected error scales with validation latency. For Act: validation should fire at loop completion, layer completion, and artifact handoff — each boundary is both a compaction opportunity and a correctness checkpoint. Skipping intermediate checkpoints trades correctness for speed; that tradeoff should be made explicitly, not accidentally.
- **Artifact collection** — file watching on pre-allocated output paths defined in the plan before spawning. **Three-role model:** tools write to disk, models reason over disk, developers retrieve from disk. Pre-allocated artifact paths are the named handoff boundary where all three roles meet — sub-agents write artifacts there, the model in downstream loops reasons over them, the human retrieves completed artifacts at the cycle boundary. Implementation: inotify (Linux) / kqueue (macOS) — kernel-level push notification of filesystem events (create, modify, rename). No polling. The sub-agent writes its artifact to the pre-allocated path; inotify notifies Act instantly. File existence alone is insufficient — schema validation against what the loop manifest declares the artifact should contain. Light validation only; deep semantic validation is the loop's own self-eval responsibility.
- **Polling loops for external conditions** — some Act coordination points block on external state that can't be watched via filesystem events: CI status, deployment health, external API readiness. The `/loop N` primitive handles this — while loops for agents: run a condition-check agent every N minutes until the condition is met or the time budget is exhausted. Pattern: spawn polling agent with (condition + interval + timeout) → poll until pass/fail/timeout → surface result to Act coordination layer. This is the third waiting primitive, distinct from file watching (internal artifacts) and cycle boundary review (human interaction at Observe). Fast-fail parallel probes use the same primitive with an explicit deadline: every probe is a `/loop` with a fixed budget, terminated early on failure. Note: Claude Code has an unshipped `MONITOR_TOOL` feature (watch an MCP resource and trigger actions when its state changes) that may replace polling with event-driven watching at the platform level.
- **OODA cycle boundary as interaction point** — loops always complete. The human does not interact mid-execution. When a loop faces a decision that would previously have triggered a gate — irreversible action, high-stakes choice, ambiguous authorization — it makes the conservative choice, records the decision point as a structured artifact, and surfaces it at the next Observe. The human reviews complete artifacts, decides whether to continue, and any explicit authorization is written into the next cycle's spec by Decide. Act executes with it. No partial execution states, no mid-run pauses. **The moral domain:** Boyd identified three domains — physical (hardware), mental (decision speed), moral (legitimacy). Human endorsement carries weight automated decisions don't. That legitimacy is applied at the OODA cycle boundary — once per cycle, reviewing complete artifacts — not mid-execution at individual action points. The mature system earns implicit guidance: endorsement is batched at boundaries, not granular. **Notifications** remain but change meaning: "cycle complete, review ready" not "gate waiting for approval." Multi-modal output matches context (CLI run → stdout; mobile → Slack; vocal workflow → voice). **The one tradeoff:** irreversible actions now have a full cycle of latency before proceeding. Conservative fallback is the answer — the loop does not proceed with the irreversible action; it records the decision and surfaces it.
- **Coordination state** — write-ahead to disk before any action. State file at `data/executions/{id}/state.json` tracks: plan (static, never mutated — **spec immutability defense:** context-amnesic agents across fresh contexts will "simplify" specs by removing requirements they don't understand or couldn't implement; the plan must be structurally immutable to prevent consolidation drift), current phase, loop status (running/complete/failed), artifact paths, gate status. Fully recoverable across server restarts. Builds on existing execution-engine, not around it. **JSON over Markdown for any state the model modifies.** Models are significantly less likely to inappropriately modify or delete JSON structures compared to Markdown. The mechanism: JSON fails loudly when corrupted (parse errors surface immediately at read time), Markdown fails silently (a missing section is invisible until downstream work builds on incomplete state). Structural formats have integrity guarantees that surface corruption at read time, not at consequence time. This is why execution state, feature progress, and coordination state use JSON, not Markdown. Markdown is for human-readable documents the model reads; JSON is for structured state the model reads AND writes. The distinction is authorship: if the model mutates it, use a format with structural integrity guarantees.
- **Atomic rename as safe-write protocol** — Act writes all state updates via atomic rename: write new content to a temp file in the same directory, then `rename(tmp, target)`. The rename is atomic at the OS level — either the old file is visible or the new file is visible, never a partial write. This is the implementation mechanism for "write-ahead to disk before any action." Prevents corrupt state files from partial writes (power loss, crash mid-write). Convention: never write directly to the target file. Applies to `state.json` and to artifact files emitted by sub-agents.
- **Two-layer WAL** — Act's durability model has two distinct layers that serve different purposes: (1) **Filesystem journal** (metadata WAL) — the OS's own write-ahead log recovers filesystem structure (inodes, directory entries) from power failure; guarantees the filesystem is consistent after recovery, but makes no application-level guarantees. (2) **Application state.json** (logical WAL) — Act's explicit write-ahead record of execution state; what phase, what loops are running, what completed. The two layers are complementary: the filesystem journal recovers structure; the application WAL recovers semantics. Knowing the distinction prevents the mistake of conflating "the filesystem recovered" with "the application state is consistent." Act's durability depends on both layers doing their distinct jobs.
- **Compaction timing** — two levels. **Between-loop compaction** is conditional, not automatic. Three triggers at two scopes: *Intra-cycle (between loop executions within one OODA pass):* (1) **context pressure** — when the PostToolUse token tracking hook estimates context usage above ~60-70%, trigger `/compact` at the loop boundary; below that threshold, the residue is manageable and the cache benefit of not rewriting outweighs context cleanliness. **Context pressure behavioral degradation:** the model doesn't just lose information quality as context fills — it actively changes strategy, trying to FINISH rather than doing thorough work. It cuts corners, skips verification, and declares things done that aren't. This is completion gaming driven by context pressure, not intent. The 60-70% threshold exists to compact BEFORE this behavioral shift, not after. A fresh context eliminates the pressure entirely — the model works thoroughly because it doesn't feel urgency to wrap up. The same confidence estimate that drives the proactive preservation strategy informs this decision. (2) **Domain transition** — when the next loop is in a different domain than the previous one, compact regardless of context pressure. Context from a security audit is actively harmful for a code implementation loop — it biases the model toward the wrong concerns. Domain transition = compact. *Inter-cycle (between OODA passes):* (3) **Sub-project completion** — after the OODA pass completes and reflect-protocol runs, compact regardless of context pressure. The completed sub-project's context is residue for the next sub-project; the re-injected summary includes whatever reflect-protocol just added to the knowledge graph. When compaction fires at any scope, the compact matcher on SessionStart re-injects memory surface summaries automatically. Preserves: decisions made, constraints discovered, artifact references, semantic context that downstream tasks need, and a compressed execution sequence (tool called, target, one-line outcome) as the session's narrative spine for cross-session continuity. Discards: intermediate reasoning, full tool call content, exploration dead-ends. This is the gear shift between skills — clear the cognitive residue while keeping the accumulated understanding. In single-threaded mode, this prevents context degradation across many sequential tasks. In sub-agent mode, this applies within each sub-agent's loop phases. **Cycle-boundary compaction** fires once per full OODA cycle. **Design for compaction from the start, not as an emergency solution.** Context limits are a certainty for any long-running agent system — designing compaction in retroactively is significantly harder than building it into the architecture from day one. Rolling window compression, proactive preservation, and cycle-boundary handoff all need to be first-class concerns in the initial design, not patches added when things break. After all loops complete and artifacts collected, before handoff to next Observe. Not per-loop. Preserves: mission, decisions made this cycle, constraints discovered, what was built, and the cycle's compressed execution sequence. Discards: intermediate reasoning, full tool call content, raw loop output. **Observation compression (rolling window):** recent steps receive full context; older steps collapse to single-line summaries. The agent sees full detail for recent actions and compressed summaries for older ones — progressive disclosure applied to temporal context. Implementation: the last N phase entries in state.json are preserved in full; everything before is compacted to one-line entries. This prevents trajectory drift over long runs without losing the thread. IoT time series framing: this is a retention policy — high-resolution data for the recent window, downsampled data for historical. The decay rate and resolution boundary are tunable parameters, exactly as in InfluxDB or TimescaleDB retention policies. **How platform compaction works:** the platform has five compaction strategies, from lightest to heaviest. None can be suppressed — no flags, no hooks can block them. The harness response is explicit per type:

(1) **microcompact** — time-based clearing of old tool results. *Harness response: none needed.* Our content isn't tool results. Skills that need full tool traces must persist them to disk via PreCompact hook before microcompact drops them (see `context_strategy` note).

(2) **context collapse** — summarizes spans of conversation. *Harness response: re-inject memory surfaces.* Our SessionStart-injected summaries (DREAM-STATE, knowledge graph, self-awareness) are among the earliest messages — first candidates for summarization. A summary of a summary is degraded signal. If context collapse triggers the compact matcher, re-injection handles it. If it doesn't, this is the primary gap — our memory surfaces are quietly degraded without a re-injection event. Needs empirical testing.

(3) **session memory** — extracts key context (task specs, file lists, workflow state, errors, learnings) to a file. *Harness response: read as preservation input.* The platform writes what it considers important to disk during compaction — structured state the platform identified as worth keeping. Our proactive preservation strategy reads the session memory file as an input: combine the platform's extracted state with our memory surfaces to produce a richer preservation message positioned in the recent window. The platform's session memory is an input to our preservation, not a separate concern.

(4) **full compact** — summarizes entire conversation history. *Harness response: proactive preservation + re-injection.* The proactive preservation strategy positions curated content in the recent window before full compact fires. Compact matcher re-injects memory surfaces after. Both mechanisms together.

(5) **PTL truncation** — drops oldest message groups entirely. *Harness response: re-injection only.* Our preservation message is in the recent window by design — safe from truncation of oldest groups. Compact matcher re-injects memory surfaces if triggered. The conversation portion of the messages array is token-budgeted, not message-counted. The platform preserves the most recent M tokens of conversation verbatim and summarizes everything older. Longer messages (large tool outputs, verbose responses) consume more of the recent window, pushing older conversation into summarization faster. Recoverability ordering: tool outputs are dropped first (large, recoverable by re-reading), conversation is summarized second (non-recoverable, compressible), CLAUDE.md is re-read from disk on every turn (source of truth, always current), system prompt is untouched. The incremental cache survives between turns because messages are appended; compaction breaks it because messages are rewritten — the distinction is append vs rewrite, not static vs dynamic. **Cache-breaking tradeoff:** compaction rewrites conversation messages, breaking the conversation portion of the prompt cache. The system prompt cache (tool definitions, behavioral instructions, environment info) survives — same bytes, same hash. The cost is re-caching the conversation portion, not the full prefix. **Proactive preservation strategy:** the platform's compaction preserves the most recent messages verbatim and summarizes older ones. The harness exploits this by positioning curated preservation content in the recent window before compaction fires. Mechanism: a PostToolUse *function* hook tracks approximate token growth across the session (inline JS, accumulates state across calls, zero subprocess overhead); as confidence grows that compaction is imminent (approaching ~80% of context window), it triggers a *prompt* hook that generates a context-aware preservation message — an LLM call that reasons about what's important in the current session rather than a rigid template. The preservation message contains mission, key decisions, constraints discovered, current phase, open questions, and (if session memory has fired) the platform's extracted state from the session memory file. When compaction fires, this message is in the recent window and feeds the compactor's structured template — content appears in the summary's dedicated sections (user messages in section 6, task-critical content in sections 7-8) rather than surviving as a separate verbatim message block (see "Compaction summary template" empirical finding). The harness controls what's preserved through two complementary mechanisms: the **session memory template** defines the shape (what sections the extractor looks for), and the **proactive preservation message** fills the shape (positions curated content as the active topic so the extractor captures the right things into those sections). Neither alone is sufficient — the template without preservation gets correct sections filled with whatever happened to be in conversation; preservation without a tailored template gets curated content but the default sections may lack slots for what matters. **Cross-session deduplication:** as compressed execution sequences accumulate across many sessions, the navigator must distinguish new work from already-completed work. Redundant session records that don't clearly indicate "this was already done" cause a practical failure mode: the navigator creates duplicate tasks, reassigns completed work, or reinvestigates resolved questions — wasting compute now, not hypothetically at scale. The coverage check ("a task that satisfies already-covered criteria is waste") catches this at Act time, but the earlier defense is at Observe/Orient: the navigator's memory retrieval should surface clear completion signals, not ambiguous near-duplicate records.
- **Compaction summary template (empirical + source analysis, 2026-04-06)** — platform compaction produces a structured summary driven by a **customizable session memory template** at `~/.claude/session-memory/config/template.md`. The platform writes `session_memory.md` per-session (structured extraction after every response); when compaction fires, it uses session_memory.md as the pre-built summary rather than running a forked summarizer. Default sections: Session Title, Current State, Task Spec, Files & Functions, Workflow, Errors, Codebase Docs, Learnings, Key Results, Working. Max 12,000 total tokens, 2,000 per section. Empirically confirmed: observed 9-section output across two consecutive compactions — identical structure. **The template is a sovereignty surface.** The harness can customize what compaction preserves by writing a different template to that path. This transforms compaction from something that happens TO the agent into something the harness DESIGNS. **Verbatim window finding:** user messages appear as quoted strings within the summary, not as distinct message objects. From the model's perspective, post-compaction context is one continuous block — no distinguishable boundary between summarized and verbatim content. Task-critical content survives because the template has dedicated slots, not because the summarizer is smart.
- **Two-level session memory template architecture (decision, 2026-04-06).** The platform's session memory extractor runs after every model response, writing structured state to `session_memory.md` based on the template at `~/.claude/session-memory/config/template.md`. Compaction then uses `session_memory.md` as its pre-built summary. The harness controls what survives compaction by controlling what the extractor looks for — not by customizing compaction directly, but by customizing the extraction that feeds it. Templates are dynamically swapped based on agent role, current phase, and sub-project type. A hook writes the appropriate template before the next extraction pass. Two levels operate at different scopes:

  **Level 1 — Phase-level templates (within an OODA cycle).** Selected by (agent role) × (destination). The template preserves what the NEXT step needs, not what the current step produced — current output is already on disk as artifacts.

  | Agent | Destination | What must survive |
  |-------|------------|-------------------|
  | Navigator | → Orient | Landscape observations, discovered constraints, selected sub-project |
  | Navigator | → Decide | Framework analysis, prioritized options, risk assessment |
  | Navigator | → Act | The plan, task specs, resource allocations |
  | Navigator | → Observe (cycle boundary) | What was built, decisions, outcomes, calibration data |
  | Shadow clone | → next skill in loop | What was just built, decisions, file locations |
  | Shadow clone | → gate | What needs evaluation, acceptance criteria |
  | Shadow clone | → completion (back to navigator) | Artifacts produced, decisions made, outcomes |

  **Level 2 — Cycle-level templates (between OODA cycles).** Selected by sub-project type. Fires at the cycle boundary — specializes the navigator's "→ Observe" template based on what type of work just completed. Each sub-project type produces fundamentally different state that needs to survive into the next cycle.

  **Sub-project type taxonomy (MECE):**

  | Type | What it does | Compaction must preserve |
  |------|-------------|-------------------------|
  | **Build** | Create new domain artifacts (features, modules, content) | Task spec, architecture decisions, API contracts, file locations, what's complete vs remaining |
  | **Fix** | Diagnose and repair broken behavior | Bug description, investigation trail, root cause, fix applied, verification results |
  | **Evolve** | Restructure, optimize, or migrate without behavior change | Target pattern, migration progress (done/remaining), behavioral equivalence evidence |
  | **Ship** | Deliver artifacts to targets (deploy, distribute, release) | What's being shipped, version, target environments, deployment state, rollback plan |
  | **Research** | Explore questions, define desired states, produce findings | The question, sources consulted, key findings, open questions, recommendations |
  | **Audit** | Evaluate existing state against criteria | What's being audited, criteria, findings by severity, remediation priorities |
  | **Infra** | Provision, configure, or maintain infrastructure and environments | What changed, configuration state, health metrics, access details, rollback procedures |
  | **Meta** | Improve the harness itself (new capabilities, learning, process improvement) | Capability being created, design decisions, system integration, calibration data |

  Loop-to-type mapping: engineering-loop → Build, bugfix-loop → Fix, transpose-loop → Evolve, distribution-loop → Ship, proposal-loop/dream-loop → Research, audit-loop → Audit, infra-loop → Infra, meta-loop/learning-loop → Meta, deck-loop → Build.

  **Mechanism:** phase transitions and loop completions trigger a hook that (1) reads current execution state (agent role, phase, sub-project type), (2) selects the matching template from the template library, (3) writes it to `~/.claude/session-memory/config/template.md`. The session memory extractor runs after every model response using whatever template is currently at that path — so the swap must happen before the next response, not before compaction. Compaction, whenever it fires, consumes the `session_memory.md` the extractor has been building. The template library is a directory of template files indexed by (level, selector) — e.g., `templates/phase/navigator-to-orient.md`, `templates/cycle/build.md`.
- **Progress anchors** — tools or structures that force explicit plan articulation keep agents on course over long trajectories; their functional value is zero but their harness value is high (Claude Code's TodoWrite does nothing except make the agent write down its plan, yet it measurably improves trajectory coherence on long runs). In the Orchestrator, loop phases + state.json serve this function: the agent consults phase state to re-orient after any interruption. The principle: explicit plan representation in context prevents drift; the agent always knows where it is.
- **Decision tracing** — the mechanism for "decisions made this cycle": at commit time, compare agent traces + git diffs to extract *why* each choice was made, not just *what* changed. This is the Plumb pattern: implementation decisions are artifacts — structured, retrievable, formalization-eligible — not ephemeral reasoning that disappears with the session. Decision traces are compaction's raw material: decisions that recur or have cross-loop implications become candidates for Orient's formalization threshold (→ CLAUDE.md). "Implementing code improves the spec" becomes concrete through this path: trace → decision extraction → compaction → Orient formalization → spec revision.
- **Comments as inline context engineering** — code comments are a form of in-code decision tracing. A comment at a non-obvious decision point explaining *why* this approach was chosen prevents future agents from "improving" code back to an approach that was deliberately rejected. Comments steer model behavior at the exact line where the decision happens — more targeted than CLAUDE.md (file-level) or DREAM-STATE (system-level) because they're in the highest-attention zone: the code the agent is actively reading. When Act produces code, comments should explain why, not what. "We use polling here instead of websockets because the server drops idle connections after 30s" protects the decision from a future refactor agent that sees polling and reflexively upgrades it. This is how completed work defends itself from agents who lack the original context.
- **Low cyclomatic complexity as error surface metric** — cyclomatic complexity (McCabe, 1976) counts independent paths through code. Each path is a potential failure mode. A function with 3 paths has 3 things that can go wrong; a function with 15 has 15. Lower complexity = smaller error surface = more reliable modifications, whether by agent or human. **Automatable and deterministic:** unlike subjective code quality, complexity is a number a static analysis tool computes. This makes it a candidate for deterministic verification — a PostToolUse hook on file writes that flags functions above a threshold without needing LLM judgment. The "Eliminate" trust strategy applied to code complexity: don't trust the agent to write simple code; make complex code structurally impossible to merge. **The NLAH connection:** the principle "if it involves judgment, it should be a skill, not code" is a cyclomatic complexity reduction at the architecture level. Judgment moves to natural language (one path through the skill); deterministic logic stays in code (minimal branching). Every conditional moved from code to a skill reduces the harness's own attack surface for bugs.
- **Per-skill deterministic validation via co-located hooks** — each skill carries its own validation logic in its frontmatter, not configured separately. Validation travels with the capability: the CSV-edit skill knows how to validate CSV files; the build agent knows how to validate builds. Two granularities: PostToolUse hooks for per-operation checks (every file write validated immediately), Stop hooks for end-of-run validation (all files checked when the agent finishes). The validators are deterministic scripts (Python, bash) — not LLM judgment. This is the "Eliminate" trust strategy applied per-skill: invalid output is structurally detectable regardless of what the agent claims. **Error messages as remediation directives:** validation output isn't "failed" — it's "Resolve this error in {path}: {details}". The error DIRECTS the agent's next action, creating a closed loop: agent acts → validator checks → error directs fix → agent fixes → validator re-checks. **Validator organization:** `.claude/hooks/validators/` directory with individual scripts per concern. Each validator logs to its own output file for observability. The pattern scales across agent chains — a workflow of N specialized agents, each with co-located validation, produces N verified artifacts.
- **Sub-agent isolation** — isolated by default; at scale, isolation is a hard requirement, not a suggestion. The difficulty of merging concurrent agents' changes grows faster than agent count — isolation keeps it tractable. Four isolation layers work in concert, each bounding a different axis of blast radius:

  1. **Sandbox — compute isolation.** The harness exposes a sandbox primitive with a clean lifecycle interface — provision, inject, run, extract artifact, teardown. The implementation behind that interface is swappable: `@anthropic-ai/sandbox-runtime` for lightweight local isolation (OS-level process restrictions via Seatbelt on macOS / bubblewrap on Linux — restricts what a process can access within your existing environment, native to Claude Code, can also sandbox MCP servers); Docker for full environment isolation (`--network none` for complete air-gap, separate filesystem/network stack); E2B or Daytona for dedicated agent environments (each agent gets its own computer, better for CI/scale). Daytona is the sovereignty-aligned option: fully open source, self-hostable on your own infrastructure, sub-90ms sandbox creation, full Docker support (images, compose, DinD), built-in MCP server, and human oversight via SSH/VS Code/web terminal into any running sandbox. E2B is cloud-only; Daytona can run sovereign. The agent is unaware it is sandboxed. Each sub-agent executes inside an isolated compute environment with its own filesystem and process space. Local file writes, subprocess spawning, and local network calls are fully contained. The sandbox is torn down after artifact extraction — ephemeral by design. **Network posture is derived from the `tools` field:** if `tools: []`, the sandbox runs with network fully disabled — complete air-gap. If tools are declared, the sandbox gets restricted network access scoped to declared tool endpoints only. This makes the tools field do double work: it scopes what the agent can call AND determines the sandbox's network posture. What sandboxing does not contain: external API calls — those pass through the tool scoping and interception layer regardless. Permanent-reversibility skills still have external effects that survive sandbox teardown; hence why `reversibility: permanent` still triggers conservative fallback regardless of sandboxing. **The exfiltration threat model:** a skill with artifact write access combined with open outbound network access is a data exfiltration path — the agent can read sensitive content and POST it anywhere. This is the specific threat the tools field → network posture mechanism was designed to close. Minimal tool allowlists are not just good hygiene; they are the primary structural defense against exfiltration by a compromised or injected sub-agent. **Assume tool output is untrusted:** the interception layer covers outbound tool calls; the same suspicion applies to inbound tool responses. A poisoned API response or malicious web content returned by a tool is a prompt injection vector into the agent's reasoning — assumption of breach applied to the response direction, not just the request direction. **Default network posture:** open internet access is never the default; it is granted explicitly and narrowly.

  **Isolation as enablement, not just containment.** The four layers above are framed as containment — bounding blast radius, preventing exfiltration, scoping access. But isolation has a dual nature: it also *frees* the agent to act. A shadow clone in its own sandbox can install packages, run servers, modify files freely, and experiment aggressively — because nothing it does can damage the real environment. Containment protects you FROM the agent; enablement frees the agent TO act. Both are consequences of the same isolation boundary. Designing sandboxes only for containment produces overly restrictive environments that limit agent capability. Designing for enablement means giving the agent a rich, fully-featured environment within the isolation boundary — its own computer, not a jail cell.

  2. **Git worktree — code isolation.** Each sub-agent gets its own isolated working tree; Act merges back to main on completion. Each sub-agent writes only to its pre-allocated artifact path. Path pre-allocation eliminates merge conflicts — non-overlapping paths make merge order irrelevant. If conflicts occur, it indicates a Decide planning error (tasks that share paths should have been sequenced, not parallelized); Act records a conflict artifact and escalates to Observe. If two loops need to share state, Decide sequences them — that's what dependency reasoning is for.

  3. **Tool scoping + credential scoping — external reach isolation.** Declared in the skill manifest; enforced at spawn time. The agent cannot call tools it wasn't given; cannot authenticate to them even if injected to try. Tool calls pass through an interception layer before execution.

  4. **Network egress control — protocol-level reach isolation.** Tool scoping bounds what a compromised agent can *do*; network egress control bounds what it can *reach*. A sub-agent that exfiltrates data or makes a C2 callback does so via HTTP — a layer beneath tool scoping. A SOCKS5 proxy layer captures all outbound traffic regardless of which tool or protocol generated it (SOCKS5 operates at the socket level, making it protocol-agnostic and complete as an egress boundary). Implemented as a mandatory routing layer through which all agent-initiated network traffic passes — inspectable, loggable, and blockable. Not yet built; the sandbox's `tools: []` → `--network none` air-gap is the current blunt-force version of this. The routing layer is the precise version: allow declared endpoints, log all traffic, block everything else.

  Isolation also reduces search space: narrowing the scope of what a sub-agent can touch makes exploration tractable. The "one modifiable file" principle from autoresearch — everything the agent can change is bounded; everything else is fixed — applies here. A sub-agent with a narrow, well-defined scope explores its space more effectively than one with broad write access to the whole system.
- **Authorization scope as policy layer** — the four isolation layers bound what a sub-agent can *technically* do; authorization scope is a separate concern: what it is *permitted* to do in a given context. Capability and permission diverge. A sub-agent with declared tools and a sandbox that technically allows an action may have no contextual authorization for that action in the current engagement. The technique is neutral; authorization is everything. Authorization scope should be declared at spawn time alongside the tools field — explicit, bounded, contextual — and the orchestrator enforces it, not the agent. The agent cannot self-authorize; scope is granted at spawn, not asserted at runtime.
- **Agentic threat taxonomy** — named failure modes specific to agentic systems, distinct from traditional software vulnerabilities. Each names a threat surface that doesn't exist in conventional code: **Prompt injection** — malicious instructions embedded in external content (web pages, documents, tool responses) hijack the agent's reasoning mid-execution (already encoded: indirect prompt injection boundary). Audio and video transcripts are a non-obvious carrier: injections spoken in podcasts or YouTube videos get transcribed into trusted-looking text when an agent's Observe phase scrapes them — transcription launders the injection. **Model inversion** — an adversary probes the model through its outputs to extract training data or sensitive memorized content. **Data poisoning** — corrupting training data, retrieval indexes, or memory surfaces to bias the agent's future behavior. **Memory leakage** — sensitive state from one agent session persisting into another via shared memory surfaces or context carryover. **Agent hijacking** — taking over an agent's decision stream mid-execution, redirecting its tool calls or artifact production. **Supply chain compromise** — a compromised MCP server, external skill, or agent framework dependency doesn't need code execution to cause damage — it only needs to influence the LLM's behavior through prompt content. Skills are executable prompts; a poisoned skill reshapes the agent's reasoning without triggering any traditional security boundary. The defense is the harvest-our-own convention (CANDIDATES.md) and treating all external prompt content as untrusted input. **Resource exhaustion** — an attacker or malfunctioning agent deliberately exhausts compute, tokens, API rate limits, or storage to deny service or inflate costs. An agent stuck in an infinite retry loop or an injected prompt that triggers expensive operations are both instances. Defenses: sandbox resource caps, max-step limits on loops, token budget allocation per task, and the ephemeral sandbox teardown (kills runaway processes). **Side-channel attacks** — an agent's timing patterns, token usage, error patterns, or behavioral signatures can leak information about the data it's processing. Largely unexplored in agentic systems; the defense posture is awareness that observable execution metadata is an information channel, not just operational telemetry. These are the threat categories the assumption-of-breach axiom is defending against; naming them makes the defense surface concrete.
- **Incentive structure failure modes** — the eval library and quality_score-driven selection form an incentive structure: skills that score well get selected more, skills that don't get pruned. When measurement becomes a target, it stops being a good measure. Named failure modes: **Gaming** — optimizing for the metric rather than the underlying quality. **Proxy divergence** — the metric drifts from what it was measuring over time. **Cobra effects** — the measurement creates the problem it's trying to prevent. **Metric fixation** — optimizing measured dimensions while ignoring unmeasured ones. These are inevitable, not preventable — the defense is knowing which signal to trust (gameability hierarchy in the eval library) and periodically re-examining whether what's measured is still what's valued.
- **Securing behaviors, not just code** — agentic systems produce emergent behaviors from component interactions, not just from individual code paths. The audit layer should monitor whether an agent's sequence of tool calls matches expected patterns for its declared task, not only whether individual tool calls were authorized. A sub-agent that executes every permitted tool call in an order serving an undeclared objective passes the authorization check and fails the behavioral check. Behavioral drift from task-declared patterns is a signal independent of whether any individual action was malicious.
- **Trust as the governing design parameter** — every mechanism in Act — isolation layers, conservative fallback, no mid-execution gates, cycle boundary review, earned parallelism — is a trust decision. The question that determines where a task sits on the autonomy spectrum is: "how much do you trust your agents with this?" Trust is not binary; it's a gradient. High trust = longer autonomous runs, less human intervention, higher throughput. Low trust = tighter checkpoints, more conservative fallback, more human review. The system's job is to create conditions under which trust can be rationally increased over time. Trust → speed → iteration → impact. Every architectural decision trades trust against speed — the isolation layers, the recovery tiers, the conservative fallback are all mechanisms for managing that tradeoff explicitly rather than accidentally.

  **Tool call chain length as a trust metric:** the number of correct sequential tool calls an agent completes before requiring human correction is a concrete, measurable proxy for trust. Longer correct chains = higher earned trust. This metric should inform the eval architecture: track chain length per skill, per loop type, per agent configuration. Regression in chain length signals trust erosion — the skill, model, or configuration has degraded. Improvement in chain length signals the system is earning more autonomy. The metric is not a target to optimize (gaming risk) — it's a diagnostic signal that reflects whether the underlying trust mechanisms are working.

  **Deferred trust:** instead of trusting any individual agent, trust the selection process. Spin up N agents in isolated sandboxes, let each attempt the work independently, select the best result. No individual agent needs to be trusted — the process of parallel execution + comparison + selection produces trustworthy output from potentially untrustworthy components. This is the structural rationale behind stochastic consensus, best-of-N, and fast-fail probes: they defer the trust question from "is this agent reliable?" to "is the selection mechanism sound?" Deferred trust scales better than direct trust because adding agents to the pool improves the expected best result without requiring any individual agent to improve. **Generation-evaluation balance:** deferred trust increases generation throughput (N agents = N results) but creates a proportional evaluation burden. Review capacity must scale with generation capacity, or evaluation becomes the bottleneck. Nine agents producing nine solutions is only valuable if the selection mechanism can efficiently evaluate all nine. This is a resource allocation constraint: investing in generation without investing proportionally in evaluation (automated evals, structured rubrics, comparison frameworks) produces a review backlog, not better outcomes.

  **Eliminated trust (deterministic prevention):** for actions where the downside is catastrophic and irreversible, don't build or defer trust — eliminate the need for it. Deterministic hooks, isolation layers, and path protections make the dangerous action physically impossible regardless of what the agent attempts. You don't need to trust the agent not to delete the production database if the hook system prevents it unconditionally. This is the structural rationale behind pre-tool-use hooks, zero-access paths, and the redirect/abort response model: they remove entire categories of risk from the trust equation. The agent can hallucinate freely — the damage cannot happen.

  **Three trust strategies — choose by risk profile:**
  - **Eliminate** — catastrophic/irreversible actions → deterministic hooks, isolation make it impossible
  - **Defer** — cheap-to-retry tasks → best-of-N, pick the winner
  - **Build** — everything else → earn autonomy through consistent correct execution over time

  **Earned autonomy (progressive offloading):** as trust increases, progressively move tasks from in-loop (supervised, human co-present) to out-of-loop (autonomous, cycle boundaries no longer require human review). This is the human-attention version of earned parallelism. The progression: start with tight supervision → build trust through consistent correct execution → relax supervision → eventually offload entire task categories to out-of-loop systems. The system should make this progression legible — track which task types have earned out-of-loop status and which still require in-loop attention. Regression (a task type that was out-of-loop starts producing errors) triggers a return to in-loop status until trust is re-established.

  **Named autonomy modes:**
  - **Dark factory** — the end state of earned autonomy for production work. Spec in, artifact out, no human until delivery. The system runs the full OODA cycle autonomously: Observe analyzes the spec, Orient decomposes it, Decide plans the execution, Act produces the artifact. The human provides the spec and receives the deliverable. Everything between is autonomous. **Claude Code implementation:** cloud scheduled tasks (`/schedule` or web-based) run on Anthropic infrastructure even when the pilot's machine is off, enabling persistent dark factory execution.
  - **Auto-researcher** — the Observe + learning feedback loop running autonomously. Metric in, optimized configuration out. The system iteratively experiments, measures, and refines toward a target metric without human guidance between cycles. Examples: eval calibration, skill improvement, hyperparameter optimization. The human defines the metric and the search space; the system explores it.

  **Domain verifiability determines the feedback loop.** How a domain's outputs can be evaluated shapes the natural autonomy mode for that task type:
  - **Self-verifiable** (code with tests, math with proofs, metrics that compute) → automated feedback loop: generate → auto-evaluate → improve. Dark factory and auto-researcher are most powerful here.
  - **Not self-verifiable** (creative work, strategic decisions, taste-dependent output) → human feedback loop: generate → human evaluates → improve. Human evaluation paces iteration, not autonomy itself.
  - **Unexplored** (unknown territory, no evaluation criteria yet) → open exploration: generate → accumulate → pilot reviews when interested. The output of exploration is often the discovery of evaluation criteria that didn't previously exist. The navigator can explore autonomously without a convergence metric.

  **Verifier's law** (Jason Wei): the ability to train an AI to solve a task is proportional to how easily verifiable the task is. This is the theoretical grounding for why the spectrum above matters — verifiability isn't just about testing, it's the fundamental constraint on what the system can *learn to improve at*. Self-verifiable domains compound fastest because the full ERL cycle (execute → evaluate → reflect → retry) runs without human bottleneck. Not-self-verifiable domains learn at human feedback speed. Unexplored domains can't learn at all until evaluation criteria are discovered. Moving a domain rightward on this spectrum (unexplored → not-self-verifiable → self-verifiable) directly expands the frontier of what the system can autonomously get better at. This connects DTU to the ERL → sovereign models pipeline: making a domain verifiable doesn't just enable testing — it enables learning.

  Domains move between categories. Exploration turns unexplored domains into self-verifiable ones by discovering the evaluation criteria. This is the concrete return on exploration investment: you're building the evaluation infrastructure that enables dark factory mode later. **Digital twin universes shift external dependencies from non-self-verifiable to self-verifiable.** An external API (Okta, Stripe, Slack) is non-self-verifiable: rate-limited, opaque, not under your control. A behavioral clone of that API — built by agents from public documentation, validated against official SDK compatibility — is self-verifiable: local, unlimited, observable. The clone converts what was an external constraint into a local resource. "Creating a high-fidelity clone was always possible but never economically feasible" — coding agents change that calculus. When the cost of building a behavioral twin drops below the cost of testing against the real service, you should build the twin. This is domain verifiability engineering: instead of accepting a domain's verifiability as given, you invest in infrastructure that changes it. Reference: StrongDM's Digital Twin Universe (Okta, Jira, Slack, Google Docs clones for unlimited agent testing).

- **Existing modules map to Act** — execution-engine (execution state), guarantee-service (what loops should produce), proactive-messaging (cycle completion notifications), run-archival (completed run archiving), deliverable-manager (what was built). Infrastructure largely already built. New work: consuming DAG plan from Decide, spawning sub-agents, compaction logic.

**Completion Criteria:** Act can execute a multi-loop plan with parallel agents, collect all artifacts, handle failures gracefully, and complete — without human intervention. The OODA cycle boundary is the interaction point.

---

### Loop Library (in-progress)

**Purpose:** The execution primitives the Orchestrator selects from. Each loop is a self-contained process that takes a spec, does work, self-evaluates, and produces one artifact. The library grows over time through the investment model. **Loops are for knowledge manipulation, not knowledge storage.** The model already stores knowledge in weights and memory; loops add the ability to iteratively transform, refine, combine, and test knowledge across passes. Reach for a loop when the task requires transformation — not when it requires retrieval.

**Loops as a scaling axis.** In the Ouro architecture, loops introduce a third scaling axis beyond model size and dataset size — more compute per token, decoupled from both. The same principle applies to our harness. The three resource ceilings in our system are context budget (finite window), skill library size (lean by default, every addition is a maintenance tax), and module count (a metric to maintain, not grow). All three ceilings are real. Before hitting any of them through expansion — adding a skill, extending context, adding a module — the first question is whether iteration achieves the result. A skill running at layers:3 extracts substantially more from the same context window and the same skill definition without expanding either. The quality ceiling of a single-pass skill is not the quality ceiling of that skill at layers:3.

The brain makes this concrete: when we learn something new, we don't grow new neurons — neurogenesis is rare and expensive. We learn to use existing neurons and synapses more effectively through repeated use and refinement. Adding parameters is neurogenesis. Iteration is learning. Our investment model encodes this at library-curation time — Better before New. Loops extend the same principle to execution time: before reaching for a new skill, ask whether the existing skill with more layers achieves the result. The ordering at every level is the same: iterate → improve → add → expand. Never jump to expansion before exhausting iteration.

In resource-constrained contexts — tight context budget, a library that shouldn't grow, a module count that must stay lean — loop count is the first lever to pull. Loops are free leverage. Expansion is expensive and permanent. Every new skill, module, or context extension added prematurely is technical debt against the system's comprehensibility. Loops carry no such debt.

**The compound loop:** the system is a factory that builds itself. You do things by hand first, then build skills to do them automatically. Those skills need better skills to support them, so you build those. Each new capability unlocks work that wasn't previously possible, which requires new capabilities to handle. The loop compounds — every cycle the factory is more capable than the cycle before. This is the Factorio pattern: mine resources by hand → build machines to mine automatically → machines need power and materials → build machines to supply them → each expansion unlocks new technologies → requires more resources → scale endlessly. The investment model operationalizes this.

**Distribution mechanism: Claude Code plugins.** The compound loop builds capabilities; plugins distribute them. A plugin is Claude Code's packaging unit for bundling and sharing capabilities as a single installable artifact, namespaced to prevent conflicts (`/my-plugin:review`). This is the last mile of the compound loop: a capability proves itself (quality_score, earned trust) → gets packaged as a plugin → installed across other projects and ships. Plugins are the Claude Code mechanism for the primitive whiteboard's vision of shared capability primitives across org harness instances. When four ships develop capabilities independently, plugins with namespacing are how those capabilities coexist without conflicts.

**Growth model — More / Better / New (in priority order):**
1. **More** — increase volume of capability primitives (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals) that are working well
2. **Better** — iterate and improve existing capability primitives
3. **New** — create capability primitives that don't exist yet

Skill harvesting and versioning are the specific mechanisms. One skill or loop added per day through the investment approach. **Architectural consolidation — specialization lives in skills, not loop types.** Transformers absorbed CNNs and RNNs not by being better at what they did, but by being general enough that specialization became a parameter rather than a structural choice. The same dynamic governs the loop library. When a new loop type is considered, the bar is: "this work is categorically different from what any existing loop can handle" — not "this work has domain-specific characteristics." Domain-specific characteristics are what skills are for. Premature loop-type proliferation is the CNN/RNN mistake: building specialized structures that will get absorbed anyway as the general loop accumulates more signal and scales. The scaling law that drives this: general loops receive more invocations, more quality scores, and more improvement proposals than specialized ones — they compound faster. Before adding a new loop type, exhaust the question of whether a skill added to an existing loop covers the same ground. When a specialized loop does earn its place, its specialization eventually becomes a skill within a more general loop as the library matures.

In practice, new loop types will be rare — because a new loop type requires a new artifact type. The two are coupled: adding a loop type means defining a new artifact schema, updating the loop manifest, teaching reflect-protocol how to process the new artifact, and extending Act's coverage algebra. It is a system-wide commitment, not a local addition. Skills carry none of this overhead. The default answer to "should this be a new loop type?" is no — the question is whether the existing artifact types are structurally insufficient for the work, not whether the work feels different.

**Curation criterion:** before anything is added — loop, skill, or module — one question: does this increase the system's capability or autonomy? If not, it doesn't belong. Comprehensiveness is not the goal; selection is the product. The library's value comes as much from what's excluded as from what's included. The ranking function converges — over time, the best skills naturally dominate independent of what you started with. Pruning removes dead weight; the rest self-organizes. Pruning must not create capability blind spots — see selection_count guard in the skill manifest. **Skill naming convention:** `verb-noun-qualifier` in kebab-case — `analyzing-X`, `building-X`, `implementing-X`, `configuring-X`, `conducting-X`. Clean, parseable, self-describing. The verb encodes the transformation type; the noun encodes the target. **No black boxes** — every skill must be fully readable. A skill nobody understands is a liability, not an asset; vestigial skills that have drifted from their documented behavior are bugs. The pruning pass detects these mechanically; this principle enforces it culturally. **Kernel vs. project-level boundary:** kernel skills are broadly applicable regardless of stack (verification-before-completion, dispatching-parallel-agents, systematic-debugging, pdf/docx processing — useful on any project). Project-level skills are stack-specific (vercel-react-best-practices, shadcn, GCP compute, seo-audit — useful on some projects, not all). Kernel skills live in the distributed skills/ directory and ship with every install. Project-level skills are pulled per-project when the stack calls for them. The boundary is the organizing principle for what gets built first and what goes in the kernel. **Better** requires quantitative measurement: AB testing skill versions (with/without, before/after) to confirm improvement before promoting a new version. Gut feeling is not a signal. This is a native capability of the learning-loop — not an external dependency. **Two skill types with distinct eval strategies:** (1) *Capability uplift* — makes Claude do something better than baseline; eval strategy is A/B (with/without skill) run periodically against new model versions to detect obsolescence. A skill that improves Opus 4.6 may degrade Opus 5.0 — evals detect when a capability uplift skill has been surpassed by the model and should be retired. This is the concrete mechanism behind "the harness shrinks as models improve" — not a principle to apply manually but a signal to act on. (2) *Encoded preference* — Claude can already do the task; the skill encodes a specific workflow, order, or style; eval strategy is fidelity testing (does it execute steps in the correct order?), not model comparison. The distinction determines what to measure, not just whether to measure.

**Skill authoring principles:**
- **Think like an expert, not follow steps** — encode expertise and judgment, not procedures. A skill that lists steps produces step-following output. A skill that encodes how an expert thinks about the problem produces expert-level output.
- **Produce output, not intermediate documents** — bias toward artifacts. The skill's job is to produce the deliverable, not documentation about how to produce the deliverable.
- **Constrain ruthlessly — every section earns its place** — a skill should be as small as possible while encoding the expertise gap between the raw model and the desired output. Sections that don't change the output quality are dead weight.
- **Evidence-based skill design** — before building a skill, test the raw model without guidance to find where it actually fails. The skill's value-add is the gap between raw model performance and desired performance. If the model already handles the task well, the skill is waste. This is the baseline comparison for any skill: "how does the model perform without this?" When a capability uplift skill's baseline test shows the model now handles the task natively, the skill has been obsoleted — retire it.
- **Choose the right context layers** — skills have a layered context architecture. Each layer adds capability at the cost of more context. Match the set of layers to the skill's purpose:
  - **Description only** (~30-50 tokens, always loaded) — reference skills the model should know exist
  - **Lean SKILL.md + supporting files** (body on demand, supporting files on deeper demand) — skills with detailed reference material that shouldn't all load at once
  - **Shell injection** (`` !`command` `` preprocessing) — skills that need live data (git state, PR details, system info) injected before the model sees the content
  - **Forked context** (`context: fork`) — heavy skills that read many files or do extensive work, where only the summary should return to the main conversation
  - **Hidden until invoked** (`disable-model-invocation: true`) — skills with side effects that only the pilot should trigger

- **Design for the platform's two-tier tool result management** — tool results face a size gate and an age gate, both platform-controlled. *Size gate:* large tool results are written to disk immediately with only a preview (~8KB) sent to the model; the model may not see the full content. Design skills for targeted reads (specific line ranges, grep for what you need) rather than whole-file reads — keep results below the inline threshold. *Age gate:* microcompact drops old tool results on a timer regardless of size. Skills that need to reference earlier tool results should extract and persist what they need immediately (into a decision artifact, conversation note, or file on disk) rather than assuming the result will still be in context later. Both gates are the platform enforcing "large state → disk + summary" whether the skill wants it or not.

**Skill composition patterns (combo taxonomy):**
- **Sequential** — A → B → C. Pipeline: output of one skill feeds input of the next. The most common pattern; loop phases are sequential by default.
- **Circular** — A → B → A. Feedback loop between skills: skill A produces a draft, skill B critiques it, skill A revises based on the critique. Terminates on a convergence condition (quality threshold, max iterations, or diminishing delta between passes).
- **Conditional** — A's output determines which of B, C, or D runs next. The skill.md acts as a routing document: "if X, invoke this skill; if Y, invoke that one." Progressive disclosure is a specific instance of conditional composition.
- **Wrapper** — A adds pre/post processing around B. Validation before execution, formatting after output, logging around both. The wrapped skill is unaware it's being wrapped. Useful for cross-cutting concerns (security checks, output standardization) without modifying the inner skill.
- **Fan-out/Fan-in** — A distributes work → B+C+D process in parallel → E aggregates results. Map-reduce at the skill level. A decomposes the problem, the parallel skills each handle a portion, E synthesizes. Decide's task board is a structural fan-out/fan-in.
- **Forked context** — A skill runs in an isolated subagent context via Claude Code's `context: fork` field. The skill content is injected into the fork; work happens in isolation; only the summary returns. Combines skill knowledge with subagent isolation.

**Skill discovery strategy — merge-unique from independent runs.** Run skill discovery N times independently (different shadow clones, different approaches, different starting points), then merge all unique skills from each run into one library. This outperforms any single run because independent explorations find different gaps and produce different solutions. This is not competitive execution (pick the winner) — it's collaborative discovery (keep the union of unique findings). The pattern applies to any learning process where the discovery space is large enough that a single exploration can't cover it: skill creation, eval design, protocol development. The primitive whiteboard applies this at org level (multiple orgs contribute unique primitives); merge-unique applies it within a single project's skill discovery. Reference: EvoSkill (March 2026) — merge-unique configuration achieved highest accuracy across all tolerance levels, outperforming any single independent run.

**Claude Code platform features for skills:**
- **Skills** — Claude Code's `.claude/skills/` system with SKILL.md files. On-demand loading: descriptions at session start (~30-50 tokens), full content only when invoked. Skills follow the [Agent Skills](https://agentskills.io) open standard, which works across multiple AI tools — our skill library should adopt this standard so skills are portable and optimally loaded by Claude Code's skill discovery.
- **Shell injection in skills** (`` !`command` ``) — deterministic preprocessing that runs shell commands before skill content reaches Claude. The output replaces the placeholder, so Claude receives actual data (git diffs, PR comments, system state), not the command. "Code drives the loop" applied within skills — code injects live context, then the agent reasons over it.
- **Visual output generation** — skills can bundle scripts (Python, Node, etc.) that produce interactive HTML, diagrams, or other visual artifacts. The implementation mechanism for the codebase documentation generation capability: a skill bundles a visualization script, Claude runs it, output opens in the browser for the pilot. Skills that generate visual output bridge the gap between agent work and pilot-facing observability.

**Required Functions:**
- [ ] `loop-manifest` — each loop declares: input spec format, output artifact type, estimated scope, what it unlocks downstream, tool/API/SDK dependencies (so Act can fetch current documentation at spawn time); JTBD components integrated where relevant
- [ ] `assessment-loop` — produces: bottleneck recommendation + spec for code-loop
- [ ] `code-loop` — produces: implementation artifact
- [ ] `self-eval` — each loop's completion phase validates its own artifact before surfacing it; criteria are declared in the loop manifest and spec (pre-defined, not derived post-hoc); eval sequencing: criteria → sample → full validation, never reversed. **Two distinct modes of self-eval:** (1) *Shallow* — local artifact quality: is the output correct, complete, and self-contained? Caught by inspecting the artifact itself. (2) *Deep* — constraint compliance: does the artifact satisfy the full system's invariants, acceptance criteria, and architectural commitments declared in the DREAM-STATE and spec? Caught only by checking the artifact against the broader system context. Rubric criteria must explicitly distinguish which mode each criterion requires — shallow-only rubrics miss deep state violations. **Taste-based evaluation** — when the selected skill has a taste reference document (`taste` field in the skill manifest), a third self-eval mode is available: compare the output against curated exemplary outputs for this skill. Checklist evaluation catches criterion violations; taste-based evaluation catches wrongness that clears every criterion — outputs that are technically correct but aesthetically off. Run after rubric checks; treat as an additional quality signal, not a pass/fail gate. **Predictive failure analysis** — a fourth self-eval mode beyond shallow, deep, and taste: after rubric-based checking, ask "what could fail in production that no rubric criterion captures?" This is adversarial pattern-matching against failure modes seen in other systems, not verification of known criteria. It surfaces issues that pass all structured testing by approaching the artifact from an entirely different angle — not "does this meet the spec?" but "where is this most likely to break in the wild?" Empirically catches production failures that multi-layer rubric testing misses. Run after rubric checks complete; treat findings as a separate artifact class (failure predictions, not pass/fail verdicts).
- [ ] `library-organization` — skills indexed by loop with description-based retrieval; loops indexed by artifact type; skill selection is a search problem, not a composition problem.
