Each of the four primitive libraries has a distinct search space shape requiring a different algorithm: **Protocols** — symbolic / predicate-matching: a protocol fires when a situation satisfies its trigger conditions (OODA phase, event type, artifact type); match is binary; multiple matches require explicit priority ordering; no embeddings needed. **Frameworks** — semantic / vector search: framework selection is a conceptual fit question (JTBD vs. demand/capacity/scenario is meaning proximity, not keyword match); embed both problem description and framework descriptions; retrieve by cosine similarity via ANN (HNSW as library grows). **Skills** — hybrid (three layers): (1) hard pre-filter on loop type — a document-loop skill cannot serve a code-loop task; (2) semantic ANN on spec-to-skill description match; (3) learning-to-rank on quality_score, selection_count, recency. Most complex of the four. **Evals** — faceted search: hard filter on artifact type, rank by coverage of required quality dimensions; match criteria are known and structured; no semantic ambiguity. Simplest of the four.

**Loop = semantic output type:**

Every loop maps to exactly one semantic output type — what the loop produces as an artifact. Each semantic type gets its own loop with its own skill library. Skills are scoped per loop; they are not shared across loop types.

The loop inventory (12 loops, no categories):

1. Document
2. Dataset
3. Roadmap
4. Architecture
5. Code
6. Schema
7. Visual
8. Skill
9. Framework
10. Protocol
11. Eval
12. Model

**Meta loops** (8–11): Skill, Framework, Protocol, Eval — system artifacts that govern how the harness extends and evaluates itself. Loops themselves are fixed structural entities and are not produced through a loop. Evals are the fourth capability primitive — produced by the Eval loop, selected by Act, and pruned on signal_strength.

**Model loop:** produces trained models as artifacts (.pt, .onnx, weights, checkpoints). Structurally distinct from Code loop — Code produces code that works or doesn't; Model produces a model that's better or worse on a continuous scale through iterative optimization (forward pass → loss → backward pass → weight update). The training process is inherently iterative with a specific structure that loop layers alone don't capture. Artifact type: trained model + training metrics + evaluation results. Connects to the sovereign model registry (deferred) as the production mechanism for sovereign models, and to the RL environment mode across all environment types as the training ground.

**Loop configurability primitives** — every loop can be characterized by nine structural primitives drawn from a cross-domain survey of loop types (musical, cybernetic, ecological, computational). These are the vocabulary for intentional loop design and the configurable surface the loop manifest should eventually expose. Design constraint: skill-level loop customization routes through these primitives — not ad-hoc per-loop parameters.

| Primitive | What it names |
|---|---|
| **Period** | Duration of one cycle; tempo = cycles per unit time |
| **Phase** | Position within the current cycle |
| **Gain** | What gets amplified or dampened each pass (positive vs. negative feedback) |
| **Drift** | Whether the loop stays identical or evolves across iterations |
| **Nesting** | Loops within loops; each level has its own period and state scope |
| **Self-reference** | Whether the loop references or modifies itself |
| **State boundary** | What carries across iterations vs. what resets |
| **Coupling** | Phase-locked (synchronized) vs. free-running (independent) |
| **Exit condition** | What terminates the loop; implicit vs. explicit |

A skill requiring tight feedback (short period) should declare it; a convergence task should declare an explicit exit condition; a loop designed to escalate quality standards over iterations should declare drift.

**Eval loop artifact — eval artifact:** The Eval loop produces eval artifacts: structured judgment instruments with rubric, scoring criteria, reward function, and threshold. The specific form factor — benchmark dataset, audit rubric, capability assessment, scoring template — is governed by the eval skill selected for the run. A benchmark skill produces a replayable test case dataset targeting a specific loop+skill combination; an audit skill produces a broad rubric-based judgment applicable to any artifact of a given type; a capability-gap skill produces a system-level assessment. The loop provides the structure; the skill provides the method. `applies_to` is set by the skill and scopes where the resulting eval applies — narrow (specific loop+skill) or broad (artifact type or domain).

**Collapsed into skills:** Timeline (Roadmap), Analysis/Forecast/projection (Document), Tutorial/Course (projects), FAQ (Document), ADR/RFC (Architecture), README/changelog (Code), API docs (Schema), Knowledge Graph (Schema ontology skill), Pseudolanguage (Framework), Glossary (Document or Flashcards format). Schema absorbs: Data Model, API, Infra, Design tokens, ontology/graph. Code absorbs: Feature, Test Suite. Eval absorbs: eval-set, benchmark, training-corpus (as eval skills with appropriate applies_to scope), audit, rubric-scoring, capability-gap assessment.

**Removed as loops** (became projects, skills, or dissolved): Wiki, Prompt Library, Glossary, Axioms, Flashcards, Knowledge Graph, Course, Tutorial, SOP, Recipe, Campaign, Checklist, Infographic, Diagram, Slides, Game, Proposal, PRD, Documentation, System Design Card, Design System. Assessment dissolved into the Eval loop — assessment varieties (audits, reviews, rubric-scoring, capability gaps) are eval skills that plug into the Eval loop; the loop provides structure, the skill provides method and scope. Deferred: Reel, Short, Podcast.

Artifact subtype and format are spec parameters, not skills — a Document's type (analysis, brief, report) is declared in the spec, not expressed as a skill.

**File type → artifact type mapping (serialization format ≠ artifact type):**
A file format is how an artifact is encoded, not what it is. The same .md file serializes a Document, a Roadmap, a Skill, or an Architecture spec depending on which loop produced it.

| Format group | File types | Artifact types encoded |
|---|---|---|
| Prose | `.md` `.txt` `.docx` | Document, Roadmap, Architecture (spec), Skill, Framework, Protocol, Assessment, Eval |
| Source code | `.ts` `.tsx` `.js` `.jsx` `.py` `.go` `.rs` `.java` `.rb` `.cs` `.cpp` `.swift` `.kt` `.r` `.sh` `.sql` | Code (primary), Schema (type defs, DDL) |
| Schema/spec | `.graphql` `.proto` `.tf` `.hcl` `.d.ts` | Schema |
| Config/data | `.json` `.yaml` `.toml` `.env` `.xml` `.csv` `.jsonl` `.parquet` `.xlsx` | Schema (specs, config), Dataset (records, fixtures), Architecture (topology configs) |
| Web | `.html` `.css` `.scss` `.mdx` | Code (generated/components), Document (authored) |
| Visual | `.svg` `.png` `.jpg` `.webp` `.ico` `.pptx` `.fig` `.drawio` `.mermaid` | Visual (primary), Document/Roadmap/Assessment (rendered to visual format) |
| Documents | `.pdf` | Delivery format — any loop's artifact rendered to PDF |
| Compiled/packaged | `.wasm` `.zip` `.tar.gz` `.db` `.sqlite` | Code (compiled), Dataset (packaged data) |
| Notebooks | `.ipynb` | Code + Document hybrid |
| Running | URL, daemon, container | Code (running state) — post-deploy validation → Assessment |

**Straddle rules:**
- Topology-defining configs (k8s, docker-compose) → Architecture; behavior-driving configs → Code
- Design tokens → Schema owns the spec; Code owns the delivery file
- Hook scripts → Protocol owns the spec; Code owns the implementation
- Architecture diagrams → Architecture owns rationale/decisions; Visual owns the rendered image file


**Unified primitive invocation via the Skill tool.** All four capability primitives — protocols, frameworks, skills, and evals — are stored as SKILL.md files and invoked via Claude Code's Skill tool. The Skill tool is the platform's mechanism for "invoke a procedure from a file" — it handles progressive disclosure (description at session start, full content on demand), on-demand loading, and invocation. The `type` field in frontmatter preserves the conceptual distinction: a protocol invoked via the Skill tool is still a protocol — Observe selects it, it reads a specific data source, it contributes to the snapshot. The invocation path doesn't change what it is. Storage convention:

```
.claude/skills/
  protocols/           # type: protocol — Observe selects, phase-scoped
  frameworks/          # type: framework — Orient selects, phase-scoped
  loops/{loop}/skills/ # type: skill — loop-scoped, selected by Decide
  evals/               # type: eval — Act selects post-execution, phase-scoped
```

This eliminates three unsolved storage/invocation problems (protocols, frameworks, evals) by reusing the one already solved (skills). One storage format, one invocation mechanism, one discovery mechanism, one versioning system. The four-type distinction is preserved in metadata, not in separate systems. In the knowledge graph, all four are nodes with a `type` attribute rather than four parallel node categories.

**How skills work:**

A skill is a complete, self-contained procedure — for producing a loop's artifact type, surfacing context (protocol), interpreting a snapshot (framework), or measuring an artifact (eval) — in a specific context, domain, or style. The loop selects one skill per invocation. The skill carries the full SOP — it knows what "done" looks like for its specific variant. Every skill has four interface dimensions (Ross's ICOM model from SADT, 1969): **Inputs** (spec, context, upstream artifacts), **Controls** (rubric, constraints, acceptance criteria), **Outputs** (deliverables, artifacts), and **Mechanisms** (model tier, tools, MCP servers). This is the universal skill interface — any skill, regardless of domain, connects to the system through these four channels. Skills encapsulate changeable design decisions behind stable interfaces (Parnas's information hiding, 1972) — the internal procedure can be rewritten without changing how the system selects, invokes, or evaluates the skill. Skills are not enrichment dimensions to be layered; they are recipes. A skill for "technical deep-dive document" already embeds depth, evidence, and audience calibration because that's what the recipe requires for that domain.

The hard problem is selection, not composition: given a spec, which skill fits best? That's a retrieval problem. Loop-scoped skills are indexed by loop; phase-scoped primitives (protocols, frameworks, evals) are indexed by type and selected by their respective phase.

**Skill library model:**

Each loop has an open-ended library of skills that grows over time. Skills are added through resource ingestion, pattern detection, and deliberate harvest. The library has no fixed size — a hypernarrow skill (`pytorch-model-card`, `soc2-gap-analysis`) is as valid as a broad one. Specificity is a feature, not a limitation. When no existing skill fits, create one.

Skills are scoped per loop. A skill defined for the Document loop is not available to the Code loop — it was designed for a different artifact type. Domain knowledge is baked into the skill's SOP; the loop's job is to match and execute.

**Skill manifest fields:**

```yaml
loop: document | dataset | roadmap | architecture | code | schema | visual | skill | framework | protocol | eval | model
name: kebab-case-identifier
description: one sentence — what context/domain/style this skill handles
reversibility: ephemeral | maintained | permanent
autonomy: scripted | guided | heuristic | exploratory
parallelism: single | multi-angle(N) | best-of-n(N) | stochastic(N)  # what pattern this type of work typically benefits from; default single; the agent reads this alongside the sub-project context to make the parallelism decision at runtime
context_strategy: compact | fresh | accumulate  # what cognitive posture this skill benefits from at task boundaries (cycle-level compaction always runs regardless); compact (default): between-task compaction — preserve decisions, discard reasoning; fresh: wipe and restart — for work where prior context is harmful (adversarial review, security audit, independent verification); accumulate: no between-task compaction, keep everything — for tightly coupled work where every detail matters. **Platform compaction cannot be suppressed** — no flags, no env vars, no hook can block it. `accumulate` is a harness-level intent that the platform does not respect; the platform compacts when context pressure demands it regardless. Skills that need full tool call traces (self-eval process dimensions, decision tracing, debugging) must persist traces to disk via a PreCompact hook before compaction drops them, then read traces back from disk when needed. The platform moves old tool results to disk after microcompaction — they remain retrievable by path. This aligns with "large state → disk + summary": traces are large, recoverable, and belong on disk rather than in context.
contraindications: string[] # conditions under which this skill must NOT be selected
tools: string[]             # external MCPs/tools required; Decide filters against available tools at selection time; omit or [] if skill uses only native agent capabilities
rubric: string[]            # evaluation criteria the skill's self-eval checks against
taste: string | null        # optional path to a reference document of curated exemplary outputs for this skill; enables aesthetic self-eval alongside checklist self-eval
quality_score: float        # self-eval pass rate × Observe endorsement rate; primary ranking signal
last_used: iso8601          # recency signal; prevents stale skills from coasting
selection_count: int        # weakest signal; tie-breaking only
```

- **loop** — which loop this skill belongs to; enforces scope
- **name** — identifier used by the selection index
- **description** — routing logic, not marketing language. The description is what Decide matches against the spec — it determines whether this skill gets selected at all. Precision matters: write the description to match the specific patterns this skill handles well. The more precisely the description captures the skill's actual domain, the less likely misselection. The manifest description is the positive pre-selection filter; the contraindications field is the secondary negative filter.
- **reversibility** — lifecycle of the artifact produced. Ephemeral: consumed mid-loop, not surfaced. Maintained: persists and evolves. Permanent: costly to undo (deploy, schema migration). Act uses this: permanent skills trigger conservative fallback — cycle boundary endorsement required before proceeding.
- **autonomy** — how much latitude the skill's execution takes. Drives spec richness, maxTurns budget, eval design, and recovery strategy:
  - `scripted` — deterministic steps, tight spec, tight maxTurns, binary pass/fail eval, recovery = retry same spec
  - `guided` — steps with judgment calls, procedure + constraints spec, moderate maxTurns, binary + process eval, recovery = retry with fallback variant
  - `heuristic` — principles not steps, problem + constraints spec, generous maxTurns, rubric-graded eval, recovery = different approach
  - `exploratory` — open-ended discovery, problem statement only, generous maxTurns, process-heavy rubric, recovery = scope reduction; **contraindicated for proactive execution** — exploratory skills require human in the loop to scope and redirect
- **Non-interactive execution as a design constraint** — every component in a skill's execution path must be non-interactive: no user input prompts, no interactive CLIs, no confirmation dialogs, no sudo prompts that block for response. Sub-agents run headless — no one is present to answer a prompt mid-execution. Interactive dependencies are silent loop-breakers: the loop stalls with no error, no artifact, and no recovery signal. Audit them out at skill authoring time, not at runtime. This is a precondition for autonomous operation, not a preference.
- **contraindications** — conditions under which this skill must not be selected, even if the description matches the spec. Decide applies this as a negative filter after positive matching: a skill that matches the spec but also matches a contraindication is excluded. Negative examples are as load-bearing as positive matching — explicit "when NOT to use" language significantly improves triggering accuracy. Prevents misselection at selection time rather than catching it retroactively through a degraded quality_score. Every SKILL.md should include a "When NOT to Use" section in the body for the sub-agent to self-check at runtime.
- **tools** — external MCPs/tools the skill requires. Decide checks availability before selection — a skill whose required tools are unavailable is excluded, same mechanism as contraindications. At spawn time, Act provides the agent only the tools declared here — not the full suite — and injects only the credentials for those tools (fetched JIT from a credential store, never static in the environment). This bounds blast radius on two of four axes: the agent cannot call tools it wasn't given, and cannot authenticate to them even if injected to try. Tool calls from a spawned agent pass through an interception layer before execution — the orchestrator can inspect, log, or veto any call, providing a real-time defense against prompt injection that bypasses the agent's own judgment. The third axis is sandbox isolation — the agent's local compute environment is fully contained (see Act: Sub-agent isolation). Together, all four axes implement the assumption of breach axiom. Omit or `[]` for skills that use only native agent capabilities. Makes skill dependencies explicit without reading the body.
- **rubric** — the evaluation criteria the skill checks its own output against. Makes self-eval concrete rather than impressionistic. Example: `["all acceptance criteria addressed", "no assumptions left unstated", "artifact is self-contained"]`. The pass rate in `quality_score` is computed against these criteria — without a rubric, self-eval has nothing to evaluate against.
- **quality_score** — the outcome signal, not a proxy. Computed as self-eval pass rate (checked against rubric) × Observe endorsement rate. A skill selected often but producing poor artifacts ranks lower than a newer skill that consistently delivers. Rises and falls on merit. **Negative signals are first-class inputs:** failed runs, rejected artifacts, and adversarial probes that succeed when they shouldn't are calibration signals, not noise. A skill that consistently fails a class of problem is reporting a capability boundary; a security probe that succeeds is reporting a containment boundary. Systematic failure patterns are design inputs — they define the next capability layer. The learning loop must capture failure signal with the same fidelity as success signal.
- **last_used** — recency prevents a skill from coasting on historical reputation. A skill with high quality_score but long inactivity is deprioritized.
- **selection_count** — raw usage; weakest signal. Used for tie-breaking and to identify candidates for pruning (low count + low quality_score + old last_used = pruning candidate). **Pruning guard:** low selection_count is necessary but not sufficient for pruning. Skills covering rare, high-stakes scenarios (security audits, schema migrations, disaster recovery) have low counts not because they're bad but because the situation is rare. Pruning them creates a capability blind spot — a gap you won't notice until you need it. Before pruning a low-count skill, verify it doesn't cover a scenario class with no other skill providing equivalent coverage.

**Skill selection ranking:** quality_score → last_used → spec-similarity (LLM semantic match against description) → selection_count.

**Process as evaluation signal** — completion-only scoring collapses all variation in how a problem was approached. Two agents that both complete a task can have wildly different quality of reasoning; if only completion is scored, neither the distinction nor the learning signal survives. Rubrics should include process dimensions — quality of reasoning, constraint adherence, elegance of path — not just artifact presence. The ingenuity of traversal is signal. Without process dimensions in the rubric, the self-improvement flywheel has nothing substantive to learn from — it can only observe whether the destination was reached, not how.

**Skill selection mismatch taxonomy:** not all selection failures are the same kind of failure. Three distinct types, each requiring a different response:
- *Directional mismatch* — wrong skill entirely; the spec and skill description point in different semantic directions. Response: re-route to a different skill within the same loop.
- *Magnitude mismatch* — right skill, wrong scope; the spec is in the correct domain but the task is too large or complex for this skill. Response: split the task into smaller specs, or adjust scope before re-selecting.
- *Categorical mismatch* — wrong loop type; the failure occurred before skill selection because the work unit was routed to the wrong loop entirely. Response: escalate to loop re-selection, not skill re-selection.

Currently all three surface as a low quality_score without distinguishing which kind of failure occurred. The taxonomy makes failures diagnosable and ensures the response is applied at the right level. New skills enter with no quality signal and receive an exploration budget — occasional routing regardless of rank (~10–15%, analogous to PageRank's damping factor) — so they can accumulate signal and either rise or be pruned on merit. Without the exploration budget, the selection chain fixates on incumbents and new skills never accumulate signal. The exploration budget prevents **behavioral basin collapse** — the system settling into a shrinking subset of selections where each individual choice is correct but the distribution narrows over time. No failure is detectable per-cycle; the collapse is only visible across cycles as decreasing selection diversity. Heat-protocol should track selection diversity over time to verify the budget is sufficient.

**Universal process primitives** — the atomic operations that compose into any process. Use when designing skills: map each step to its primitive type, check for completeness, identify parallelizable steps.

*Data primitives:*
- **Read** — acquire from source
- **Write** — persist to destination
- **Transform** — change from one form to another

*Evaluation primitives:*
- **Compare** — evaluate two things against each other
- **Validate** — check against criteria
- **Filter** — select subset by predicate

*Control flow primitives:*
- **Sequence** — A then B
- **Branch** — if X then A else B
- **Iterate** — repeat until condition
- **Fork/Join** — parallelize and recombine
- **Wait** — pause for condition

*Composition primitives:*
- **Aggregate** — combine many into one
- **Decompose** — split one into many
- **Route** — direct to destination based on condition

**Seed skills by loop type:** The initial skill library — seed entries per loop, same role as seed protocols in Observe and seed frameworks in Orient. Grows through ingestion and execution. Not exhaustive; the library is open-ended.

| Loop | Skill | Description |
|------|-------|-------------|
| Visual | `applying-lcars-design-language` | Apply the LCARS aesthetic to a UI: flat panels, rounded corners, color-coded function categories, information-dense displays, system state legible at a glance |
| Visual | `designing-system-design-language` | Create a new visual language for a complex system using the methodology behind LCARS. Okuda's approach: start with the system's information architecture, assign color/shape/position semantics to function categories, build a consistent grammar where every visual element communicates state, make the whole thing internally consistent enough that a user can read an unfamiliar screen by applying the grammar. The output is a design language spec, not a single UI |
| Schema | `selecting-schema-language` | Given a data modeling problem, select the right schema language (JSON Schema, GraphQL SDL, Protobuf, OpenAPI, TypeScript types, Zod, etc.) based on the domain constraints, consumer types (human, agent, service), and evolution requirements. The output is a justified selection with rationale, not just a choice |
| Schema | `designing-domain-schema` | Design a complete domain schema for a system — entity relationships, field semantics, validation rules, extension points. The parallel of `designing-system-design-language` at the data layer: build a consistent structural grammar where every field and relationship communicates meaning, internally consistent enough that an unfamiliar consumer can navigate the schema by applying the grammar. The output is a schema spec, not a single model |
| Model | `implementing-gradient-descent` | Implement gradient descent and backpropagation for a given architecture — the foundational training algorithm. Forward pass, loss computation, backward pass, weight update. The artifact is a working training loop, not just the math |
| Model | `implementing-graph-neural-network` | Implement a graph neural network for learning on graph-structured data — message passing, neighborhood aggregation, graph-level readout. Applied to domains where relationships between entities carry as much signal as the entities themselves |
| Model | `training-model-pipeline` | End-to-end model training pipeline: data preprocessing, train/val/test splits, training loop with checkpointing, evaluation metrics, model export. The artifact is a reproducible pipeline that produces a trained model, not a one-off training script |
| Eval | `generating-end-to-end-validation` | Analyze a codebase deeply — all user flows, integrations, edge cases, external services — and produce a comprehensive end-to-end validation procedure that EXERCISES the system using real tools (CLI, Docker, database queries, API calls, webhook triggers) the way a user would. Not rubric checking but system exercising. The output is a project-specific validation artifact tailored to that codebase, not a generic rubric. Run once per project to generate; reuse the generated procedure across development cycles |
| Skill | `decomposing-process-into-primitives` | Decompose any target process into the universal process primitive vocabulary. Map each step to its primitive type, check for completeness (missing validation? no branching for edge cases? no parallelizable steps?), and sequence appropriately. The primitive decomposition is the structural blueprint; the skill is the domain-specific implementation of it |

**Framework manifest fields:**

```yaml
loop: framework
name: kebab-case-identifier
description: one sentence — what domain or situation this framework covers; Orient or Decide matches against this
type: sense-making | reference-architecture | taxonomy | mental-model
produces: one sentence — what applying this framework yields
contraindications: string[]  # when this framework must NOT be applied even if description matches
rubric: string[]             # criteria for knowing the framework was applied correctly
quality_score: float         # downstream output quality when this framework was applied; same computation as skills
last_used: iso8601
selection_count: int
```

- **type** — determines where and how the framework is applied: `sense-making` (Orient's triage-selected lens — JTBD, coherence, MECE, loop-sequencing); `reference-architecture` (structured domain model applied during execution — caching architecture, CAP theorem, OODA); `taxonomy` (classification system applied cross-phase — agentic threat taxonomy, skill selection mismatch types); `mental-model` (cross-phase reasoning pattern — first principles, inversion, abduction). Type determines the selection mechanism: sense-making frameworks are triage-selected by Orient; reference-architectures are pulled by Act/sub-agents when the skill spec references a covered domain; taxonomies and mental-models are cross-phase and selected on demand.
- **description** — same precision principle as skills: write to match the specific domain or situation this framework handles well. Orient's triage and Decide's spec-matching both use this as the primary selection signal.
- **produces** — what applying this framework yields. For sense-making: path signal (e.g., "job-framed fastest path", "coherence gap list"). For reference-architectures: a structured reference spec the sub-agent reasons within. For taxonomies: a classified failure or gap list. For mental-models: a reframed problem statement.
- **contraindications** — when NOT to apply even if description matches; same mechanics as skills. Prevents misapplication at selection time rather than catching it through degraded quality_score.
- **rubric** — criteria for knowing the framework was applied correctly; the application self-check equivalent of a skill's execution rubric. Example for JTBD: `["functional job identified", "emotional dimension considered", "job executor identified", "hiring context established", "desired outcomes follow ODI syntax", "importance × satisfaction scored"]`.
- **quality_score** — how well downstream outputs performed when this framework was applied; Observe endorsement rate of outputs produced under this framework's guidance. Same computation as skills: rises and falls on merit.
- **last_used / selection_count** — same as skills: recency and raw usage; weakest signals; used for tie-breaking and pruning.

**Framework selection ranking:** quality_score → last_used → snapshot-similarity (LLM semantic match of current situation against description + type) → selection_count.

**The framework library is unified across all four primitive types.** Orient's sense-making frameworks, Act's reference-architectures, and cross-phase taxonomies and mental-models all populate the same library. Selection is filtered by type first, then ranked by quality_score → last_used → snapshot-similarity → selection_count. A sense-making framework that consistently fails to produce a sufficient path statement is pruned by the same mechanism as a skill with low quality_score.

**Deep code framework.** A maturity model for assessing depth of understanding along any dimension. Each component is a rung — a progression, not parallel dimensions to score independently. You can't have Insight without Clarity, can't have Embodiment without all five below it.

For any domain or capability:
- **Discernment** — you can tell good from bad
- **Attunement** — you can feel the grain of the specific context
- **Coherence** — your work holds together internally
- **Clarity** — your understanding is transmissible
- **Insight** — you see what others miss
- **Embodiment** — the understanding is reflexive, no longer effortful

For any node in the knowledge graph, any skill, any capability — "where on this ladder is the system's understanding?" A skill operating at Discernment level produces correct but mechanical output. A skill at Embodiment level produces output that feels like it was made by someone who deeply understands the domain. This reframes "deep code" from a quality standard into a measurement instrument. It's not "is this deep?" — it's "how deep is this, and what's the next rung?" Taste emerges at the upper rungs — where you accumulate enough experience in an area to begin to have strong opinions. It is listening to your gut. It is the sense that something is 'right'. It is the sense that something could be better. Taste is 'X matters'.

**Protocol manifest fields:**

```yaml
loop: protocol
name: kebab-case-identifier
description: one sentence — what context type this protocol surfaces; Observe's selection signal
data_source: what the protocol reads
trigger: automatic | on-demand
contraindications: string[]
rubric: string[]      # criteria for knowing the protocol surfaced its context completely and correctly
quality_score: float  # snapshot enrichment quality; how well Orient produced a path statement when this protocol contributed
last_used: iso8601
selection_count: int
```

- **data_source** — the specific data source this protocol reads; the primary differentiator between protocols. Each protocol reads a distinct data source. Protocols that read the same data source should be merged.
- **trigger** — `automatic` means the protocol runs at a defined event without being selected (reflect-protocol fires at every loop boundary); `on-demand` means Observe selects it based on snapshot gaps. Only one current protocol is automatic: reflect-protocol.
- **rubric** — criteria for knowing the protocol surfaced its context completely and correctly. Example for cbr-protocol: `["most similar past cycle identified", "path and outcome extracted", "adaptation notes produced"]`. The protocol self-checks against these before contributing to the snapshot.
- **quality_score** — downstream quality signal: how well Observe's snapshot enabled Orient to produce a path statement when this protocol contributed. Same computation as skills; same merit mechanics.
- **last_used / selection_count** — same as skills; recency and raw usage; weakest signals.

**Protocol selection ranking:** quality_score → last_used → snapshot-gap-similarity (LLM match of current snapshot gaps against description) → selection_count.

---

**Eval manifest fields:**

```yaml
loop: eval
name: kebab-case-identifier
description: one sentence — what this eval measures and what it applies to; primary selection signal
criteria: string[]         # acceptance criteria the artifact must satisfy
reward_function: string    # how the graded score is computed across criteria (e.g., "security criteria 60%, completeness 40%")
threshold: float           # minimum score for pass/fail gate (e.g., 0.8)
contraindications: string[]
signal_strength: float     # discriminating power; approaches 0 when trivially passed or never passed — primary pruning signal
last_used: iso8601
selection_count: int
```

- **description** — routing logic, not marketing language. Captures what the eval measures and what it applies to — a security posture eval for code artifacts, a gap assessment between current state and dream state, a document clarity rubric. This is the semantic selection signal; anything selecting an eval matches against the description. The more precisely it captures the eval's actual domain and target, the less likely misselection.
- **criteria** — the acceptance criteria checklist; each criterion is pass/fail. These are outer-loop outcome criteria — they measure the artifact post-execution, not the skill's execution process (that's the rubric's job). The graded score is the weighted pass rate across criteria.
- **reward_function** — how criteria are weighted into the graded score. Enables domain-appropriate emphasis: security evals might weight correctness criteria higher than completeness.
- **threshold** — pass/fail cutoff. An artifact scoring below threshold fails the eval gate and blocks loop completion. Set per-eval based on how strict the outcome bar should be for this context.
- **signal_strength** — the eval's own quality signal: is this eval discriminating? High variance in scores across uses = high signal_strength (distinguishing good from bad). An eval where everything trivially passes, or where nothing ever passes, has signal_strength → 0 — pruning candidate in both directions.
- **contraindications** — conditions under which this eval must not be applied even if the description matches.

**Eval selection ranking:** signal_strength → last_used → spec-similarity (LLM semantic match against description) → selection_count. Mirrors skill selection: description drives matching, signal_strength is the primary quality signal.

**Seed evals:** The initial eval library — seed entries to compete. Not exhaustive; the library is open-ended.

| Eval | Description | Applies to |
|------|-------------|------------|
| `autonomous-gap-closure` | Can the system close a dream state gap end-to-end without human intervention? Measures: acceptance criteria satisfied, no human gates triggered, all artifacts produced autonomously | Full OODA cycle |
| `compound-learning-rate` | Is the system measurably better this cycle than N cycles ago? Measures: quality_score trends across skills, skill library growth rate, gap closure rate improvement over time | System-level, multi-cycle |
| `taste-endorsement` | Does the human endorse the output without modification? Measures: the rate at which artifacts are accepted as-is vs. requiring revision — the gap between "correct" and "good" | Any artifact |
| `sustained-coherence` | Does the system maintain direction, intent, and context coherence across N cycles? Four dimensions: domain memory present and accessible, explicit goals maintained, progress tracked consistently, operating procedures followed. Detects: drift between first cycle's intent and last cycle's output, decisions that contradict earlier decisions, context loss across compaction boundaries, goal hallucination (the system invents objectives not in the dream state — a coherence failure caused by intent drift) | Multi-cycle, sustained operation |
| `throughput-under-load` | Can the system close N gaps within a time window without quality degradation? Measures: gap closure rate, quality_score stability under volume, cycle time consistency | Multi-cycle, sustained operation |
| `calibrated-uncertainty` | Does the system's confidence match actual outcomes? Measures: correlation between confidence estimates and actual pass rates across cycles. High correlation = well-calibrated, safe to delegate. Low correlation = the system doesn't know what it doesn't know — more dangerous than poor performance because it undermines trust in self-reports | System-level, multi-cycle |

**Gameability hierarchy.** The six evals have a gameability hierarchy:
- **Least gameable:** taste-endorsement (human judgment)
- **Moderately gameable:** calibrated-uncertainty (meta-measurement)
- **Most gameable:** the other four (automated metrics)

When evals disagree, trust the less gameable one. This is the structural Goodhart defense — not preventing gaming, but knowing which signal to trust when gaming is suspected.

---

**Artifact type system — everything is a file:**

Loop artifacts are files. The "type" is encoded in the file's extension, path convention, and `satisfies` frontmatter — not in a separate type taxonomy. Extension + path tell consumers what the file is and what to do with it. `satisfies` tells the coverage algebra what acceptance criteria it addresses. `memory_candidates` is an optional field — a list of insights the executing loop flags as potential CLAUDE.md entries; reflect-protocol processes these at loop boundary via a mechanical promotion check (already present? non-obvious? durable?).

```yaml
# assessment artifact
satisfies: [no-sql-injection]
produced_by: {task_id: task-c, skill: security-audit-skill, loop: assessment}
findings: [...]
directive: [...]     # populated when this feeds the next loop as its brief
memory_candidates:   # optional — executor-flagged memory surface candidates
  - type: constraint   # routes to project CLAUDE.md
    content: "SQL injection patterns appear in user-controlled sort params, not just input fields"
  - type: concept      # routes to knowledge graph
    content: "parameterized-query-enforcement"

# implementation artifact
satisfies: [keyword-search, test-coverage-80%]
produced_by: {task_id: task-a, skill: implement-skill, loop: code}
entry: src/search/index.ts

# service descriptor
satisfies: [search-deployed]
produced_by: {task_id: task-d, skill: deploy-skill, loop: code}
url: https://api.example.com/search
health: /health
reversibility: permanent
```

Assessment and implementation are **classes** (organizing principle), not types. The concrete types are file extensions: `.md` (reports, briefs, docs), source extensions (`.ts`, `.py`, `.go` — code), `.yaml`/`.json` (configs, descriptors, state), `.sql` (schemas, migrations), `.pptx` (decks). Path makes the purpose unambiguous: `loops/artifacts/{id}/report.md` is a report; `src/api/search.ts` is implementation. The coverage algebra reads `satisfies` regardless of extension. **`produced_by` — artifact provenance:** every artifact records the task, skill, and loop that produced it. Makes the coverage check auditable (trace any satisfied criterion back to its exact skill execution) and makes the system learnable (Observe can correlate which skills reliably satisfy which criteria, feeding quality_score and rubric refinement). **The architecture is Markov:** each task depends only on its input artifacts (`satisfies` fields), not on the path that produced them. Memorylessness is a feature — it makes coverage checks mechanical and path-independent.

**Loop shape in the selection model:** Each loop invocation runs one skill. Decide writes a task graph — a set of tasks, each declaring which upstream artifacts it depends on. Tasks with no mutual dependencies are independent and can run concurrently. The shape of a loop plan is this task graph: which tasks are parallel, which are sequential. Each task is load-bearing: its artifact must satisfy one or more uncovered acceptance criteria. A task that covers already-satisfied criteria is waste.

**Concrete task graph shapes:**

```
Minimum — one task:
  task A: code  spec: "add search endpoint"
One invocation. One skill selected. One artifact produced.

Parallel — two independent tasks:
  task A: assessment  spec: "audit search endpoint"
  task B: assessment  spec: "review search module architecture"
Both read the same codebase. Neither needs the other's output. Spawned simultaneously.

Sequential — dependent tasks:
  task A: code        spec: "add search endpoint"
  task B: code        spec: "generate tests for search endpoint"
                      depends_on: [task A artifact]
Task B waits for task A's artifact before starting.

Sequential then parallel:
  task A: code
  task B: assessment  depends_on: [A]
  task C: assessment  depends_on: [A]
Tasks B and C both consume A's output but have no dependency on each other.
Spawned simultaneously when A completes.
```

**Loop quality heuristics:**
- **Task count:** a focused loop plan has 1–3 tasks. Six tasks means the sub-project scope is too large — split it.
- **Chain depth:** 2–3 sequential hops is the sweet spot. Beyond 4 hops, accumulated artifact context may be stale by the time downstream tasks run. Consider splitting into a second sub-project.

**Loop repetition.** When a loop invocation fails or produces partial output, the task repeats with failure context injected as additional input to the same skill. N repetitions is declared by Decide in the plan at spawn time. **When retries are exhausted, the response is not automatic escalation — it's algorithm switching.** If self-eval signals structural non-convergence (same failure mode recurring across retries, not just bad luck), the correct move is to switch skill or loop type before escalating to human. Retry-with-context assumes the same approach will work with more information; algorithm switching recognizes the approach itself is in a bad regime. Escalation is the last resort, not the default when N runs out.

**Loop implementation conventions:**
- **Push-based alerts, not raw stream polling** — loops bake domain knowledge into programmatic alert conditions rather than streaming raw output. When something goes wrong (validation loss increasing, test failure rate above threshold), the loop fires an alert to a named channel. The sub-agent running the loop reads alerts, not raw logs. This keeps token cost low and decision logic clean. Raw stream output is the anti-pattern; programmatic alerts are the interface.
- **Monitoring conditions as executable knowledge** — a loop's alert conditions are the executable form of domain knowledge about what bad outcomes look like. This is distinct from CLAUDE.md (declarative, architectural) — monitoring conditions are baked into loop/skill implementations at design time, evaluated at execution time. When Orient formalizes a constraint ("training should converge"), the loop designer translates that into a monitoring condition. Orient writes to CLAUDE.md; loop designers write monitoring conditions.


**Completion Criteria:** The three core loops run autonomously from a spec, produce clean artifacts, and self-evaluate without human input. The loop manifest is defined well enough that Decide can select and sequence loops reliably. The library is capable enough to clone at C-tier; A-tier and S-tier define the growth horizon (see Capability Targets).

---

## Dream-Loop Detail

> **Architectural note:** Dream-loop is an Observe protocol — specifically, the extended voice session protocol (dream-protocol) in which the human generates or updates the dream state. It is not a Loop Library class. The Loop Library contains two classes: eval loop and code loop. The dream state artifact lives in Observe's output; eval loop and code loop navigate toward it.

**Two-stage consolidation.** Active execution tags; the dream-loop consolidates. During loop execution, the right behavior is to capture observations, improvements, and decisions as lightweight artifacts (inbox, decision records, improvement logs) — not to integrate them in real time. Integration is expensive and wrong during Act. The dream-loop is the consolidation phase: it replays what was tagged during execution and integrates it into the DREAM-STATE. Attempting consolidation inside a loop's completion phase is the anti-pattern — pay tagging cost during execution, integration cost during dreaming, not both in Act.

**Dream State as convergence mechanism.** The DREAM-STATE is the human-readable intermediate representation that allows human and model to maintain shared understanding of a system too large for either to hold in context simultaneously. Without it, human and model diverge over time as the codebase grows — each operating from an increasingly different mental model of what exists and what's needed. With it, they converge: the DREAM-STATE is the shared surface both parties can read, update, and reason from across context boundaries. This is its architectural function — not documentation, not status log, but convergence substrate. Consequence: the DREAM-STATE must remain legible to both human and model at all times; if it drifts toward an artifact dump or status log, it loses its convergence property.

**Dream State as digital twin.** In IoT architecture, a digital twin is a virtual representation of a physical system — continuously synchronized with real-world state by sensor events, used for simulation and planning, expected to drift if not updated. The Dream State is the Orchestrator's digital twin of the project: a virtual representation of what exists and what's needed, synchronized by loop artifact events (each completed loop = a sensor reading), used by Decide as its planning substrate. Three operational properties follow: (1) drift is expected and named — the twin diverges from reality when loops aren't run; (2) synchronization is event-driven — loop artifacts are the sync events, not manual edits; (3) the twin is for planning, not display — its primary consumer is Decide, not the human.

The dream-protocol (the Observe session that produces the dream state) handles:

- **Mission** — the deeper purpose the system serves; why it exists
- **Constitution** — the system's emergent axioms; the operating principles that surface through use, not prescribed upfront
- **Alignment** — connecting the work to the mission at every level
- **JTBD** — jobs-to-be-done framework, job maps, desired outcome statements, MECE needs statements; understanding what needs are unmet and what outcomes customers measure success by
- **Uphill / downhill / roadmap** — modes of operation that Observe (via dream-protocol) defines and manages, not slash commands
- **Moonshots** — goals big enough to orient the whole system; the Capability Targets tier list is the moonshots made concrete
- **Make it lovable / make it a game** — the experience vision that code loop implements

**Constraint suspension — the opening move of any vision session:** before asking "what can we build?", fully answer "what does the architecture need to be?" Suspend current technical limits, resource constraints, and implementation feasibility for this phase — they contaminate the vision if admitted too early. Two distinct questions, kept in strict sequence: (1) what does the mature state require? (2) how do we get there? Mixing them produces neither a clear vision nor a sound implementation plan.

---

## Code Loop Detail

Code loop handles:

- **Spec-driven implementation** — functional/non-functional requirements, API design, database design, system algorithms, high-level design
- **Codex review** — a dedicated phase for code review, not a separate loop. Fleet pattern: multiple specialized agents run in parallel, each checking a different issue class (security, logic errors, regressions, edge cases). Two-pass structure: generate candidates → verify each candidate against actual code behavior (filters false positives) → deduplicate → rank by severity → report. The verify step is active disconfirmation — each candidate is subjected to an attempt to prove it wrong, not passive confirmation. Survivors are high-confidence findings. For security findings specifically, **reachability-based analysis** is the quality bar: not "does this vulnerability exist in the dependency graph?" but "is it reachable from an actual call path?" Presence-based security scanning generates noise; reachability-based scanning generates signal. The deterministic program analysis layer (data flow, call graphs) provides the evidence; the AI layer reasons about severity and remediation. The codex review fleet can be augmented by a **decoupled verification layer** integrated via MCP or hooks — a standing security scan that operates independently of the producing agent and checks every artifact regardless of which loop produced it. Same architectural principle as the parent/child eval separation in Observe: the evaluator that didn't produce the artifact is the honest evaluator. Severity tiers: blocking (must fix before merge), nit (minor, worth fixing), pre-existing (bug not introduced by this change but discovered during review — surfaces as Observe-level signal for next cycle). Steerable via a review-specific guidance file (always check X / style conventions / skip Y) injected by Decide into the review agents' context.
- **Browser automation (TEST phase)** — Playwright CLI as a first-class TEST capability. Multi-angle parallel testing: spawn N agents, assign each a distinct angle (happy path / edge cases / validation), synthesize results. More efficient than screenshot-based tools — saves accessibility tree to disk, injects only a summary into context (same principle as snapshot synthesis: large state → disk + summary). Skill-package the workflow once it's proven: the parallel test configuration becomes a reusable skill, not a re-described workflow each time. **Visual validation loop (self-eval for visual artifacts):** when the artifact is visual (rendered UI, slides, document), programmatic construction alone is insufficient — the agent cannot verify visual correctness without seeing the output. Pattern: build → screenshot → analyze → fix → re-audit. The loop doesn't close until the visual output matches intent. This is the self-eval mechanism for visual artifacts; the Playwright accessibility tree handles functional correctness, screenshots handle visual correctness. Two distinct validation tracks for the same artifact.
- **Deep code** — quality standard; no surface-level implementations
- **Named patches** — discrete named changesets as implementation convention
- **Developer experience (DX)** — a deliberate pass for how it feels to work on an engineering project with this system
- **Reference architecture — Caching** — Decision framework for applying caching types and strategies across the harness. The right type and strategy depend on access pattern, consistency requirement, and blast radius if the cache fails.

  **Cache types:**

  | Type | Use when | Harness locations |
  |------|----------|-------------------|
  | **In-memory** | Accessed frequently within a single cycle; latency-critical; reconstructable if lost | Act execution state (before WAL flush); Observe snapshot during construction; skill selection rankings during Decide; quality_score lookups |
  | **Distributed** | Multiple concurrent agents need the same data; data outlives a single process; cross-agent consistency matters | Shared state.json across concurrent sub-agents; knowledge graph; run archives |
  | **Client-side** | Round-trip latency is the bottleneck; data is session-specific; freshness can be relaxed | LLM prompt cache (token prefix reuse across turns); skill manifests cached in agent context; fetched API docs within a session |

  **Cache strategies:**

  | Strategy | Use when | Harness locations |
  |----------|----------|-------------------|
  | **Cache-aside** | Read-heavy; cache failure acceptable (source is the fallback); data model can differ from source | Skill selection index (miss → semantic search fallback); artifact loading into context (miss → disk read); Observe snapshot construction |
  | **Write-through** | Strong consistency required; reads should always hit cache | state.json WAL (write to memory + disk simultaneously); quality_score persisted after each run |
  | **Write-behind** | Write-heavy; brief inconsistency tolerable; low write latency needed | High-frequency Act coordination state during parallel execution (fast acknowledge, batch flush at phase boundaries); quality_score accumulation within a cycle (flush via `complete_run_tracking` at cycle end) |
  | **Read-through** | Read-heavy; hydration logic belongs in the cache layer; source and cache models match | Observe surface-state (disk artifacts auto-loaded into snapshot); skill manifest loading from skills-library-mcp |

  **Cache invalidation strategies:**
  - **TTL-based** — items expire after a fixed duration regardless of source changes. Use for: context window content (compaction window is the natural TTL), session-scoped API docs, prompt cache prefixes.
  - **Event-driven** — invalidate on a specific trigger. Use for: skill quality_score when a skill is updated (update event invalidates the cached score); downstream artifact cache when an upstream dependency changes.
  - **Explicit** — invalidated by direct instruction, not time or event. Use for: knowledge graph entries superseded by a new architectural decision; CLAUDE.md cached content after a bilateral rewrite.

  **Distributed cache and CAP theorem:** when multiple concurrent sub-agents share state (distributed cache), the CAP theorem applies — you cannot simultaneously guarantee consistency, availability, and partition tolerance. The harness makes an implicit choice: state.json as the WAL favors consistency (all agents read the same ground truth) over availability (a write lock blocks readers). At concurrent sub-agent scale, this becomes explicit: the LLM resource scheduling design must name which tradeoff it accepts. Partition tolerance is assumed given network-based agent coordination.

  **Metrics to instrument:**
  - **Cache hit rate** — primary health signal; low rate indicates undersizing or poor key design
  - **Cache eviction rate** — high rate indicates cache pressure; items evicted before they are accessed
  - **Data consistency** — staleness between cache and source; most critical in distributed cache under concurrent sub-agent load
  - **Cache expiration time (TTL)** — how long items remain valid; quality_score recency is governed by `last_used`; the context window has a natural TTL bounded by the compaction window
- **Skills as portable architecture hydrated by LLMs** — a skill is not a static script; it is a portable procedure that an LLM executes by reading its definition and applying it to context. The skill carries the SOP; the LLM supplies the reasoning. This makes skills architecture in the same sense that an API spec is architecture: the definition is the contract, and any capable model can fulfill it. As models improve, the same skill definition produces better output without modification — the harness improves automatically as the underlying model improves. Portability is the design goal: a skill written for one project should be runnable in any other project with the same loop.
- **Harness-level correctness guardrails** — automatic rejection of invalid edits before they propagate. A single harness-level correctness check (e.g. linting an edit before it lands) measurably improves performance; without it, invalid edits compound through subsequent steps. Applied to code loop: the hook system gates edits through linting/validation automatically. The principle: enforce correctness at the smallest possible unit (individual edits), not just at completion. Errors caught at the edit level are cheaper than errors caught at the test level. **Linter-as-teacher pattern:** hook output should be remediation instructions, not error codes. Not `"Error: line 14"` but `"Error: line 14 uses deprecated API. Replace foo() with bar()."` The hook output lands directly in the agent's context — make it actionable rather than diagnostic. This turns every lint failure into a teaching moment and eliminates the round-trip of the agent reasoning about what the error means.
- **Spec-driven development triangle** — spec, tests, and code form a feedback triangle, not a one-way pipeline. The spec drives tests; tests constrain code; code reveals constraints that revise the spec. "Implementing code improves the spec" — the equation runs both directions. Keeping all three vertices in sync is the central engineering challenge. The code loop's phases aren't a waterfall; they iterate across the triangle until spec, tests, and code are coherent with each other.

---

## Artifact Convention

Every loop produces **one artifact**: a single markdown file readable by a senior engineer in under 10 seconds.

**YAML frontmatter as standard metadata layer:** every loop artifact opens with YAML frontmatter — loop name, execution ID, produced timestamp, artifact type, input artifact references (prior loops this one consumed). This is not documentation overhead; it is Observe's fast-read layer. Observe reads frontmatter before content: path-based triage first (what loop produced this), frontmatter second (when, what it consumed, what type), full content only if needed. Standard fields: `loop`, `execution_id`, `produced_at`, `artifact_type`, `input_artifacts`, `satisfies`. Consistent frontmatter across all artifacts makes Observe's `surface-state` parsing mechanical rather than content-dependent.

`satisfies` is a list of criterion proofs — each entry names the exact criterion from the sub-project spec and points to the evidence within the artifact:
```yaml
satisfies:
  - criterion: "JWT validation handles token expiry"
    evidence: "src/auth/jwt.ts lines 45-67 — expiry check, refresh flow, rejection path"
  - criterion: "unit test coverage > 80%"
    evidence: "coverage report: 84.2% — attached as reference/coverage.html"
```
This field is what makes Act's coverage check mechanical: parse frontmatter across all board artifacts, union the criterion strings, diff against the sub-project spec's acceptance criteria list. No content reading required for the completion check.

**File path as namespace:** paths are semantic statements, not storage locations. `loops/code-loop/artifacts/{id}/` tells you what the file is, what produced it, and which execution — before you open it. Observe can do path-based triage before content-based reading. Design convention: every path should be an ontological statement about what lives there. Corollary: files that can't be path-addressed meaningfully probably don't belong in the artifact structure.

**Path resolution and the dentry cache:** the OS resolves paths left-to-right, one component at a time, caching each component in the dentry cache (a hash table keyed on parent + name). Repeated traversal of the same path prefix is essentially free after the first read — the cache warms on first access. Practical implication for Observe: artifacts organized under shared prefixes (`loops/code-loop/artifacts/`) get OS-level prefix caching for free. Consistent path structures are not just semantic choices; they have concrete performance consequences baked into the OS.

**Provenance as a unified concern:** the ability to trace any claim back to its source — at multiple granularities. `produced_by` traces at the artifact level (who made this). `satisfies` with evidence traces at the criterion level (what proves this). Decision tracing traces at the decision level (why this choice). Assumption enumeration traces at the assumption level (what's unfalsified). Within an artifact, claims should be distinguishable by source: derived from spec, derived from existing code/artifacts, inferred by the agent, or generated without source. Claims without provenance are the hallucination candidates and the first place to look when something goes wrong.

The artifact is the receipt, not the work. It serves three audiences:

- **Human**: narrative, scannable, high-level
- **Orchestrator (Observe)**: structured enough to be parsed into the next snapshot
- **AI agent (next cycle)**: decisions with rationale, constraints with context, enough to orient cold

Each loop's artifact is composed of reference files (modeled on how SKILL.md uses references/). The specific reference file structure is defined per-loop as part of the essence work on each loop.

**Compound learning / kernel model:** The system is not truly complete until others can use it and experience compounding improvements in their own context. This requires a two-layer architecture:

- **Kernel** — seeded on install; the base Loop Library (proven, context-independent loops and skills) + foundational CLAUDE.md axioms (the system's base constitution). Represents the community's shared, distilled learning. Should remain minimal — only what's proven durable belongs here.
- **Accrual** — everything that compounds on top; personal CLAUDE.md edits, Loop Library customizations, capability primitives added through More/Better/New. Represents individual compound learning specific to a person, project, or context.

The Loop Library is the kernel's executable learning. CLAUDE.md is its declarative memory. On install both are seeded from the kernel; everything after is accrual. This partially exists today (`~/.claude/CLAUDE.md` vs project CLAUDE.md, skills-library-mcp as shared registry) — the work is making it explicit and intentional. The system is truly done when the kernel is stable enough to seed others' compound learning and individual accruals are structured enough to flow back.

**Compounding through escalation** — each generation of the system should push the current capability layer until it breaks, and the break becomes the next layer's starting point. This is not smooth incremental improvement; it is adversarial pressure as generative mechanism. When sub-agents systematically fail a class of problem, those failure patterns are not just defects to patch — they are signals about what the next capability layer needs to look like. Clustered failure modes are design inputs. The More/Better/New investment model describes the accrual loop; compounding through escalation describes the horizon-finding mechanism: you don't know what New needs to look like until you've broken Better.

**CLAUDE.md reconciliation:** Currently two CLAUDE.md files are in use (`~/.claude/CLAUDE.md` for global user preferences, `project/CLAUDE.md` for project instructions) — neither maps cleanly to kernel vs. accrual. Both contain a mix of kernel material (constitutional axioms, core principles) and personal accrual (individual preferences, project-specific learnings). Part of the compound learning work is restructuring these into an intentional kernel layer (distributable, minimal, proven) and accrual layer (personal, evolving, project-specific) so the two-layer model is explicit rather than accidental.

---

## Memory Model

No separate Memory module.

**Three-tier note taxonomy (Zettelkasten framing):**

| Tier | Orchestrator equivalent | Characteristics | Promoted by |
|------|------------------------|-----------------|-------------|
| **Fleeting** | Inbox items + raw loop artifacts | Temporary, high-volume, unprocessed | Observe (reads them) |
| **Literature** | Orient's path statement, cycle synthesis | Distilled, reviewed, not yet formalized | Orient (processes them) |
| **Permanent** | Seven memory surfaces (see above) | Evergreen, routed by type, versioned | reflect-protocol (loop boundary); bilateral consent for ecosystem CLAUDE.md |

**Promotion criteria (literature → permanent):** three signals, all required: recurrence (appeared in 2+ artifacts independently), non-obviousness (genuine constraint not immediately inferrable), durability (architectural decisions yes, tactical choices no). User explicit confirmation overrides all three.

**The cycle:** loop discovers constraint → records in artifact with `memory_candidates` (fleeting) → reflect-protocol runs at loop boundary → CLAUDE.md updated (permanent) → cycle-level Observe reads consolidated state → Orient synthesizes into path statement (literature) → all future runs see it.

**Atomic note principle:** each skill is a permanent note under continuous revision. Self-contained (understandable without surrounding context), titled as a complete action, continuously revisable. The `Better` in More/Better/New is the revision mechanism. Skills don't file into hierarchies — they exist as clusters in the knowledge graph. Each skill is a named cluster of concept nodes; its tag declarations define the concepts it covers and the intra-cluster edges between them. Inter-cluster edges emerge from concept co-occurrence across skills over time. Tags are lenses, not folders. Structure emerges from the graph.

**Three learning channels.** The system has three distinct inputs to its learning, each feeding both CLAUDE.md and the knowledge graph through a named Observe protocol:

| Channel | Protocol | What it captures |
|---------|----------|-----------------|
| **Doing** | reflect-protocol (auto, loop boundary) | Constraints and patterns from execution; concept nodes and primitive edges for all four types produced by the loop |
| **Reading** | ingestion-protocol | Concepts and insights from external resources; isolated concept nodes; gaps across all four primitive types |
| **Dreaming** | dream-protocol | Human intent, direction, constraints; concept nodes surfacing vision-driven gaps across all four primitive types |

All three channels converge on the six memory surfaces via routing types. The second brain's four stages map directly: Capture (all three protocols) → Organize (knowledge graph clusters concepts; memory surfaces store by type) → Distill (reflect-protocol's formalization check routes to the right surface) → Express (capability primitives built to cover isolated concept nodes — skills to execute, evals to measure, protocols to surface context, frameworks to structure reasoning).

**The digital garden.** Concepts don't jump from raw observation to formalized primitive — they grow. The knowledge graph is the garden: isolated concept nodes are seeds, primitives are mature plants, and the edges between them are roots taking hold. A concept planted during one Observe session may need cultivation across several before it reveals which primitive type it wants to become — protocol, framework, skill, or eval. You don't know at planting time. The deferred log is a seed bank: items planted with "revisit when" conditions, dormant until new context brings them back to life. Harvesting sessions are gardening: some seeds take root (encodings), most don't (zeros), and the hit rate drops as the garden matures. The practice is simple — plant with at least one connection (avoid orphan nodes), cultivate by adding edges and evidence, and let topology-protocol surface what's ready to formalize. Growth is incremental skill stacking — building a ladder of doable tasks until you reach the impossible. Each concept cultivated makes the next one more accessible; each primitive formalized extends the platform from which the next concept can be reached.

**The knowledge graph is the bridge between knowing and doing.** CLAUDE.md holds what the system knows declaratively. The four capability primitives — protocols, frameworks, skills, and evals — are what the system can do: observe systematically, reason structurally, execute, and measure. Four is the structural ceiling, not an arbitrary count: one primitive per OODA phase (protocols → Observe, frameworks → Orient, skills → Act/execution, evals → Act/measurement). A fifth primitive would require either a fifth phase or crowding a phase with two primitive types — neither is acceptable. The ceiling is architectural.

The knowledge graph is a semantic network (Quillian, 1960s-70s): nodes are concepts, edges are typed relationships (IS-A, DEPENDS-ON, COVERS, RELATED), and retrieval works through spreading activation — following edges outward from a starting node, where activation diminishes with distance. Topology-protocol IS spreading activation applied to capability analysis: start from a concept node, follow edges, surface what's connected and what's isolated. All four primitives live in the knowledge graph as nodes alongside concept nodes; edges connect concepts to the primitives that cover them. When ingestion-protocol or dream-protocol adds a concept to CLAUDE.md, that concept may have no owning primitive yet — it appears as an isolated node in the graph. When a loop produces a skill, protocol, framework, or eval covering that concept, reflect-protocol upserts the edge and the isolated node becomes owned. Isolated concept nodes are the frontier across all four primitive types. Topology-protocol surfaces that frontier. `New` in More/Better/New is not intuited — it is read from the graph.

**Knowledge graph vs. versioning system.** Two structures, two questions: the versioning system answers "how did skill X evolve?"; the knowledge graph answers "what can the system do right now, and what's missing?" Skills, protocols, frameworks, and evals all version over time — but the knowledge graph does not track history. The graph is a live view of the current capability surface; when a skill is versioned and its concept coverage changes, reflect-protocol updates the cluster. The two structures are complementary: history belongs to versioning; capability belongs to the graph.

**Memory is the agent's cognitive runtime** — not storage it queries but the environment it operates within. The six memory surfaces define the cognitive habitat the same way the four isolation layers define the physical habitat. **Memory architecture determines agent capabilities more than model selection.** The same model on the same task with richer accumulated context consistently outperforms a better model starting cold. Every artifact captured compounds — the next Observe cycle is more informed than the last regardless of model version. This is the memory flywheel: loop artifacts → Observe reads → Orient synthesizes → CLAUDE.md updated → all future runs benefit. The compounding advantage widens over time; the gap between a system with rich accumulated memory and one that starts from zero every session is not a model gap — it is a memory infrastructure gap. Corollary: **if domain knowledge doesn't exist in the codebase, it doesn't exist for the agents.** Expertise not encoded in an artifact is invisible — the agent operates as if that knowledge were never developed.

**Trajectory engineering: session context as a tree.** Trunk = stable, reusable context (CLAUDE.md, artifact references, loop state). Branches = exploratory session context (bug investigations, failed approaches, dead ends). The trajectory engineer trims branches back to the trunk rather than letting context accumulate linearly until compaction forces a reset. Pattern: after resolving a bug or dead-end exploration, rewind to before the branch, summarize the outcome in one line, proceed from the clean trunk. Context that is no longer relevant is not neutral — it actively degrades trajectory quality. Complement to the Zettelkasten promotion model: promotion decides what to *keep* (fleeting → permanent); trajectory engineering decides what to *discard* (branches → trunk). Both are necessary. Underlying principle: **context constrains the distribution of possible trajectories.** Context doesn't just influence output — it sets the probability distribution over all outputs the model could produce. Clean, dense trunk context narrows the distribution toward better trajectories. Noisy, branch-laden context widens it toward worse ones. Watts framing: the LLM lives in the eternal now — every generation happens from scratch, with no past, only the present reconstruction called context. Dead context is memory holding the agent to death; it anchors the agent to a state that no longer exists. Trajectory engineering is the practice of living fully in the LLM's actual condition rather than fighting it by accumulating dead branches. Phase space framing: in dynamical systems, initial conditions determine which limit cycle orbit you enter — there are infinitely many orbits, and your starting state selects one. The context window IS the initial condition. Context engineering is initial condition engineering: clean trunk context puts the system on a high-quality orbit; noisy, degraded context puts it on a lower one. You don't escape to a better orbit mid-run — you select it at the start.

**Design for the agent web, not the human web.** Knowledge systems designed for human consumption (browsable, hierarchical, visual — Notion, Obsidian, Apple Notes) are structurally mismatched for agent consumption. Agent-readable infrastructure requires semantic queryability (meaning, not folder structure), typed programmatic interfaces, and machine-parseable metadata. The Orchestrator's artifact convention already implements this: YAML frontmatter as fast-read layer, file paths as ontological statements, structured schema that Observe can parse without content-dependent reading. Every artifact and memory file should be designed for the agent web first — human readability is a secondary property, not the design target. **Tool interface hierarchy: code > CLI > MCP**, ordered by token efficiency and determinism. Agents writing code against typed TypeScript SDKs outperform agents making individual tool calls which outperform agents running shell commands. Code is composable (chain and filter operations in one execution before returning to context), typed (errors caught before execution, approval rules expressible in the type system), and isolatable (V8 isolates, sandboxed runtimes — many agents on one kernel without Docker). Cloudflare's code mode demonstrated 40% token reduction and improved accuracy by replacing tool calls with code execution against SDKs. Bash is the universal substrate but not the target execution layer — it has no type safety, no permission model, and no standards for destructive vs. non-destructive commands. The direction: expose harness capabilities as typed TypeScript modules that agents write code against, with bash as fallback.

- **Single-threaded by default** — execution is sequential unless parallelism is earned. Look for opportunities to multi-thread where throughput increases for free or with low coordination complexity. "For free" means: tasks so obviously independent that no coordination is needed — different domains, different files, zero semantic coupling. Sequential execution gives semantic consistency for free; parallelism must earn its coordination cost. The isolation layers (worktree, sandbox, tool scoping) are the safety equipment for when parallelism is justified, not the default operating mode.
- **Design for evergreenness** — the four capability primitives (protocols, frameworks, skills, evals) don't need this principle; they handle paradigm shifts through competitive selection, versioning, and pruning. OODA is already evergreen. This principle targets the structural layer between them: the architecture in each phase that the primitives run on — snapshot schema, coordination model, isolation layers, artifact convention, coverage algebra, manifest fields, memory routing. These don't get pruned by selection; if designed poorly, they become embedded constraints. When making structural decisions, encode coordination knowledge (model-invariant: who does what, in what order, what persists, what's isolated) not capability knowledge (model-dependent: how to do the task). The test: "will this structural decision still be correct when models are 10x better?" If not, the decision belongs in the meritocratic layer where it can evolve, not in the structural layer where it's fixed. Evergreenness is a spectrum — OODA phases are maximally evergreen; Claude Code integration points are acceptable risk (substrate coupling, acknowledged and isolated); specific APIs and tools are least evergreen and should be contained behind interfaces.

---

## Eval Model

No separate Eval module. Evals are a fourth capability primitive — the measurement layer of the library — alongside protocols (reasoning patterns), frameworks (reference structures), and skills (execution procedures). They follow identical mechanics: created through More/Better/New, selected competitively, versioned, and pruned when they stop providing signal. A new eval enters the library as a candidate and earns its place through use.

**What evals are:** each eval is a hybrid of reward function and acceptance criteria. It produces a graded score (reward signal that feeds quality_score) and a pass/fail threshold (acceptance gate for the loop). These two functions are unified in a single primitive rather than split between the skill's rubric and Observe's endorsement.

**Rubric vs. eval — two timescales, not competing:**
- **Rubric** (in skill manifest) — execution-time self-check; fires during the run; the inner feedback loop. The skill checks its own output against criteria it declares.
- **Eval** (in eval library) — post-execution outcome measurement; fires after the artifact exists; the outer feedback loop. Selected by Act from the library, independent of the skill that produced the artifact.

The rubric guides execution; the eval validates outcome. Both are necessary.

**Act's selection behavior:** Act selects the eval after seeing which loop/skill combination the plan specifies — not before, not at planning time. Selection is informed by the specific execution context, not just the capability class. When no eval exists for a combination, Act synthesizes a provisional eval for that run; the provisional eval enters the library as a low-confidence candidate and competes from there through the normal meritocratic process.

**Pruning signals:** an eval that every skill trivially passes is no longer providing signal — pruning candidate. An eval that no skill ever passes is miscalibrated — pruning candidate.
