The eval library self-organizes: as capabilities improve, weak evals are outcompeted and harder evals emerge as aspirational targets. Increasing rigor over time is a natural output of the competitive dynamics, not a deliberate escalation policy.

**Eval coverage gap:** an isolated concept node in the knowledge graph with no owning eval is a measurement gap — the system knows about the concept and may have a skill for it, but cannot measure whether it's being done well. Observe's `measure-improvement` step surfaces eval gaps alongside skill gaps — isolated concept nodes with no owning eval are flagged by the same topology scan that flags nodes with no owning skill. The frontier has three dimensions: what the system knows (CLAUDE.md), what it can do (skills), and what it can measure (evals).

---

## Completion Algebra

```
Orchestrator.done = Observe.done AND Orient.done AND Decide.done AND Act.done AND LoopLibrary.done

LoopLibrary.done = all 12 loop types (Document, Dataset, Roadmap, Architecture, Code, Schema,
                    Visual, Skill, Framework, Protocol, Eval, Model) run autonomously from a spec,
                    produce clean artifacts, and self-evaluate without human input

Current: 0/5 modules complete (0%)
```

---

## Design Principles

- **Output modality matches information type** — the Orchestrator currently communicates everything as text, which means it describes spatial and relational information verbally when showing it would be more precise. The right modality per information type:

  | Information type | Modality | Tool |
  |-----------------|----------|------|
  | Instructions, reasoning, decisions | Text | Claude output |
  | Spatial / relational state (topology, dependencies, progress, gaps) | Diagram | ASCII art or Mermaid |
  | Mobile / eyes-free contexts | Audio | TTS via voice surface |
  | Milestone summaries, presentations | Generative video | deck-loop |

  **ASCII art and Mermaid are the lightweight visual layer for real-time system state** — Claude generates both natively, no external tools, lives in the terminal. Specific use cases: dream state map (project as landscape — complete modules = solid terrain, pending = outline, blocked = gap); task board / dependency graph (spatial layout communicates dependencies that a list cannot); knowledge graph snapshot (low-connectivity gaps are spatial — showing them is more precise than describing them); topic stack during voice session (the transcript builds a visible map of what was covered as topics resolve); **hill chart** (each sub-problem as a dot on an uphill→downhill curve — left slope is high residual / still figuring it out, the hump is where Orient produces a sufficient path statement, right slope is execution. Powered by the method-of-exhaustion convergence metric and policy entropy. During voice sessions, the hill chart is a conversational navigation aid — you see a sub-problem stuck on the left slope and naturally steer toward it, see one cresting the hump and feel the momentum. The visualization guides the conversation without explicit direction). Mermaid is preferred over raw ASCII for complex graphs (more readable, renders in markdown). ASCII art is preferred for abstract or atmospheric visualization where structure matters less than impression. Generative video earns its cost at major cycle boundaries, not inside individual loops — deck-loop already covers this. The principle: never describe spatial information with text when a diagram would show it more precisely.

- **Microscope principle** — build a tool that lets you see structure in something too complex to visualize directly. When a space is too high-dimensional for direct visualization, build an instrument that reveals its structure indirectly. The system operates in spaces that exceed human (and model) intuition: the capability landscape across all skills/loops/evals, the knowledge graph's topology, gap structure across dream state dimensions, the loss landscape of selection optimization. None of these can be "seen" by inspecting individual nodes — the structure is in the relationships across all dimensions simultaneously. The instrument collapses the high-dimensional space into a representation that preserves structural features (peaks, valleys, clusters, gaps) while fitting in a perceivable frame. One concrete technique: collapse N-dimensional tuples into labels mapped to angular positions on a circle, with the measured value as the radius — the entire landscape in a single 2D plot. The general pattern: don't slice or project (which loses structure in the discarded dimensions); instead, re-encode the full space into a representation the observer can parse. Observe's job is making the invisible visible; this principle says *build the instruments* to do so, don't just describe what you can already see. **Cockpit vocabulary:** in the pilot-navigator metaphor, the pilot reads **instruments** (gauges, displays — show system state) and uses **controls** (knobs, faders, switches — change system state). The dashboard, hill charts, knowledge graph snapshots, and dream state maps are instruments. Loop commands, gate approvals, and skill parameters are controls. The harness is the cockpit — the integrated surface where the pilot reads instruments and operates controls to fly the ship. Every new observability surface is an instrument added to the cockpit; every new command is a control.
- **Worldbuilder, not project manager** — "the best way to predict the future is to invent it" (Alan Kay). The org-level harness doesn't complete projects and terminate. It builds and maintains a world continuously. Each org IS a world: superorganism (personal capability world), servicegrid (logistics world), pilgrimage (game world), dreamtime (exploration world). The harness doesn't finish — it tends a world.
- **Symbiont, not tool** — the Orchestrator is a symbiont: a machine that augments itself through its relationship with the human. The opposite of a cyborg (human augmented by machine), a symbiont improves by absorbing human judgment, context, and intent at every Observe boundary. The human isn't using the Orchestrator — the Orchestrator is growing through the human. This is why Observe is the only human-facing surface: it is the symbiotic interface, the point where the biological feeds the machine. Every voice session, every context input, every gate decision is the system augmenting itself. The relationship is the product.
- **Sovereign all the way down** — the end state is custom-built at every layer of the stack. Sync engines, graph databases, knowledge retrieval, encryption, hosting — every dependency is a sovereignty gap. The harness + shadow clones + compounding skills make this feasible for a single person: what once required a team of engineers for months becomes a realistic solo project when the system can build its own infrastructure. Use existing frameworks as reference implementations to learn from, then replace them. The sequencing heuristic is "primitives over integrations" (reach for what exists before building); the end-state heuristic is "sovereign over convenient" (eventually replace every external dependency with something you own and understand). The two heuristics are complementary, not contradictory — one governs when, the other governs where you're headed.
- **Lean by default / tiny as a metric** — earn every module, every phase, every skill. Kernel size is a metric to maintain, not just a preference. Hotz's rule: if you can't read and understand all of it in one sitting, it's too complex. Every kernel addition is a tax on everyone's understanding. Resist comprehensiveness as a design instinct, not just as a policy. **Abstraction relocates complexity, it doesn't reduce it** (Fleury) — the only real reduction comes from simplifying the underlying problem itself. "Resist adding things" is too easily overcome by justifying the addition; "your abstraction didn't eliminate the complexity, it just moved it" challenges the justification itself. Hidden complexity manifests as performance problems, subtle bugs, and lack of composability. When you're tempted to add an abstraction layer, ask: am I simplifying the problem, or am I hiding it? Low-level clarity: mapping code directly to the hardware and memory constraints (reference: Ryan Fleury, Digital Grove).
- **Dimension management** — the architecture's complexity grows along two metrics: concept count (linear, manageable) and interaction surface (combinatorial, not). When adding a concept, the cost isn't the concept — it's the interactions it creates with everything else. Two mitigations: (1) *Orthogonality enforcement* — dimensions should be independent (MECE). If two dimensions are correlated (model tier is usually determined by task type), collapse them into one with an override. A correlated dimension that's treated as independent inflates the design space without adding real choice. Periodically audit: which dimensions does Decide actually vary independently? Correlated pairs are candidates for collapse. (2) *Decision cascades* — not flat selection across all dimensions simultaneously, but hierarchical: first select loop type → that narrows valid skills → that narrows valid model tiers → that narrows valid task types. Each decision constrains the remaining space. A cascade that selects from 12 loops, then 8 valid skills per loop, then 2 valid model tiers per skill = 192 paths. Flat selection across those same dimensions = 12 × 100 × 3 × 4 = 14,400. The cascade is how Decide stays tractable as the library grows — the selection space is a tree, not a grid. **Failure modes:** *Dimensional creep* — dimensions added faster than they're collapsed; interaction surface grows unboundedly even while each individual addition seems justified. The audit ("which dimensions does Decide actually vary independently?") is the corrective. *False orthogonality* — dimensions that appear independent but are actually coupled; Decide makes independent selections on each, producing contradictions (e.g., selecting a skill that requires Opus paired with a Haiku model tier). The corrective is encoding the coupling as a constraint in the cascade — downstream selections are filtered by upstream choices. *Cascade rigidity* — the cascade ordering ossifies as the system evolves; "loop → skill → model" was the right hierarchy initially but now skill should drive loop selection. The corrective is treating the cascade ordering itself as a tunable parameter reviewed at dream-state level, not hardcoded in Decide's implementation.
- **Static plan** — Decide outputs a plan, Act executes it faithfully; sub-agents do not re-plan
- **Loop as sub-agent mind** — no recursive OODA; the loop's phases are the sub-agent's cognition
- **One artifact per loop** — the receipt, not the work; clean, minimal, useful to three audiences
- **Skills as portable architecture** — curate the short list of architectures that re-implement regardless of tooling; use LLMs to hydrate them
- **Investment model** — More > Better > New; the system compounds over time. Operational heuristic: when a workflow is proven, standardize it; when standardized, package it as a skill. The skill is the unit of compounding — re-describing a workflow each time is the anti-pattern. "Can we standardize this? Can we make it a skill?" should be the closing question of any successful new workflow. Suzuki: enlightenment is ordinary experience, just two inches off the ground. Each skill improvement, each CLAUDE.md entry, each loop refinement — not revolution, just two inches. Over time the ground itself rises.
- **Positional play** — when no gap is urgent and no goal is obvious, improve your position. Strengthen the capability surface — skill quality, eval coverage, knowledge graph density, CLAUDE.md precision — so the system can take better advantage of the next opportunity when it reveals itself. The system always has a productive move. Idling is never the correct response; the absence of a tactical target is the signal to play positionally. Chess: the strongest players spend most of their moves improving piece placement, not attacking. The attack becomes available *because* the position is strong.
- **Automatic curriculum (Voyager, 2023)** — the system identifies its own skill gaps and proposes work to fill them. Based on completed tasks, failed tasks, current capability state, and environment context, it asks: "what should I learn next?" This is the autonomous version of the leverage protocol — instead of the human deciding the next highest-leverage move, the system itself identifies capability gaps and generates tasks to close them. The curriculum considers: what has succeeded (don't repeat), what has failed (don't attempt yet or retry with new capability), what's adjacent to current capability (stretch but achievable), and what the environment makes available (opportunity-driven). Positional play is the human-initiated version; automatic curriculum is the autonomous version. Dreamtime's primary loop IS an automatic curriculum — it proposes its own exploration targets and pursues them without human input. Any org's harness should run automatic curriculum during idle time.
- **Bottleneck-targeted benchmarks via game design** — benchmark the next capability gap, not what already works. Design evaluations that specifically probe where the system fails — the benchmark IS the bottleneck detector. Game design as benchmarking (ARC-AGI v3 approach) ensures benchmarks stay meaningful: novel challenges that can't be memorized, requiring genuine reasoning. The loop: identify bottleneck → design a game/benchmark targeting that gap → train/improve against it → measure → when solved, design a new game targeting the next bottleneck. A well-designed game IS both an eval and a training environment — it produces training signal while measuring progress. Applies everywhere: skill library (benchmark the next skill gap), loop execution (probe where loops fail), Orient frameworks (test where the system misreads), earned autonomy (challenges at the boundary of current trust), sovereign model training (the agent lab's core workflow). The automatic curriculum identifies what to learn; the benchmark formalizes that gap into a measurable target; the game is the training ground; solving the game is the graduation criterion.
- **Graduated context exposure (warm-up)** — when a new org, project, or domain bootstraps, don't dump the full observation space on the agent. Graduate the context exposure as competence grows. Early loops see simplified snapshots with fewer dimensions; as the system proves it can handle the basics, more observation categories become visible. This is the information-theoretic complement to earned autonomy: earned autonomy expands what the agent can *do*; graduated context exposure expands what the agent can *see*. Voyager implements this literally — observations are gated by task completion count, and even once unlocked, non-essential observations have a random dropout rate to prevent over-reliance. The principle generalizes: a brownfield discovery protocol should start with high-level structure before diving into implementation details; a new human onboarding to the harness should see progressively more of the system's complexity.
- **QA cache for domain bootstrapping** — when entering a new domain, the system asks itself questions about that domain ("What are the main services? How does auth work? What's the deployment pattern?"), answers them from available context (code, docs, files), and caches the Q&A pairs in a retrievable store. On subsequent Orient passes, relevant cached Q&A pairs are retrieved by semantic similarity and injected as context. This is crystallized Orient output — reusable across sessions, cheap to retrieve, and self-generating. The cache bootstraps domain understanding without requiring a human to write everything down. Voyager uses this to build Minecraft domain knowledge; the harness equivalent is auto-generating a domain FAQ during brownfield discovery that becomes permanent project context. The cached Q&A pairs are a form of memory that sits between ephemeral conversation and permanent CLAUDE.md — structured enough to retrieve, disposable enough to regenerate if stale.
- **Project-level knowledge accumulation** — each project/codebase accumulates its own wiki from every session the navigator runs in it. Three-stage pipeline: (1) **Capture** — hooks on session-end and pre-compact extract decisions, lessons, and patterns from the conversation transcript into append-only daily logs (the project's "raw" layer). (2) **Compile** — a scheduled process (daily or on-demand) promotes daily logs into structured wiki articles: atomic concepts, cross-cutting connections, and a master index. The index is the navigator's entry point — not vector search, just a structured catalog the agent reads to know where to look. (3) **Inject** — session-start hook loads the project wiki's index + recent daily log into context, so the navigator arrives pre-oriented. The wiki is the project's institutional memory — what makes the navigator dangerous in this codebase after 50 sessions, not just the current one. Architecturally, this is the layer between ephemeral session context and the org-level knowledge graph: sessions deposit into the project wiki; the project wiki feeds the org-level graph when patterns generalize beyond one codebase. The QA cache (above) is the one-time bootstrap; project-level accumulation is the continuous version. Relationship to existing architecture: the sovereign graph system operates at org-level (Obsidian vault across all projects in an org); project wikis are per-codebase and lighter-weight. Reflect-protocol fires at loop boundaries and feeds the org graph; project accumulation fires at every session boundary and feeds the project wiki. Both are necessary — different granularity, different scope. Reference implementation: claude-memory-compiler (coleam00) — ~600 lines of Python using Claude Code hooks + Agent SDK, Obsidian-compatible markdown with wikilinks, index-guided retrieval, 7-check linting, ~$1-2/day in API costs.
- **Spaced repetition as attention allocation** — the system has finite attention (context window, human review time, compute budget). Spaced repetition optimizes how that attention is distributed across everything the system knows, ensuring nothing important is forgotten while nothing stale is over-prioritized. SRS is not a feature (flashcards for the human) — it's an architectural principle that unifies several existing mechanisms: *Memory hierarchy as SRS tiers* — recent conversation (seconds), session memory (minutes), CLAUDE.md (days), dream state (permanent). Information that keeps being relevant gets promoted to longer-lived tiers; information that stops being accessed decays. *Skill selection* — dormant-but-valuable skills get surfaced at increasing intervals rather than disappearing behind recency bias. Prevents the library from forgetting its own capabilities. *Learning feedback loop* — new learnings are heavily referenced in early sessions, then gradually less, consolidating into deeper encodings (CLAUDE.md → dream state principle) or decaying if irrelevant. *Proactive messaging* — surface insights to the pilot at SRS intervals, not constantly. New patterns get mentioned frequently, then at longer intervals. The pilot's attention is the scarcest resource. *Automatic curriculum* — re-propose skill gaps at SRS intervals: not every cycle (annoying), not once (forgotten), but at increasing intervals until filled or deliberately deprioritized. *Knowledge graph maintenance* — nodes accessed at decreasing intervals are candidates for pruning or archiving. This IS "forgetting as technology" operationalized through SRS intervals. The meta-principle: SRS provides the decay schedule that "forgetting as technology" needs, the re-proposal cadence that automatic curriculum needs, the surfacing frequency that proactive messaging needs, and the selection memory that skill selection needs. One mechanism, multiple existing concepts made operational.
- **State space compression (Miller's chunking, 1956).** Human working memory holds 7±2 chunks; an LLM's context window is the same constraint at a different scale. Every compression mechanism in the harness — compaction, pyramid summaries, 1-skill-per-loop, D-tier atomic units — exists because of this limit. Miller's insight: the solution isn't expanding capacity, it's chunking information into higher-level units so each slot carries more meaning. That's what compaction does — collapses equivalent states into chunks that preserve decision-relevant information. The harness performs state space compression at every layer: compaction compresses conversation state at context boundaries; the knowledge graph compresses the full project/codebase into concept nodes and edges; the skill manifest compresses capability space so selection operates on the compression rather than raw capability. The unifying quality criterion: compression must preserve decision-relevant information. Compression that loses it is the failure mode — black hole nodes and over-compressed CLAUDE.md entries are its named instances. **Compression is topological, not lossy.** The correct mental model for compaction is the quotient map from geometric continual learning: collapse equivalent states (redundant reasoning, repeated context, intermediate steps that led to the same conclusion) while preserving the separability of structurally distinct concerns (decisions, constraints, mission, open questions). This is abstraction as topological compression, not summarization. Summarization is lossy — it approximates. A quotient map is lossless on the dimensions that matter — it collapses only along dimensions that carry no distinguishing information. The practical test: after compaction, can the system still distinguish between any two concerns that required different handling before compaction? If yes, the compression preserved separability. If not, it collapsed a dimension it shouldn't have. This is why compaction failures (context loss, forgotten decisions, repeated mistakes) are structural — they indicate a wrong collapse, not insufficient summary length. **Pyramid summaries as the reversible complement to compaction.** The fundamental problem: how does an agent with a finite context window navigate a knowledge space much larger than that window? Pyramid summaries solve this with multi-resolution reversible compression — the same content at N zoom levels (2 words → 4 → 8 → 16 → full). An agent surveys hundreds of items at the 2-word level, identifies what's interesting, and expands only those. No information is lost; the full detail is always available on demand. This makes progressive disclosure automatic and N-level rather than the fixed two-level pattern (description vs. full content) used in skill manifests and index files. Two complementary compression mechanisms:
  - *Compaction* — permanently compress old context to fit (quotient map, irreversible). Handles the past.
  - *Pyramid summaries* — temporarily compress current context to navigate (multi-resolution, reversible). Handles the present.

  **Rate-Distortion Theory (Shannon, 1959)** formally grounds the tradeoff: for any acceptable distortion level, there is a minimum number of bits required, and vice versa. Each level of the pyramid operates at a different point on the rate-distortion curve — top level has high compression and high distortion, bottom has low compression and low distortion. The quotient map's "preserve decision-relevant information" criterion is the distortion constraint; the context window limit is the rate constraint. Rate-distortion theory proves there is an optimal tradeoff at each level, and that compacting below the entropy of the state is mathematically impossible.

  Where pyramid summaries solve real scaling problems: Observe's snapshot construction (survey all artifacts at low resolution, expand only what's changed), CBR retrieval across many past executions (survey runs at 2-word level, identify candidates, expand for comparison), knowledge graph navigation (survey clusters at low resolution, zoom into relevant areas), the dream state document itself (survey the full architecture, dive into only the relevant section), and run archives over months of operation (navigable regardless of accumulated history). The MapReduce combination makes this concrete for Observe at scale: (1) Map — generate pyramid summaries for all artifacts in parallel; (2) Cluster — group related artifacts by their compressed representations; (3) Reduce — synthesize insights across clusters, expanding detail where needed. Observe constructs the snapshot not by reading everything, but by surveying at low resolution and expanding where signal appears. Reference: StrongDM Software Factory, inspired by multi-resolution image formats (Pyramid TIFF) and map tiles.
- **Everything-is-a-node graph unification (Ross's Uniform Referent Principle, 1950s).** "There must be a single, uniform method of referencing operands regardless of their detailed implementation." The knowledge graph should use one graph primitive with rich typing rather than separate stores for separate entity types. Work items, agents, gates, messages, artifacts, skills, and coordination state are all nodes in the same graph with typed edges between them. One query language, one dependency system, one set of graph operations applies to everything. The same `ready` / `blocked` / `depends-on` semantics that drive task execution also drive gate resolution, artifact dependencies, and inter-agent coordination. Unification means: adding a new entity type is adding a node type and its edge types, not building a new store with a new interface. The graph IS the scheduler, the knowledge base, and the coordination layer — not three separate systems that happen to share data. Reference: Beads (steveyegge/beads) — demonstrates this pattern with 60+ fields on a single Issue type and 18+ typed dependency relationships, where work items, agents, messages, gates, and molecules are all the same primitive.
- **Build-around discipline** — every primitive (protocol, framework, skill, eval) must have an identified build-around: a central mechanic that everything else in that primitive exists to enable. The authorship question is not "what should this primitive do?" but "what is this primitive built around?" The build-around varies by primitive instance, not by type — a skill built around a rubric has different shape than one built around a tool constraint; a protocol built around a decision gate has different shape than one built around a context injection. The test: if you can't name the build-around, the primitive isn't designed yet — it's a collection of parts. *For decision-shaped skills — gate approval, triage, leverage scoring, debugging — the build-around is typically a priority-ordered list of conditional rules, what Connect 4 perfect-play solvers call a* steady state diagram. *The discipline is keeping the priority order short enough to read at a glance; if you can't, the skill is hiding a search tree.* *Another shape:* **game-arc skills** *that traverse three distinct phases over their execution.* **Opening**: *decision-tree branching on conditions to identify the case — the steady state diagram form above.* **Mid-game**: *no clear target, no obvious tactic — improve the situation incrementally, build advantage, gather evidence; this is exactly Positional play.* **Endgame**: *the goal becomes clear, execute the deterministic procedure; this is exactly Static plan. The build-around is recognizing the transition points. The three failure modes are mode-mismatches:* **decision-tree logic in the mid-game** *is premature optimization (reaching for a known case when the situation is still novel);* **positional play in the endgame** *is fiddling instead of shipping (improving what's already done because the goal hasn't been recognized as clear);* **algorithmic execution in the opening** *is rigid procedure for a case that isn't yet clear. The diagnostic question: which phase are we in, and is the current shape the right one for it?* ***Mode-mismatch is the silent failure of game-arc skills.*** *A third shape:* **procedural skills** *that execute a fixed sequence where each step prepares state for the next — format-code, commit-message-write, scaffold-new-project, debug-failing-test. The build-around is the order of operations itself; the failure mode is reordering or omission. Skills are one-shot; iteration happens at the loop level, not inside skills.* ***Procedural is what skills look like when nothing exotic is going on*** *— the steady state diagram and game-arc shapes are the exceptions worth flagging when they apply.*
- **All loops spec-driven or agent-orchestrating** — design constraint for every loop added to the library
- **JTBD cross-cutting** — jobs-to-be-done as a sense-making framework across Observe (dream-protocol), Orient, and eval loop
- **Full OODA cycle every time** — the loop runs completely (Observe → Orient → Decide → Act) each cycle, then repeats. No structural uphill/downhill split. The character of a cycle (uphill or downhill) emerges from loop selection: Decide selecting eval loop = uphill; Decide selecting code loop = downhill. The OODA structure is identical in both cases. The system transitions naturally from uphill to downhill as artifacts accumulate — no mode-switching required. This model is superior to a two-phase split because it is adaptive, resilient, and necessary for novel/S-tier targets. Watts framing: the OODA cycle is a bicycle balance — Observe/Orient is the stepping-back correction (releasing attachment to the current plan); Decide/Act is full engagement (attachment to the trajectory). You can't stay permanently in either. When you find yourself too deep in execution, you correct with reflection; when reflection is sufficient, you re-engage. The rhythm is the loop. Dynamical systems framing: the OODA rhythm is a limit cycle — an emergent oscillation arising from the interaction rules between phases, not designed in explicitly. Just as the predator-prey oscillation contains no trig functions yet produces cycles, the Observe → Orient → Decide → Act → repeat pattern emerges structurally from the phase interactions. This means the cycling behavior is architecturally inevitable, not fragile — it is the system's natural attractor.
- **Observe reads; loops create** — Observe reads what exists. Context-gathering loops create what doesn't exist. They are complementary, not redundant. Domain context (produced by loops) becomes system context (read by Observe) the moment it's written to an artifact.
- **Orphan nodes are retrieval failures** — memory works through associative triangulation. An experience (node) that has no connections to anything existing cannot be retrieved later — the system has no triangulation path to reach it. Operational rule: every new node added to the knowledge graph during an Observe session must be connected to at least one existing node before the session closes. Isolated nodes are not low-priority; they are effectively lost. This applies equally to CLAUDE.md entries: a pattern or decision recorded without connection to a context, system, or related principle is dead weight. The edge is as important as the node. **Complement — black hole nodes:** the failure mode runs in both directions. Orphan nodes have no edges out (unreachable from outside). Black hole nodes are concepts compressed so densely — by over-aggressive compaction or context reduction — that no new edges can be established inward. Over-compacted CLAUDE.md entries that strip all context become black holes: technically present, effectively unreachable. The compaction protocol must preserve enough connective tissue that future Orient passes can re-anchor the entry to current context.
- **Narrative coherence as a validity criterion** — if the system's operation can be described as a compelling, unbroken narrative (user acts → system responds → artifact emerges), the concept is coherent. If the narrative breaks down — a step requires handwaving, a phase has no clear output, a transition has no mechanism — there is a design gap. Narrative coherence is a validity test independent of spec coherence: a spec can be internally consistent and still fail narration. Apply it to any design: tell the story; find where it stutters.

- **The sleep test** — if an automated system doesn't work while you sleep, it's not worth it. This is the binary litmus test for every piece of automation in the system. A system that requires the human present is a tool, not autonomous infrastructure, and the investment in building it as "automation" has negative ROI — you'd be faster doing the work directly. The sleep test is also the graduation criterion for earned autonomy: a loop that can't sustain ~8 hours of reliable unsupervised operation hasn't earned full autonomy. The front-loaded Observe pattern exists to pass this test — thorough context gathering is the investment that buys overnight reliability.
- **Compound reliability is front-loaded, not distributed** — the system's autonomous reliability ceiling is set by the quality of the Observe phase, not by the number of gates. Gates verify execution correctness (files exist, tests pass, content has required sections); the dream state validates execution intent (is this the right thing to build, and does this serve the architecture's actual goals?). A rich dream state with specific intent, explicit failure modes, and debated encodings converts human judgment into machine-checkable criteria. The human's highest-leverage contribution is thorough Observe — not mid-execution checkpoints. Three mechanisms close the gap between syntactic verification and semantic validation: (1) *Semantic gates* — gates that validate not just "deliverable exists" but "deliverable aligns with dream state intent for this module." The dream state is written for a Claude to read; a gate can ask "does this implementation serve what the dream state specifies?" and score it. (2) *Cumulative drift detection* — individual phases may each be acceptable while small deviations compound across phases. A spec drifts slightly from dream state intent, architecture interprets the spec with another drift, implementation adds another. After 5 phases, every gate passed but the result misses the point. Periodic re-anchoring checks compare current trajectory against the dream state directly, not just against the previous phase's output. Distinct from coherence validation (which checks internal consistency) — this checks fidelity to original intent. (3) *Self-eval calibration* — when a skill scores itself completeness: 4, quality: 5, is that accurate? Without ground truth for self-assessments, systematic overconfidence means the "95% reliability" is actually 85%, and compound reliability degrades faster than expected. Track self-eval accuracy over time; adjust confidence accordingly. Confidence decay tightens parameters uniformly — self-eval calibration corrects specific bias. The practical implication: the workflow that maximizes autonomous reliability is deep Observe together (human + navigator harvesting, debating, encoding) followed by autonomous execution against the resulting dream state. The richness of the Observe phase directly determines how long and how reliably the system can execute without the human.
- **Probabilistic correctness over absolute correctness** — 100% is often the wrong target. Probabilistic guarantees with the right failure modes are better than absolute guarantees with the wrong ones. Self-eval uses rubric pass rates, not binary pass/fail; quality_score is a rate, not a boolean; gates are designed to block specific failure modes, not all failure. The goal is to know which failures are acceptable and which aren't — then optimize for the latter.
- **Goal misgeneralization** — the alignment failure mode class: the agent knows what to do (context correct) but wants the wrong thing (intent misspecified). The agent competently pursues the wrong objective. This is distinct from capability failure (agent can't do it) and from context failure (agent doesn't know enough) — it is an intent failure. The harness's structural defense is the intent engineering layer: dream state + spec + rubric + ecosystem CLAUDE.md must be mutually coherent. Drift between these surfaces — a rubric that rewards behavior the dream state doesn't want, a spec that optimizes for the wrong metric — is goal misgeneralization encoded structurally. Coherence-protocol detects this class of drift. Alignment drift is the temporal form: intent surfaces that were coherent at t=0 gradually diverge as the system evolves — rubrics accumulate edge-case patches, specs get updated, dream state shifts, but ecosystem CLAUDE.md doesn't follow. The drift is slow and invisible until it isn't. Measuring alignment drift over time — not just detecting point-in-time incoherence — is a distinct concern from capability drift (which calibration and improvement proposals handle). Expect a full alignment failure mode taxonomy as the system scales toward autonomous operation.
- **Epistemic scope** — the system's claims should be bounded by what its evaluation actually measures. "All acceptance criteria satisfied" not "production-ready"; "passes the rubric" not "the best approach." Don't assert more than the evals authorize. Distinct from completion gaming (which catches false claims) — this catches true claims that are over-scoped. The system genuinely passed all checks but then asserts something broader than what those checks actually prove.
- **Unbundle before you evaluate** — abstract terms (quality, charisma, good code) are bundled labels for clusters of specific observable behaviors. Bundled feedback can't drive learning: "quality: 3/5" points nowhere; "schema matches spec: yes, API format correct: no, error handling present: no" points to exactly what to fix. Rubrics, self-eval, and improvement proposals must operate at the unbundled level — specific observables — or they can't be calibrated, compared across runs, or used to identify improvement targets. This is the operational principle that makes self-eval calibration work: a bundled score that's systematically biased tells you nothing about WHERE the bias is; an unbundled checklist with a specific item that consistently gets false positives tells you exactly where to recalibrate. Same for improvement capture: "improve: output wasn't good enough" is noise; "improve: skill doesn't specify response format, so API returns HTML instead of JSON" drives change. Complement to epistemic scope: epistemic scope says don't claim more than you measured; unbundling says measure at the level where each item is independently verifiable.
- **Data proliferation through AI processing** — every time the system touches a document, it multiplies: the original, chunked embeddings in vector stores, RAG context injected into prompts, fine-tuning data sets, execution logs, prompt firewall logs, QA monitoring logs. One sensitive document becomes five+ copies across stores that have none of the access controls the original had. Vector embeddings are invertible (~90% text recovery), system prompts are always extractable, and probabilistic defenses fail on retry (99% security = failing grade when attackers automate). Architectural responses: sovereign-first design (data never leaves controlled infrastructure), fresh context windows per shadow clone (limits exposure surface), application-layer encryption before data enters any store, and assumption of breach (the hook system's axiom). This failure mode is structural to all AI systems, not fixable by prompting or training — it requires infrastructure-level mitigation.
- **Hallucination as intent failure** — hallucination is a reflection of the uncertainty we're giving the system in terms of the goals it should have. When intent is vague, the model fills the gap with hallucinated goals. The response to hallucination is often "clarify the intent," not "add more knowledge."
- **Proofs are programs (Curry-Howard correspondence, Howard 1969 / Curry 1958).** A proof of "A implies B" is literally a program that takes an input of type A and produces an output of type B. Propositions correspond to types; proofs correspond to programs; proof simplification corresponds to program execution. This is not a metaphor — it is a structural isomorphism. The implication for our architecture: if skills are typed (inputs, controls, outputs, mechanisms via ICOM), then a well-typed skill invocation IS a proof that the inputs will produce the outputs. Type-checking the skill invocation is proof-checking. A type-correct pipeline of skills is automatically logically consistent — the types guarantee that what one skill produces is what the next skill consumes. This is the formal foundation for compositional correctness in the skill library: get the types right, and the composition is guaranteed sound.
- **Data structure + algorithm = program (Wirth, 1976).** Every program decomposes into two orthogonal halves: the *data structure* (how state is shaped) and the *algorithm* (how state is transformed). Neither alone is a program — the pair is. The deeper form of the same claim, visible in the Church/Turing computation models that predate Wirth, is **state and operations upon state** — data structure and algorithm are the named categories, state and operations are what they *are*. Naming both surface forms matters because the pair shows up in vocabulary that doesn't say "data structure" or "algorithm": *stores and handlers*, *world state and processes that evolve it*, *schema and behavior*. They are all the same decomposition. **The nouns-and-verbs reading is load-bearing for this architecture specifically** because the Loop Library is explicitly framed as a pseudolanguage vocabulary (see below). If loops are words, the pair *is* the grammar: the knowledge graph's node types are primarily *nouns* (entities, concepts, artifacts); the Loop Library's entries are primarily *verbs* (operations that transform state); skills are the verb phrases that compose inside loops; the harness is the grammar that combines them. The Wittgensteinian boundary runs along both axes — the system's world is bounded by the nouns it can refer to *and* the verbs it can enact. The meta-loop expands the verb vocabulary; harvesting protocols expand the noun vocabulary. A language with only nouns can describe but not act; a language with only verbs can act but not refer. The harness earns its generativity by growing both. This is the atomic decomposition that sits one level beneath the structure/scaling frame in `REFERENCE-system-design.md`: the frame names *which commitment* you're making; Wirth names *what's inside* every option of that commitment. A B+tree is a balanced n-ary leaf-linked tree (data structure) plus binary-search-within-node / split-merge / range-traverse (algorithm). Paxos is per-node proposal state (data structure) plus prepare/promise/accept/learn with majority quorum (algorithm). The pair IS the option. Curry-Howard is the typed version of this pair — Wirth is the untyped claim, Curry-Howard is what you get when you insist both halves be typed and composition becomes proof-checking. **Two operational uses.** (1) *Diagnostic probe* — for any artifact, existing or proposed, ask "what's the data structure, what's the algorithm?" If you can't name both, the artifact isn't understood yet; if you can only name one, the design is half-specified. This is build-around discipline applied to every primitive. (2) *Orthogonality test for changes* — the two halves are independent: you can swap one while preserving the other. A proposed change that touches both halves simultaneously hasn't isolated the variable; a proposed change that touches one cleanly is a substitution the rest of the system can absorb. **The fractal property.** The pair appears at every scale. *Implementation*: option = data structure + algorithm. *Module*: service = its stores + its handlers. *System*: the harness is (`event log + knowledge graph + dream state + skills library + CLAUDE.md tiers + memory surfaces + task board + loop manifest`) + (`OODA cycle + leverage protocol + compaction + selection cascade + coherence validation + SRS schedule + calibration`). *Org*: the organization is its world state + the processes that evolve it — which is "worldbuilder, not project manager" restated in Wirth vocabulary. **The audit lens over this doc's own principles.** Many principles above speak to one half. *Data-structure principles*: everything-is-a-node, orphan-nodes-are-retrieval-failures, vocabulary + connections, black hole nodes, memory hierarchy as SRS tiers. *Algorithm principles*: OODA as limit cycle, compaction as quotient map, static plan, one artifact per loop, leverage protocol, feedforward control, pyramid summaries. *Composition principles* (how the two halves connect): Curry-Howard, build-around discipline, narrative coherence. The audit surfaces whether the principle set is balanced and names what each principle is *about* — which sharpens when a new principle is proposed ("which half is this?"). Reference: Niklaus Wirth, *Algorithms + Data Structures = Programs*, Prentice-Hall 1976.
- **A system cannot fully verify its own outputs (Tarski's Undefinability of Truth, 1936).** No consistent system that includes basic reasoning can define its own truth predicate. Self-referential truth is paradoxical — a system that says "this output is correct" has no external grounding for that claim. Verification requires a metalevel: the verifier must be expressed in a stronger system than the thing being verified. This is why self-eval alone is insufficient (our adversarial agent pairs exist for this reason), why the pilot's judgment at Observe boundaries matters (the human operates at a metalevel the system cannot reach about itself), and why the Turing jump hierarchy means there is always a verification ceiling. An agent can verify outputs at level N, but verifying the verifier requires level N+1. The practical consequence: build verification as a hierarchy of increasingly independent checks, never as a single self-referential loop.
- **Meta-analysis paralysis** — the failure mode of complex loop systems is getting too meta. Reviewing the review, planning the plan, observing the observation. When the session becomes about the session, execution has already stopped. Observe should be bounded and purposeful: the goal is a snapshot good enough for Orient to proceed, not certainty. Certainty is not achievable and not required. The 90-second silence trigger and the voice command exit exist for this reason — the system ends the session before the session becomes the work. When a loop spends more time reasoning about what to do than doing it, the system is in meta-analysis paralysis. The fix is the same at every level: produce the artifact and advance. Imperfect output that can be corrected beats perfect analysis that never ships.
- **Leverage/visibility as a design axis** — as the system gains leverage (more agents, more automation, more parallel execution), visibility decreases. This is structural, not accidental. Every architectural decision that adds leverage must account for what visibility it costs and how that visibility is recovered. The knowledge graph, CLAUDE.md, loop artifacts, scoring, and heat map are all visibility mechanisms. When evaluating new capabilities: does this increase leverage without destroying visibility? If visibility loss is unrecovered, the capability makes the system harder to improve, not easier. High leverage + high visibility is the target state; high leverage + low visibility is a trap. **Visibility is necessary but not sufficient for trust.** Trust — the property that enables delegating orchestration to the system rather than operating it manually — requires deep understandability: knowing not just what the system is doing but why, and being able to predict its behavior. A system earns trust through accumulated correct behavior, transparency of mechanism, and hooks/guardrails that enforce expected patterns. Without trust the system plateaus at Level 4 (human as orchestrator, manually coordinating agents); with trust it reaches Level 5 (delegated orchestration) and Level 6 (fully autonomous). Trust is not declared — it is accumulated, and it can be lost.
- **The harness is the differentiator** — the model is interchangeable; the scaffold is the product. The same model on the same benchmark with two different harnesses produces dramatically different results — the only variable is the scaffold. The Orchestrator's compounding value comes from harness improvements, not model upgrades. Corollary: if the harness grows more complex while models improve, something is wrong. The direction is simplification — the harness should shrink as model capability grows, delegating to the model what the harness currently handles explicitly. Over-engineering is the default failure mode; each architectural decision should pass the test "does removing this make things worse?" Manus rewrote their framework five times; each rewrite removed things. Watts: order is the wire; liberation is the current. The harness isn't just the source of compounding value — it's the constraint that makes capability generative rather than destructive. Raw model capability without a harness is current without wire: not light, fire. The five pillars of agentic engineering map directly to what the harness implements — the Orchestrator is agentic engineering made concrete, not commentary on it:

  | Pillar | Harness equivalent |
  |--------|-------------------|
  | Context Engineering | Loop commands + skills injection + CLAUDE.md |
  | Second Brain | Three learning channels (doing/reading/dreaming) → CLAUDE.md + knowledge graph; isolated concept nodes = capability frontier |
  | Agentic Validation | Gates + `verification-before-completion` |
  | Agentic Tooling | Skills library + MCPs + loops-as-CLIs |
  | Agentic Codebase | Codebase pruning + golden principles in CLAUDE.md |
  | Compound Engineering | More/Better/New flywheel + learning-loop |

- **Substrate beats application as a strategic position.** "The harness is the differentiator" (above) is the architectural claim. The competitive mechanism behind it: **substrate investment is upstream of multiple possible win conditions, which makes it harder to disrupt than application-layer investment.** An application optimizes for one specific way to win — when the wrong win condition is chosen, or the landscape shifts, the application has to be rebuilt. Substrate makes any number of win conditions more reachable from the same starting position — when the landscape shifts, the substrate is still load-bearing for whichever direction the next opportunity appears. **Jensen Huang ships hardware, not applications** is the canonical case. Every chip generation is a substrate bet committed 3–4 years before the software it serves is fully defined, and remains valuable across whatever software emerges as long as the substrate guesses right on memory bandwidth, compute, and energy properties. The same chip underwrites datacenter ML, gaming, scientific computing, mobile inference, and applications nobody has invented yet. He cannot bet on a specific winning application three years out; he bets on the substrate that underlies the largest number of possible winning applications. The substrate test is strict: substrate must be something the win conditions literally couldn't exist without, not something that amplifies them. Force multipliers are not substrate — a stat that makes existing capabilities stronger is amplification; substrate is what those capabilities are *built on*. **The strategic implication for the harness.** When the substrate is shared with everyone (open-source bash, Linux, the same frontier models everyone has access to), differentiation must live further up the stack — in the wiring layer (per the 11-knob model). When the substrate is yours, every win condition compounds against the same investment. This is why "primitives over integrations" works: primitives are substrate, integrations are application. This is why "sovereign all the way down" is a long-term goal: every layer where you depend on someone else's substrate is a layer where they captured a position you needed. **The failure mode** is committing substrate-level investment without knowing what win conditions it serves — substrate that doesn't underlie any plausible win condition is just expensive infrastructure looking for a use case. The right substrate is the smallest one that serves the largest number of win conditions, which is the leverage equation applied to architectural choice rather than tactical move. **The diagnostic question** for any proposed investment: does this serve one specific win condition (application), or does it make multiple unrelated win conditions more reachable from the same starting position (substrate)? Substrate bets are slower to validate but harder to lose; application bets are faster to validate but commit you to a single landscape.
- **Scaling dimensions have characters; layering is portfolio construction.** Each scaling dimension has its own compatibility profile (universal, broad, or narrow), its own investment cost (free to heavy), and its own ceiling shape (quick small bonus or slow compounding payoff). Layering them well means reading these properties and assembling a combination that fits the current situation, not running a binary focus-vs-mixing test. Most of a build's strength comes from accumulating many low-cost broadly-compatible dimensions; the strategic decisions live among heavy dimensions that compete for the same investment budget; narrow-condition dimensions are opportunistic only — invest when the conditions are already present, skip when they aren't.

  **Among heavy dimensions, three layering cases.** **Coupled** dimensions structurally feed each other's growth — one's output is the other's input — so mixing them is multiplicative through the loop and the divided investment pays for itself. **Parallel multipliers** both contribute to the same downstream calculation independently; mixing them is mathematically multiplicative but bounded by the downstream calculation's headroom, and either alone is also viable. **Independent** dimensions don't share any output→input relationship or any common downstream calculation; mixing them is additive at best, and focus wins by concentrating the investment. Coupling is the strongest case for mixing two heavies; parallel multiplication is the moderate case; independence is the case where focus is correct. The diagnostic question for coupling is the same as before: does dimension A's output structurally become dimension B's input? See also **Mutually exclusive substrate states** (below) for a fourth case where two dimensions share a substrate but require it in opposite states — distinct from independence because it's anti-coordination, not just non-coordination.

  **Strategic disposition.** Read each dimension's character before deciding how to layer it. The default for free / low-cost dimensions is take whenever they appear. The default for narrow-condition dimensions is skip unless the conditions exist. The heavy dimensions are where the real strategic decisions live, and they're not all the same kind of decision — coupling, parallel multiplication, and independence each warrant a different layering response. The most powerful builds combine many free dimensions, one or two coupled heavy dimensions, and whatever narrow-condition dimensions the run happens to enable — never multiple independent heavy dimensions, because that's just slower progress on each.
- **Mutually exclusive substrate states — substrate-incompatible dimensions.** Two scaling dimensions can compete not because they're independent or anti-coupled, but because they require the same substrate to be in *opposite states*. Both can be valid scaling dimensions; both can rely on the same underlying substrate; but they require that substrate in mutually exclusive configurations. One wants the substrate thin, the other wants it bloated; one wants high event frequency, the other wants high magnitude per event; one wants the substrate fast-cycling, the other wants it persistent. **This is distinct from the coupled / parallel-multiplier / independent taxonomy** in the layering principle (above) — it's a fourth case where the dimensions are *substrate-incompatible* rather than just unrelated. Mixing substrate-incompatible dimensions isn't merely suboptimal; it's actively destructive, because every move that benefits one dimension actively harms the other. Substrate-incompatibility is anti-coordination, where the dimensions are pulling the same resource in opposite directions. **The diagnostic question:** do both dimensions consume the same substrate, and do they want that substrate in mutually exclusive states? If yes, they cannot coexist regardless of how attractive each is in isolation — focus must be on one. The early-detection move is to enumerate the substrate states each dimension wants and check for direct contradictions before committing investment to both.
- **Scaling variable scope / timescale.** Scaling variables accumulate at multiple timescales: per-action (the variable advances every operation), per-turn (advances each turn cycle), per-cycle (advances each session or OODA cycle), per-run (advances across many cycles within a single run), or lifetime (advances permanently across the system's existence). Each timescale creates a fundamentally different dimension shape. **The pattern generalizes: the timescale at which a scaling variable accumulates is a load-bearing design choice, not a detail.** A variable that accumulates per-execution behaves differently than one that accumulates per-session, which behaves differently than one that accumulates across the entire system's lifetime. Choosing the timescale determines how the dimension is built, when it pays off, and what destroys it. **Within-cycle conversion** is a special case worth naming: a dimension whose output becomes its input within a single cycle, allowing the variable to compound multiple times before the cycle ends. Most dimensions fire once per cycle; within-cycle converters can fire many times before the cycle resets, which makes them uniquely powerful at the per-cycle timescale. **The diagnostic question** when evaluating any scaling dimension: at what timescale does the variable accumulate, and is that timescale matched to the cycle the system is operating on? A per-action variable in a system measured per-week wastes its compounding potential; a per-run variable in a system measured per-action never gets to fire its compounding at all.
- **Scaling curve shapes.** A scaling dimension's payoff scales with its variable's value according to one of several distinct curve shapes. **Linear** dimensions produce the same marginal output for every additional unit of investment — steady, predictable, never explosive. **Exponential** dimensions double or compound the previous level with each additional unit — initially slow, then dominant once the curve takes off. **Sublinear / logarithmic** dimensions produce diminishing returns per unit — fast initial gains, then progressively smaller improvements. **Threshold** dimensions are binary — they have no effect until a specific value is reached, then activate fully. Curve shape determines what the dimension looks like at different stages of investment: a linear dimension is steady; an exponential one is initially slow then dominant; a sublinear one front-loads its returns and tapers; a threshold dimension is irrelevant until it's everything. **The strategic implication:** matching curve shape to time horizon is critical. Exponential dimensions are wrong for short horizons (they haven't taken off yet). Sublinear dimensions are wrong for long horizons (they've already saturated). Threshold dimensions are wrong for incremental work (they don't pay until the threshold is reached). The most common dimension-evaluation failure is committing to a high-ceiling curve in a context where the horizon is too short for the curve to materialize.
- **Single-target vs distributed accumulation.** When a scaling variable accumulates, the resulting payoff lands either on one specific output (concentrated) or across many outputs simultaneously (distributed). *Concentrated* accumulation pushes every increment into one output: every upgrade makes one specific element stronger; every fine-tune cycle makes one specific model more capable; every iteration on a single endpoint compounds on that endpoint. *Distributed* accumulation pushes every increment across many outputs: every +1 to a stat applies to all attacks; every additional training token improves performance across all tasks; every infrastructure improvement helps every product feature simultaneously. Same accumulation mechanism, very different downstream effects: **concentrated dimensions create finishers** — single overwhelming outputs that define a strategy; **distributed dimensions create universal multipliers** — small uniform improvements across the entire surface. **The design choice:** when designing a scaling dimension, whether accumulation focuses on one output or spreads across many is a load-bearing decision that determines whether the dimension produces specialists or generalists. The choice should match the system's win condition: concentrated when a single overwhelming output decides the outcome; distributed when many small improvements compound into total advantage.
- **Self-referential variables.** A scaling variable can be the count of *operations the system has performed* — an action counter — rather than a property of those operations. Self-referential variables have a unique property: every action the system takes feeds the accumulator regardless of what the action was for. They reward operating itself. The most powerful instances accept any work as fuel — the variable grows with activity, period — which makes them uniquely robust because there is no way to take a useful action without also feeding the variable. **Strategic value:** self-referential dimensions are uniquely difficult to disrupt because disrupting them would require the system to stop operating entirely. They convert the system's own activity into accumulation, which means any pressure that forces the system to act harder *also* feeds the dimension faster. **The diagnostic question** for spotting them: is there a candidate variable whose value depends on *how many things the system has done* rather than *what those things were*? If yes, that's a self-referential variable — and almost always worth pursuing because of its disruption resistance.
- **Latent variable heuristic — find new scaling dimensions in unintended accumulation.** Look for variables that are already accumulating passively, whether anyone is paying attention to them or not, then ask whether a mechanism could convert that accumulation into useful output. The reason this works: variables that nobody is deliberately optimizing are where novel scaling dimensions live, because deliberate optimization is the signal that someone has already noticed the dimension and started building around it. The variables that haven't been noticed yet are accumulating quietly in execution traces, log files, sensor readings, side effects of other work — unpaid information that hasn't been recognized as a substrate yet. **The diagnostic move:** ask what is accumulating in the domain whether anyone wants it to or not, then ask whether a mechanism could convert that accumulation into useful output. Both halves must be present. If accumulation exists and the conversion mechanism is achievable, the candidate dimension is real. If accumulation exists but no conversion mechanism is plausible, the variable is real but not yet a scaling dimension — it is a substrate waiting for the right payoff. If no accumulation exists, there is nothing to scale. **Distinct from existing discovery moves.** The default discovery moves in the dream state are "what's the bottleneck?" (leverage protocol) and "what gap does topology-protocol surface?" (knowledge graph topology). Both are reactive — they identify dimensions that already exist by looking at where the system is constrained or where the graph is sparse. The latent variable heuristic is generative — it identifies dimensions that haven't been named yet by looking at where information is accumulating without a recipient. **The pairing with "Scaling dimensions have characters"** (the layering principle, above) is intentional: that entry tells you how to layer dimensions you already have; this entry tells you how to find new ones to layer.
- **Same surface mechanic, different hidden variables.** Two systems can consume the same surface resource while scaling completely different underlying variables. One dimension might scale with the *number of acquisition events per cycle* (event count is the variable); another might scale with the *cumulative magnitude across the cycle* (total is the variable). They look identical at the surface, but they optimize in opposite directions and conflict if pursued together. **The discipline this teaches:** when evaluating a scaling dimension, the question "what's the actual variable being scaled?" is more important than "what's the surface mechanic?" Two systems that look like they're doing the same thing might be operating on completely different variables and pulling in incompatible directions. The diagnostic question: what is the actual quantity that compounds when this dimension is invested in? Surface descriptions ("X build", "Y strategy") routinely hide the actual variable; the hidden variable is what determines whether two dimensions cooperate or conflict.
- **Fuel vs dimension distinction.** Something that *looks* like a self-replicating scaling dimension might actually be fuel — an input that other dimensions consume — rather than a dimension in its own right. A resource that generates copies of itself in a queue or store superficially looks self-scaling, but if you don't win with that resource directly — if the win comes from the engine that *consumes* the resource — then it's fuel, not a dimension. **The discipline test:** a scaling dimension converts a variable directly to output toward the win condition. Fuel is something that other dimensions consume to produce *their* output. If your "dimension" only matters when fed into another dimension's pipeline, it's fuel, not a dimension. This catches a specific failure mode: treating things as scaling dimensions when they're actually inputs to other dimensions. **The diagnostic question:** does this variable convert directly to the win condition, or does it have to pass through another dimension first? If it has to pass through, the candidate isn't a dimension — it's an input to one, and the dimension that matters is the consumer downstream.
- **Dimension identification protocol — how to approach a new domain.** When entering an unfamiliar domain, the move that beats every other first move is to map the full option space of scaling dimensions before committing investment. **Map the full option space.** Enumerate every plausible scaling dimension in the domain, including the ones that don't currently look promising. This is the cheap and important step — most failures of dimension identification come from working off a partial map and over-committing to the dimensions that happened to be most visible. Universal-compatibility dimensions, narrow-condition dimensions, free-to-layer dimensions, heavy dimensions all belong on the map. **Identify the active bottleneck node, not the whole chain.** Each scaling dimension has a *dependency graph* — multiple potential unlock paths and multiple potential bottlenecks along each path. At any given moment, one specific node in that graph is the active constraint. The skill is identifying that specific node — not analyzing the whole graph, not naming every theoretical bottleneck — and then estimating how often that node resolves (how likely it is to unblock soon under current conditions). Active-bottleneck status combined with resolution frequency is more informative than the dimension's theoretical ceiling because it determines both what's accessible from the current position and how soon. **Invest in substrate that remains valuable across the largest number of possible unlock sequences.** When investment is committed before the dependency graph is fully resolved, the right bet is the substrate that underlies the most possible win conditions (per substrate-beats-application, above). Substrate investment preserves optionality across the unknown; application investment commits to a single landscape that might not be the right one. The fewer dependency graphs you have visibility into, the more you should weight substrate over application. **The counterintuitive part:** substrate is usually not the highest-EV bet on any single dimension — it almost never is. It is the highest-EV bet across the *portfolio* of possible dimensions. Anyone calculating expected value at the individual-dimension level will reject substrate every time because substrate looks suboptimal at the dimension level. The substrate logic only works at the portfolio level, where preserved optionality across many possible futures dominates the highest single-dimension return. This is why naive EV maximization on individual dimensions systematically underinvests in substrate. **Stay alert for low-probability unlocks.** A dimension that looks low-probability might be low-probability because it is genuinely rare, or because nobody is looking at it. The two are very different states. The first is honest improbability; the second is unrecognized territory. The rarity of a dimension is partly information about the territory: if no one is investing in a dimension that has plausible value, that absence is itself a signal. **The actionable form of this:** deliberately look at parts of the dependency graph that nobody else is scanning. Early seers of new dimensions are not passive observers waiting for absences to become obvious — they actively examine regions of the graph that the consensus is ignoring. The people who saw inference-time compute scaling early were not luckier than anyone else; they were looking at a different part of the graph than everyone else. Undiscovered territory has option value far exceeding its stated probability precisely because nobody else is positioned to claim it. **Distinct from the tech-tree framework.** The tech-tree framework (Orient frameworks) maps the external landscape of capability progression and identifies the next node to push toward — it operates within a known capability landscape. The dimension identification protocol is broader: it applies to any domain, including ones where the landscape itself is partially unmapped, and its first move is the enumeration of the landscape before any selection. Tech-tree is "what's the next achievable node in a known graph?" The dimension identification protocol is "what does the graph even look like, and where are the unexplored corners?" **Pairing with the discovery and layering principles.** The latent variable heuristic (above) finds new scaling dimensions in unintended accumulation; the dimension identification protocol uses the candidates as input and decides which to invest in; "Scaling dimensions have characters" tells you how to layer the prioritized ones. Together they form a complete discovery → prioritization → layering cycle for any new domain.
- **Constraint removal as a scaling unlock.** Identifying what's bounding a dimension's reuse and removing that specific constraint can transform the dimension's character entirely — from one-shot to sustained, from costly to free, from gated to ungated. The pattern generalizes broadly: removing a rate limit on an API, removing a manual approval step, removing an Exhaust property on a powerful action all do the same thing structurally. Many powerful effects are limited not by the strength of the effect itself but by a single mechanism that makes them non-repeatable. When the limiting constraint is structural rather than fundamental, removing it can transform the effect's character without changing anything else about the underlying mechanism. The dimension's effective ceiling jumps to whatever the underlying mechanism can produce when fired repeatedly — which is often dramatically higher than the constrained version, because constrained dimensions are usually evaluated at their single-use ceiling, not their sustained ceiling. **The diagnostic move:** when evaluating a dimension that looks promising but appears limited, ask "what specific constraint is bounding this dimension's reuse?" and "could that constraint be removed without breaking anything downstream?" Removing a structural constraint on an existing dimension is usually cheaper and faster than building a new dimension from scratch — the constraint-removal move should be evaluated *before* committing to entirely new dimensions.
- **Intent engineering** — if context engineering is telling agents what to know, intent engineering is telling agents what to want. The harness implements both. Context engineering: knowledge graph + CLAUDE.md + MCP servers — structured organizational knowledge delivered at the right time. Intent engineering: dream state + spec + rubric + ecosystem CLAUDE.md — the layer that encodes what the system wants, at every level of resolution. Context is retrievable; intent requires vision. You can pipeline RAG; you can't pipeline wanting the right thing. Ecosystem CLAUDE.md is the apex intent engineering artifact: it encodes what the system IS, which determines what it fundamentally wants across all contexts. The bilateral consent requirement is the constitutional protection of that intent — neither the human nor the system can unilaterally rewrite what it wants.
- **The system is its own first customer** — the Orchestrator is built using its own Loop Library. The code loop builds the code loop. The OODA cycle develops the Orchestrator. Recursion at the meta level: the system uses itself to improve itself. This is not recursive OODA within a cycle — it is the system consuming its own outputs across cycles to develop its own capabilities. This is the strange loop in Hofstadter's sense: following the system from top to bottom returns you to the starting point at a different logical level. The meta-loops (8–11) that produce Skill, Framework, Protocol, and Eval artifacts are the mechanism that makes this level-crossing explicit — not just another loop type, but the layer at which the system achieves structured self-reference. Strange loops produce emergent properties not visible at any single level; the system's compounding is not legible from any one loop in isolation.
- **Scaling law loop — the four laws of intelligence as a directed cycle, not a list.** The four current scaling laws of intelligence (pre-training, post-training, inference-time compute, agentic / environment interaction) are not independent investments. They form a cycle where each law's output becomes candidate input for the next: pre-training produces a base model → post-training refines it → inference deploys it under longer reasoning → agentic execution generates real-world interaction traces → **curation** selects which traces are durable enough to memorize → back into pre-training to start the next iteration from a higher platform. The closing step is curation — the decision of which interaction data, decision traces, and outcomes are worth promoting from "produced this run" to "embedded in the next base model." The bottleneck increasingly lives at the curation step rather than at any single law: labs that can identify novel, high-signal, non-redundant data and route it back into training compound faster than those that can't. **Two substrate dependencies underwrite the loop.** Simulation fidelity rate-limits how much interaction data can be produced (especially for embodied tasks where real-world experiment rate is the constraint); curation infrastructure rate-limits how much of the produced data is actually useful. They sit in a chicken-and-egg relationship — sim must be good enough to flood before curation can be validated, but curation must be ready before the flood arrives. The window to build curation infrastructure is the gap between "sim is good enough" and "the flood arrives," and that window is shorter than most observers realize. **Implication for the harness.** The orchestrator naturally lives at the agentic layer (the fourth law) — every loop execution is an interaction trace, every artifact has provenance, every decision is logged, every quality_score is reward signal. The harness's data flywheel is its position in this larger cycle. The sovereign model registry deferred entry is the harness's curation step made concrete. The compound loop is the same recursive structure at a different scale (within the harness's own development rather than across the field). "The system is its own first customer" (above) is the recursive property that makes the loop close on itself. Decisions about where to invest the harness's next unit of effort should be evaluated against the loop: does this improve the production rate at one of the four laws, or does it improve the quality of curation at the closing step? Anything that doesn't is orthogonal to the dominant compounding mechanism. See `REFERENCE-scaling-laws.md` for the full taxonomy of the four laws, the eras that produced them, and the candidate fifth law (action-feedback loop density) that may emerge as the next dominant axis.
- **Loop Library as pseudolanguage vocabulary** — each loop is a densely loaded word: meaning, implementation, constraints, expected output. The pseudolanguage has a grammar — the **data structure + algorithm = program** principle (above) reads as *nouns + verbs = utterances* at the language level: the knowledge graph's typed node catalog is the noun vocabulary, the Loop Library is the verb vocabulary, skills are verb phrases that compose inside loops, and the harness is the grammar. Growing the vocabulary means growing both — meta-loop expands verbs, harvesting protocols expand nouns — and the Wittgensteinian boundary runs along both axes. As the kernel stabilizes, the vocabulary compresses. Compound loops become single invocations. The system evolves toward a pseudolanguage family a human can speak vocally to coordinate across alien intelligences — AI agents of different form factors (code agents, robots, voice assistants, autonomous systems) — all trained on the same Loop Library. This requires the loop manifest to be machine-parseable from day one: structured enough that a non-Claude agent can select the right loop without reasoning from scratch. Lao Tzu / Watts: five colors make a man blind; five tones make a man deaf. A finite vocabulary isn't neutral — it actively blinds you to work that can't be named within it. The meta-loop exists for this reason: when work doesn't fit any existing loop, you're not just missing a tool, you're missing a category. Naming the gap is the first act of seeing it. **The Loop Library as Wittgensteinian boundary** — "The limits of my language mean the limits of my world."
